THE RED PAGE .
Symbolist Poetry.
THERE are three foreign critics of the
French symbolist poets at whose feet
it is a pleasure and a profit to sit
—in Australia, Chris. Brennan ; in
America, Vance Thompson ; and in Eng-
land, Arthur Symons. In the matter of
textual knowledge our own critic is the most
learned of them all. Vance Thompson is
rather interviewer than critic, but his fine
journalist’s style is helped by sympathy,
and his recently-published “French Por-
traits ” tells you a thousand little intimate
things about the men which illuminate their
work and the motive of their work. Arthur
Symons is one of the most lyrically-intense
of current English poets—he was the friend
of Verlaine And the visitor of Mallarmd—and
though occasionally his criticism seems care-
less, occasionally inadequate, his work con-
tains the stimulus of his brilliant capacity,
and is always worth attention.
In his'new book, called “The Symbolist
Movement in Literature,” Arthur Symons
collects and expands various articles which
he has printed in English reviews during the
past three or four years ; and adds sufficient
new matter to justify warm protest, from
people at another pole of view. He is a
professed mystic now, with a debt to Ploti-
nus ; and, if he has not already forgotten his
doubts on the bosom of Holy Mother Church,
he seems far on the path to that Lethe of
jangled modern wits. Yet “it never can be
necessary to throw away your reason to save
your soul ” unless your body is so sick that
reason is reason no longer.
What is Symbolism in Literature ? Liter-
ally, it is a method of statement by allu-
sions, of description by images. In order to
display an idea, you do not consider it ana-
tomically or as a criminal is registered for
identification—for so, as a Symbolist holds,
you would lose the idea completely. You
are dealing with abstract things, not with
concrete, and you cannot apply the methods
of weighing aud measuring which you would
apply to the concrete. Thus, instead of
nakedly stating the idea, you suggest the
impression which the idea makes upon you,,
in the belief aud the hope that such
a suggestion of an impression will bring
the reader closer to the idea than any exact
statement. You endeavour to portray the
thing, the fact, the thought, not as it is, but
as it affects yourself—that is, you do not
stand aloof from it, and describe it literally
as an object outside yourself, but you bring
it within yourself, fuse it with your con-
sciousness, and hint in words the reaction
of its action upon your consciousness. More
than hint, suggest, you cannot do—since
it is impossible to state an emotion by means
of mere words. So you work roundabout,
by metaphors, images, symbols.
Poe’s poem of the Conqueror Worm
is a good example of the use of symbolism
as a literary device ; but a modern symbolist
would not explain the symbol. He would
leave “the Tragedy Man” and “the-Con-
queror Worm ” to be inferred.
Arthur Symons begins his book by affirm-
ing that “ without symbolism there can be
no literature ; indeed, not even language ”
—since words themselves are arbitrary sym-
bols. And in a sense that is true, though by
dint of familiar usage many words give such
a direct impression of things that their sym-
bolic value is lost altogether. And he quotes
Carlyle’s eulogium of the symbol—declaring
that “in the symbol proper there is ever,
more or less distinctly and directly, some
embodiment and revelation of the Infinite ;
the Infinite is made to blend itself with the
Finite, to stand visible, and as it were,
attainable there.” Which is a quotation
useful to show the relation of Symbolism
with Mysticism. The Mystic, generally
speaking, is a person who, like everybody
else, finds himself unable to finally ex-
plain or account for the material Universe
which confronts him. Hence he spins a
beautiful series’ of dreams to convince
himself that the material Universe does
not really exist—it is a phantasm, a passing
show, an emanation of Deity which is only
worth studying for the sake of what it im-
plies. It is a kind of symbolic poem, in
fact, written by the Almighty Poet. The
ordinary eating-drinking-loving-swearing in-
dividual sees only the words of the poem—
the houses and trees, the women and
liquors ; but'the Mystic, by dint of turning
his eyes inward or Brahminically contemplat-
ing his navel, realises the secret meaning
of the poem. What the Religious Mystic
does with the universe the Literary Mystic
tries to do with language ; but the literary
result is usually less satisfactory. The Reli-
gious Mystic has his world ready made for
him to evolve hidden meanings from—and
very well made too, on the whole ; the crea-
tive Literary Mystic goes to work the
reverse way, and hides his meanings in
a poem. Then his disciples proceed to
find them out. The Poem is a mystic
universe, a finite clue to infinity. Yet the
literary disciple is in better case than the
religious disciple. If the religious mystic
fail to find the inner meaning of the universe
the fault is his alone —it is plain that he has
not sufficiently contemplated his navel. But
if the literary mystic fail to find the inner
meaning of the obscurities of Mallarme he
can shrug shoulders and hint that the Deity
is a better artificer than Mallarme.
Arthur Symons’s book deals with Gerard
de Nerval, Yilliers, Rimbaud, Verlaine,
Jules Laforgue, Mallarme, Huysmans, and
Maeterlinck. Gerard de Nerval (1808-55)
was a lunatic with poetic intervals, or a poet
with sane intervals. It was he who led a
lobster through the Palais Royal at the end
of a blue ribbon ; and he finally hanged
himself to a cabaret window with an apron-
string which he called the garter of the
Queen of Sheba. Of Yilliers (1838-90)
Symons says his last word is faith—
faith against the evidence of the senses, against
the negations of materialistic science, against the
monstrous paradox of progress, against his own
pessimism in the face of these formidable enemies.
He affirms ; he “ believes in soul, is very sure of
God ” ; requires no witness to the spiritual world,
of which he is always the inhabitant ; and is con-
tent to lose his way in the material world, brush-
ing off its mud from time to time with a disdain-
ful gesture, as be goes on his way (to apply a sig-
nificant word of Pater) “ like one on a secret
errand.”
Rimbaud (1854-91), and Verlaine—the
finest poet whom the Symbolists can claim—
have been recently scrutinised for this page.
Symons comments that Verlaine’s poetry
sums up in itself the whole paradox of humanity,
and especially the weak, passionate, uncertain,
troubled century to which we belong, in which so
many doubts, negations, and distresses seem, now
more than ever, to be struggling towards at least
an ideal of spiritual consolation. Verlaine is the
poet of these weaknesses and of that ideal.
Symons might have added that the artis-
tic valuq of Verlaine’s poetry, though doubt-
less a consequence of his character, is a
thing independent of his character. With
his lyric passion, bis sense of words, and his
power over musical combination of words,
he is independent of mystic theories. That
is to say, it is quite conceivable that as the
poet of materialism and reason—had his
character tended thither —he would have
achieved quite as high a poetic result a$ he
achieved in the region of misty Catholicism.
The power in him which made “ Sagesse ”
is a thing apart from the sentiments which
“; Sagesse ” expresses. ■ ■
Of Jules Laforgue (18(50-87) Symons says
that his art is an art of the nerves.” His
verse and prose are alike a land, of travesty,
making subtle use of colloquialism, slang, neolog-
ism, technical terms, for their allusive, then-
factitious, their reflected meanings, with which
one can play, very obviously. The verse is alert,
troubled, swaying, deliberately uncertain, hating
rhetoric .so .piously that it prefers, and finds its
piquancy in, the- ridiculously obvious. Laforgue
lias invented a new manner of being Rone or
Werther; an inflexible politeness towards man,
woman, and destiny. lie composes love poems
hat in hand, and smiles with an exasperating
tolerance before all the transformations of the
eternal feminine.
In this wise —

And this is Symons's explanation of
—
To evoke, by some elaborate, instantaneous
magic of language, without the formality, of an
after all impossible description ; to be, rather than
to express : that is what Mallarme has consistently,
and from the first, sought in verse and prose.
. . . Remember his principle : that to name is to
destroy, to suggest is to create. Note, further,
that he condemns the inclusion in verse of any-
thing but, “ for example, the horror of the forest,
or the silent thunder afloat in the leaves, not the
intrinsic, dense wood of the trees.” ... In the
final result there must be no sign of the making,
there must be only the thing made. .... . “The
pure work implies the elocutionary disappearance
of the poet, who yields place to the words, immor-
talised by the shock of their inequality ; they take
light from mutual reflection, like an actual trail of
fire over precious stones, replacing the old lyric
afflatus or the enthusiastic personal direction of
the phrases.”
Of Huysmqns, Symons says—
We know that the finest imaginative work can
be built only out of the primary emotions. Here,
then, is a novelist who has obtained complete
mastery over one of the primary emotions, pre-
cisely because it has obtained complete mastery
over his own soul: the emotion of faith.
And of Maeterlinck—
Maeterlinck has realised, better than anyone
else, the significance, in life and art, of mystery.
He lias realised how unsearchable is the darkness
out of which we have but just stepped, and the
darkness into which we are about to pass.
Symons’s conclusion is an eloquent plea for
mysticism—which will convince those whom
it. is fitted to convince. To weak and
feminine minas mysticism will always
appeal, and with its appeal comes the emo-
tion requisite to the achievement of the
highest Art. But while blood is red, pulses
full, and brain strong, no man wittingly
adopts the creed of individual renunciation,
of worldly denial, and of living death.
Mysticism is associated with individual
decay or racial decadence. And though
Art be found in the mystic's quiet moonlit
paths rather than in the heat of worldly
effort and struggle, victory and defeat, yet *
since the universal doom is sure, let us
rather take it fighting than lying down.
For, though Art is good, Life is always
better.
The Utuander Grievances.
“ THIS LAND OF LlES.”—Winston Chuk.chili«.
More than anything else, it was the impression
that fellow-countrymen of our own have been the
victims of “Boer brutality” and “police terror-
ism ” that created a sentiment favorable to war.
The “helots” on the Rand, we were told by the
St. James's Gazette, “ have been robbed, assaulted,
murdered at the sweet will of Mi\ Kruger’s janis-
saries.” “ Every Boer,” said the Daily Mail,
“ believes that the Englishman is a creature who
can be spat upon,” and of the whole crisis no less
an authority than Sir A. Milner has said that it
“arose out of the Edgar incident.” The demand for
the franchise would not have brought war with it.
Mr. Kruger conceded what we had asked. More-
over, it was too notorious that the Uitlanders, in the
words of Mr. Lionel Phillips, did not “ care a fig”
about the franchise. If we are at war to-day it is
because the English public was convinced that the
lot of the Uitlander in some way resembled that
of the Armenian.
The most singular feature of this agitation is its
novelty. Compare the Uitlanders’ Petition of
1899 with the Pre-Raid Manifesto of 27 Dec., 1895,
and you will find that, while both documents com-
plain of the same economic grievances and the
same political wrongs, the former deals at great
length with the whole subject of the misconduct
of the police, a point regarding which the earlier
appeal is wholly silent. Now why did the Uit-
landers complain in 1895 of the dynamite mono-
poly, the educational grievances, the refusal of
the franchise, etc., and yet omit all reference to
the outrages which have brought at last the war
for which they clamored '! It was not from reti-
cence, it was not from a desire for moderation.
Have the Boors, then, in the interval, grown more
brutal, more violent, or have the Ujtlanders
learned at last the secret of successful agitation ?
An ingenious leading article in the 7 lines of Natal
has answered our question for us :
Franchise, paramountey, and so forth, have
been all very useful in the evolutionary pro-
cess of education. But before we shall have
finished we shall have to reach that point
when home opinion shall have come into line
with opinion in Natal as to the necessity for a
clean sweep of the present order of things in
the Transvaal and Orange Free Slate, and of
the Bond in Cape Colony. To do this there is
. but little cause now to harp Upon did well-
worn grievances. Those have already become
fairly well understood at home. The best
object lessons we can now employ are those
atrocities to our women and children. If wo
are to disarm those who are now against us at
home, and who will endeavour in the day of
our triumph to restore as much of the Boer
power as possible, out of a misplaced senti-
ment for. a weak foe, and who.could go frantic
over atrocities in Bulgaria, then, if they re-
quire atrocities to assist their comprehension of
facts, and to disarm their opposition, so be it.
It should not now be difficult to supply the
necessary chapter of horrors to strike the
imagination even of these good people.'
In other words, the failure “to supply the neces-
sary chapter of horrors ” was an unfortunate
omission in the campaign of 1895. It has now
been remedied, and our dull “imagination” lias
been stirred by “ atrocities Wo have been
“assisted” to understand the facts by a sort of
vigorous symbolism—much as a child is taught its
alphabet by pictures. Thanks to this lucky im-
provement in the method of illustrating and
adorning the truth, we are now at war.
. * . * ,*
For the most part the talk about outrages has
been somewhat vague. Thus the Daily Mdil
assured us on the 11th October that “the Eng-
lishman is fighting ... to protect his women'
from insult.” We have searched in vain through
the Mail file for any concrete case of outrage
against women. What we do find are stories of
hired villains who were “instructed” by the
Boers “to strip Englishwomen naked,” but for
some reason not stated did nothing of the kind.
Two genuine cases of murder have occurred. It is
really rather striking, that in a new country,
among a mining population, throughout a long
period of unrest and agitation,, there have been no
more than two. But let us take them in detail.
The Edgar incident is the more important.
It began with a drunken quarrel between
two Uitlanders, Edgar and Forster. Edgar
struck Forster, knocking him senseless, and
thereupon a third Uitlander named Sheppard
called for the police, who on their arrival
found Forster still senseless, whilst. Edgar
had got into his house and locked the door.
The police were informed that Edgar had
armed himself, and were discussing tne ques-
tion whether they should then force an entry
into his house, when the assembled Uitlanders
urged them to make the arrest at once, and
they determined to do so. The door was
easily forced, and the policeman in front;
whose name was Jones, was met by Edgar,
who struck at him with a formidable life pre-
server constructed of tough wood, with an iron
nut screwed on one end and a loop at the
other for the wrist. Either blow would have
killed the policeman if it had reached its
mark. Thereupon Jones drew Lis revolver
and fired at Edgar, intending, as he said, to
disable him only, but the bullet entered the
heart and killed him. Forster died from the
injuries inflicted by Edgar. Jones was put
upon his trial and acquitted on the ground
that he acted in self-defence. Jones, by the
way, was the son of a Welsh father and a
Dutch mother.
This incident is admittedly the kernel of the
whole case against the Boers. The villain of’ the.
piece was not himself a Boer, he acted in self-
defence, and at the worst was only a trifle hasty
in anticipating the work of the common hangman.
Yet the Welshman Jones is to-day the type of the
brutal Boer and the murderer Edgar the personi-
fication of the oppressed Uitlander.
The second murder that is cited as a proof that
“the lives of British subjects are insecure inthe
Transvaal,” is the distressing Applebe case. Mrs.
Applebe was murdered under circumstances of
great brutality, but her assailants have never been
discovered, and the chances are that they were not
Boers, or even Uitlanders, but natives. It is the
failure to find them that is made a charge against
the Boers. With much greater reason one might
argue that “the lives of British subjects” were
“insecure ” in Whitechapel some few years ago.
* * * •
It seems needless to labor the question further.
Had the Boer police been systematically brutal,
would we find to-day that thousands of non-British
Uitlanders were fighting for the Transvaal and
not a man of them for England ? So strong is this
argument that the Daily Mail was obliged to anti-
cipate it with a lie. Thus on 29th June it Said
that “practically the whole of the non-English
Uitlanders who are not on Mr. Kruger’s pay-Jists
are with Sir Alfred Milner ” ; and again on 31st
Aug., “The Scandinavians have rejected a pro-
posal to form a volunteer corps.” Some scores of
them fell gallantly in the cause of their oppressors
at Magersfontein. To dispose finally of the legend
of Mr. Kruger’s system of terrorism, it is only
necessary to quote the only independent investi-
gator sent out by the English Press to Johannes-
burg—Mr. J. A. Hobson, who represented the
Manchester Guardian. The rest of the English
Press was content to take its news from the
offices of Soutli African papers owned by the
Rhodeses, the Beits, and the Ecksteins. Mr.
Hobson says of Johannesburg :
Everyone who has lived in that golden city
is perfectly aware that liberty, not to say
license, of speech and action has prevailed to
an extent unknown in any other city of the
civilised world. This talk about “ helotry ”
and “the intolerable burdens of the Uit-
landers ” deceived no one in Johannesburg;
it was simply part of the jargon bv which a
clique of politicians handling the Press
operated upon the British Government and
public opinion for their own purposes
Scores of people whom I have closely ques-
tioned inform me that they have lived many
years in Johannesburg, moving about freely
at all hours, and have never got into any
trouble or been molested by the police ; the
condition of the streets even at a time of
public excitement like the present is far more
orderly .than that of largo districts of London.
But there were other kindred grievances. It is
said that in Johannesburg there was neither right
of public meeting nor freedom for the Press.
There was also the “education” grievance. Let
us glance at these in detail :
1. This complaint is founded on the fact that
on one occasion a mass meeting of “reformers”
was broken up. Some weeks later something of
the kind occurred in Trafalgar Square. But both
of these, let us hope, were isolated cases. A suc-
cessful meeting of protest against -Boer rule was
held unmolested in Johannesburg on 12 June.
The Jingo Press, when it suited its argument,
used to appeal to the support of “ mass meetings
held everywhere up and down the Rand.”
2. The Press, so far from being let and hindered,
indulged in daily provocations, in daily invective
against Mr. Kruger and the Boers. How coarse
and violent its language was only a series of quota-
tions for which we have not space would
adequately show. Ultimately Mr. Pakeinan, of
the Leader, was arrested. He had openly preached
treason. In the Leader of 29th August appears
the following :
The abolition of the Republic is not the end
we anticipated and hoped for; yet we fully
recognise the necessity for the step in view of
the hopeless attitude of the Gdvernment.
Would we have tolerated such talk in Ireland in
the days of the Coercion Act ?
3. The grievance regarding education was that
in the State-aided schools of the Dutch republic,
Dutch was the language of instruction. It seems
a natural state of affairs. English voluntary
schools existed for Uitlander children.
* * * *
We have left to the last a set of grievances
which were genuine. There can be.no doubt that
the administration of the Transvaal was on the
whole corrupt and unstatesmanlike, and that its
fiscal system was vicious. The Uitlanders made
organised efforts to corrupt the Boers, as Mr.
Leonard admits. ( Appendix to Report on the Raid,
p. 591.) They succeeded so far as to make the
petty venality of the Boers a nuisance; their
votes, however, were never for sale. A nuisance
Pretoria no doubt was, as Tammany Hall and
Yildiz Kiosk and the Tsung-li-Yamen are
nuisances. But who would dare say that all this
justified war? The taxes were rather wrong in
their incidence than excessive in their amount,
The Government had a right to raise a revenue
from the gold extracted from its soil. It chose to
do it by monopolies and indirect taxation. Its
fiscal policy, like that of the Cape, was strin-
gently Protectionist. As a result everything was
dear, from dynamite and railway rates down to
soda-water. This, if the Uitlanders were only
honest, was the grievance that really brought
about the war. Real grievances though these were,
note that they fell rather on a few capitalists than
on the mass of the Uitlander population. If prices
were dear, wages were high, and thus the burden
really lay on the mine-owners on the Rand and the
shareholders at home. Professional men were not
grievously taxed. Thus Mr. Leonard, in his evi-
dence before the Select Committee in 1897, ad-
mitted that from an income of £lO,OOO he paid
only £l5O in taxes, even including those which are
indirect. Working-men were practically exempt.
(See “Report of Select Committee on Raid,” p.
xlviii., §15.) The fact is that the war has been waged
to day with no other object than to raise the dividends
of a few gold-mines. There would have been no
Stormberg disaster, no Magersfontein massacre,
no Tugela repulse, if, instead of talking of out-
rages and atrocities, the Uitlander magnates and
the Press they owned had honestly said that what
they really wanted was cheaper dynamite and a
lower wages bill. — London Morning Leader.

Bards of the Backhlocks.
Groom and Glory Boys!
(Dedicated- to various Australian Contingents.)
When you’re lyin’ in your hammock, sleepin’ soft
and sleepin’ sound,
Without a cai* or trouble on vour m.nd,
And there’s nothin’ to disturb you but the engines
goin’ round,
And you’re dreamin’ of the girl you left behind ;
In the middle of your joys you’ll be wakened by a
noise
And a clatter on the deck above your crown,
And you’ll hear the corporal shout as he turns the
picket out,
“ There’s another blanky ’orse fell down ! ”
You can see ’em of a mornin’, when you're cleanin’
out the stall,
A-leanin’ on the railin’, nearly dead,
And you reckon by the evenin’ they’ll be pretty
safe to fall,
And you curse them as you tumble into bed.
Oh, you’ll hear it very soon, “ Pass the word to
Denny Moon !
There’s a ’orse ’ere throwin’ ’andsprings like a
clown,”
And “Ere, shove the others back or’e’ll cripple
half the pack—
There’s another blanky ’orse fell down !”
And when the war is over, and the fightin’ all is
done,
And you’re safe at home with medals on your
chest,
And you’ve learnt" to sleep so soundly that the
firin’ of a gun
At your “ear-hole” wouldn’t rob you of your
rest;
As you lie iii slumber deep, if your wife walks in
her sleep
And tumbles down the stairs and breaks her
crown,
Oh, it won’t awaken you, for you’ll say “It’s
nothin’ new,
It’s another blanky horse fell down ! ”
* This was, it is said, written by “ someone who went
to Africa in the s.s. Kent.” It bears the stamp of
“ Banjo."
The Revival.
Comes a spirit working mightily for grace
To our little river township now and then.
When it’s here you feel it stirring in the place,
And you see it in the faces of the men ;
There’s a hush by day—the cursing tongue is still;
And the street is dark and empty in the night,
But the chapel on the hill is a thumping gospel
mill,
And a beacon of salvation blazing bright.
Hallelujah, sing to glo-er-ee ! Ha'lelujah, ahmen !
Hear the old rip-snorting chorus rovr right up the
sky again,
Rake the hottest, lowest level,
Making trouble for the devil.
Hallelujah, ahmen !
There’s a travelling evangelist on hand ;
He’s a monument of piety and zeal,
And he makes the blackest sinners understand,
And the harder kind of infidel to feel,
’Tis a day-and-night revival. Oh ! it’s rare
When the choir has got her going well and
strong,
And the hundred brothers there get a hitch upon
the air,
And they lash the spinning world with whips of
song.
Hallelujah ! sing to glo-er-ee ! Hallelujah, ahmen !
There’s no stiff knee in the chapel, there’s no
grinning scoffer then —
When we hear the angels o’er us
Taking up the grand old chorus,
Hallelujah, ahmen !
At the pub the landlord’s standing by the door,
And a cloud of care is black upon his brow,
For the men are swearing off it by the score,
And the sisters will not touch or taste it now.
Women’s voices that were awful in the night,
When the bottle went too often to the bar,
Swelling yonder in their might, all with harmony
unite
In the hymn that shakes the gates of God ajar.
Hallelujah, sing to glo-er-ee ! Hallelujah, ahmen !
Souls go soaring on the music, through the open
windows then,
Up to see the White Throne flaming,
Hear the million harps exclaiming—
Hallelujah, ahmen!
Now the town has got religion, carnal mirth
Has no savor for the men, and every Miss
Has been raised above the vanities of earth,
And there’s only saintly comfort in the kiss:
Wives are meek and men are mild in words and
deeds,
But a wonderful distraction they can find
When the sup’rintendent reads, and the Rev.
Prater leads,
And the hymn goes by like thunder on the wind.
Hallelujah, sing to glo-er-ee ! Hallelujah, ahmen !
When the well-remembered chorus rakes the
startled sky again,
And it haunts the quaking devil
In his hottest, lowest level.
Hallelujah, ahmen !
V.
EDWARD DYSON.

SATURDAY, MAY 19, 1900.
G. R. Dibbs, Australian Nationalist and
Jingo-Imperialist.
Tub question of creating a standing army [in Australia] is
one which to my mind is almost more repulsive than the
question of re-adjustment of territorial boundaries. It
means the existence in our midst of a certain number of
idle men—men sharpening their knives and their swords
for the first fitting opportunity of fleshing them on the
people of their own country, BECAUSE WE HAVE NO
OTHER ENEMIES. . . . This question of the creation of
a military force is one of the blots upon these resolutions.
We want no military force in N.S.W. . . . We have no
enemies within and there is no necessity to fasten THE
CURSE OF A STANDING ARMY upon us. . . . Our own
police are quite sufficient for the preservation of order
within. . . . I presume, then, that members of the conven-
tion are prepared at once to give the go-by altogether to
the idea of Imperial Federation. . . . But we are laying
the foundation and step by step are following in the iines
of a great nation and IN DUE TIME WE SHALL BE-
COME, WHAT AMERICA HAS BECOME, A SEPARATE,
FREE AND INDEPENDENT STATE.
.... Whenever 1 have the opportunity I will do my
utmost to cut down the military spirit and to instil into
the people of this land a love of their homes , and also the
necessity of defending them in the only legitimate manner.
.... THEFUTURE OF AUSTRALIA MUST BE “A
NEW BfyTANNIA,” WITH HER OWN FLAG.
.... the national spirit of the rising generation of
Australia is instinct with freedom which will impel our
people at the earliest possible moment to form a nation of
their own. That is the aspiration, instinct and spirit I
hope of young Australia. [Thus, at the Federal Conven-
tion of iB9l, spoke G. Republican Dibbs, who now struts
round Sydney with a sword and things and has the front
to cable ’Ome, as voicing the opinions of 82,000 N.S.W.
electors, an expression of approval of the proposed amend-
ment of clause 74 of the Commonwealth Bill.]
* * * *
“ The War—lts Speedy and Successful Termination ”
was proposed by Sir George Dibbs. It seemed to him, he
said, an extraordinary thing that he, a man of unexampled
peace—(laughter)—should be called upon to propose a
toast which had to do with war. Without taking his
audience too much into his confidence, he might say he
had been essentially a man of peace. (Laughter). When
he went to church the one prayer which evoked his
heartiest response was “Give us peace in our time, O
Lord.” But there were two ways of maintaining peace.
His idea of the best way was never tor be stamped on
without your own consent. '(Laughter.) In the law
courts, in Parliament, and in other ways, his idea had
been to have his own way—to have it peaceably, if
possible. (Laughter.) If he might touch more imme-
diately on the theme, it had, moreover, bound us closer to
the mother-country. (Applause.) Now, to his mind, it
was only a question of time before England must conquer
the whole of Africa, and it would be to Australia’s credit,
as well as her interest, to assist to that end. Finally,
then, while they all wished for the speedy and successful
termination of the war, the best way to preserve peace
was to be prepared for war. (Cheers.)—G. R. Dibbs,
as per Sydney daily paper report, 20th April,
At one time, not so long ago either, George
Dibbs was practically a republican. He was a
republican, an Australian Nationalist, and a
believer in separation from Britain, right
up to the time when he went to England
and accepted (purely “to oblige a lady”) a
title from Queen Victoria. His republican
ideas fled when he got a large, black cigar
from the next heir to the throne and had
his paw vibrated by that personage. And
now 1
* * * *
George Dibbs, in the loyal fury of his old
age, demands that Australia shall bear a
hand at making all Africa British. To do
that means war with Germany, France,
Italy, Turkey, Spain, Morocco, the Arab
tribes of the Soudan, Abyssinia, the still
semi-independent Gold Coast kingdoms,
possibly with Portugal, and also with any
outside State which may think that Britain’s
difficulty is her opportunity. It means the
biggest fight that Britain ever saw, or heard,
or dreamt of. And when the fight is over, if
it ever is oyer, it means the addition of some
hundred million more niggers to the millions
of undigested niggers that Britain has
already to keep in order ; and millions more
square miles of territory to police; and
more frontiers to defend, and more enemies
to face, and more white man’s burden to
hump through a weary, waterless land.
• * * *
It is one thing to annex empty, or nearly
empty, countries in the temperate zone
where the Anglo-Saxon race can spread and
the Anglo-Saxon nation grow bigger. It is
another thing, but still not a very different
thing, to annex countries occupied by white
races who may be completely assimilated
by-and-bye, and become as British and as
enthusiastic in the defence of the British
Empire as the British themselves. But it
is quite a different thing to annex hundreds
of millions of brown and black and yellow
men—of Moslems, Buddhists, Brahmins,
idolaters, naked men, and men who are
mostly naked, and women to match. They
can t be racially assimilated ; if a process of
inter-marriage set in, the British nation
would vanish, and not leave a white
streak on the surface of all this blackness
and brownness and yellowness. They can’t
be politically assimilated ; even if they were
educated up to the British standard, Britain
can’t give them an equal franchise and let
the huge brown and black majority rule the
empire. In many cases they can’t even be
policed by scattering permanent British
settlements through their country, because
in many of their countries the Briton can’t
permanently reside. If their white con-
querors educate them, the time must come
when they will be so far educated that they
will demand to share in the government of
the empire—which will make trouble. If
their white conquerors don’t educate them,
they remain the same unreliable savages,
which makes the work of government all the
more dangerous. Britain is the very newest
and shortest-lived of the great world empires
and hasn’t yet begun to know how her great
annexation policy will pan out. It is only
within the last 50 years or so that she has
gone in for nigger-owning on a stupendous
scale. The question of how many niggers
one white man can keep in order is unsettled
as yet.
* * * *
At all events, it is certain that there must
be some number of niggers that are too many
niggers for even Britain to be responsible
for. Britain’s responsibilities are growing
far faster than her population, and the line
of armed men that she cfin spread round her
frontiers isn’t half as thick a line as it was
even in the memory of men now living. And
the kind of patriot who urges Britain to rush
in and annex all the niggers available is just
the same kind of evil genius as urged
Napoleon I. to over-balance himself reach-
ing out for the last scrap of Europe at
Moscow. To fight half Europe for the
possession of the great African desert and
the great African fever-swamps, and for the
right to keep order among some of the most
aggressive tribes on earth, is much the same
as fighting eight men for a venomous serpent.
But, if Dibbs’s proposition is a disastrous
one as regards Britain, it is still more so as
regards Australia. That wild, hairy enthusiast
proposes that this country, with a population
of only 4,000,000, should actively assist in
fighting France, Germany, Spain, Turkey,
Morocco, Abyssinia, Italy and some sundries,
in order to'tear from them their African
possessions. Dibbs himself, so far as the
world knows, has never seen a shot fired in
anger, except possibly when he became
cross while aiming at a native bear. The
worst enemy he encounters is a column of
figures that won’t add up properly in the
Savings Bank. If the awful war to which he
is sooling on his country did come in his
lifetime, he is too old to go to it; he would
remain at home and prance in a gorgeous
uniform at the head of his National Guard.
And under these circumstances
♦ * * *
It is questionable if any man over 65
should have a vote or be qualified to sit in
Parliament. Old men are mostly divided
into two classes—those who don’t want
reform because they won’t live to see the
benefits of it, and those who don’t care
what happens because they won’t, be, here
when the disastrous consequences arrive.
Ex-republican Dibbs appears qualified for
membership of both classes. He is too old
to be impressed into active military service,
and too old to expect, in the ordinary course
of nature, that he will be here when the
awful bill for his great war is presented for
payment. From the safe pinnacle of his
years he can propose almost anything. And
though Australia knows its Dibbstoo well to
take him seriously, the trouble is that other
countries don’t know him so well. He has
been three times Premier, and for many
years a notable figure in the polities of the
leading Australian province. He has left
his big, irresponsible ink-splash dabbed
largely and liberally on its history. He has
been received in England with attentions
which were probably owing only to his re-
markable length and the breeziness of his
manners, but which seemed to mark him as
a man of some political weight. People
outside Australia may easily make the mis-
take of taking Dibbs for a statesman.
* * * *
If there were a few dozen important poli-
ticians like Dibbs in the empire, that empire
would probably soon be ruined, and Aus-
tralia would be ruined first of all. The
politician who gets up apropos of nothing in
particular, and proposes ejecting three great
Powers and five secondary ones, all eight
being then at peace with us, if he is a big
enough man for his warning to be taken
seriously, is merely a first-class bungler for
giving these eight Powers notice to get
ready for the fray. When he is merely a
small political bygone, the ornament of a
gaudy and absurd company of doddering
volunteers, and a personage who peacefully
pursues the humble threepenny up and
down a column of a bank ledger, then his
obscurity may save him from being danger-
ous, but not from being ridiculous. Dibbs
is either dangerous or ridiculous, according
to his size. The fact that not one of the
Ministers present when the remarkable
utterance was made rose and explained that
Australia had no desire at present to eject
eight friendly nations out of Africa would
possibly leave an impression that they and
the people they represented were also
anxious to tear up Europe by the roots in a
general sort of way. No British statesman
goes round trying to make needless enemies
in this loud, roaring, off-hand fashion.
• * f *
The saying that the surest way to preserve
peace is to be ready for war is only half a
truth. To be ready for war is the surest
way to preserve peace, and it is also the
surest way to break it. The nation that is
ready for war often escapes being attacked
by other nations, but it also becomes so
inflated over its readiness that it generally
ends by itself attacking other nations. But
if a nation which isn’t ready for war grows
so inflated that it talks of fighting eight
other nations it is a painful situation. That
is Australia’s case, if Dibbs represents Aus-
tralia. So far this country has supplied,
for about three months, one-fortieth part
of the army which hasn’t yet managed to
extinguish a very small white nation—almost
the smallest white nation in the world. That
is no reason to begin talking about our
joyous readiness to help in a war against
eight good-sized nations—a war that might
last ten years or more. The alleged Boers
who are said to have contemplated seizing
London after their success at Majuba were
not a circumstance compared to this folly.
And Australia isn’t going to be always
mad. It doesn’t want to perpetually
seek the bubble debenture at the cannon’s
mouth. It isn’t going to conquer Africa
in general, nor Europe, nor Asia, nor
America. It isn’t going to send ColonelNEiLD
to burn Moscow a second time. Because it
has had a look-in at some small skir-
mishes with a nation that, all told, has less
than half the population of Sydney, it doesn’t
desire to quarrel with France, Germany,
Italy, Turkey, and Spain simultaneously. It
doesn’t want a war-debt of £100,000,000 or
so. It doesn’t propose to be a raging lunatic
all its life, nor does it want to make itself
ridiculous by offering advice to the British
Empire at banquets, nor to spend all its
existence killing people who have done it no
harm. In short, it repudiates Savings-Bank
manager Dibbs.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Joe Chamberlain’s Whim.
We thought in Australia that we were self-
governing communities in all that did not concern
British Imperial interests. We thought that, re-
solved on union of the haphazard provinces which
have taken root and grown in Australia, we could
unite. We thought that a measure of Federation
devised by the ablest among us, debated for years
by Press and Platform, approved by ten Houses of
Parliament, carried by vast majorities at a
referendum of the People, would be safe from
captious British interference. But we reckoned
without the influence of anti-Federal wire-pullers
in London. And we reckoned without Joe
Chamberlain. That miniature maker of E.npire;,
it is cabled, has given ear to the anti-Federal
cry, and has resolved upon submitt.ng the
Australian Commonwealth Bill to the British
Parliament with such alterations as in his Ja;t
Napoleonic mood seem fit. The British Cabinet
—a fine collection of fossil Tories —is, it appears,
ready to follow Chamberlain’s sphere-shaking
nod. So we are to learn this week —as Ireland
has already learnt—what measure of self-govern-
ment the British Tories are prepared to allow
“self-governing Australia.” .We are to see at
what price the British House of Commons values
the magnificent assistance recklessly given by
Australia to the British arms. We are to receive
our reward for self-sacrifice to England—even as
Ireland has received it. Our new Constitution is
to be thrown into the British melting-pot—and
how will it come out ? This much is clear—t. at
Chamberlain and his Cabinet {.for the Cabinet
does his bidding so blindly that he is the real Prime
Minister of Britain) allege their resolve to flout the
Australian majorities in the interest of the minori-
ties. This much is certain —that at the root of
the sectional clamour for amendnient in the Bill
lies the defeated anti-Federal opposition to th§
Bill. Australia bruised the serpent’s head, dnd it
crawls upoif'its belly to England to bruise her heel.
Well do the anti-Federalists know that even one
amendment will invalidate the whole Constitu-
tion. If the Bill comes back from London altered
in one iota, then it is not the Bill which Australia
approved—and short of another popular vote
there is no authority in Australia to approve it.
The Premiers cannot. The Parliaments cannot.
Nobody but the people can unsay what the people
has said. Nothing but another referendum can
overthrow the original referendum. And, if we
are to have another referendum, all the old
strife begins anew. The minorities will fight
their battle all over again. Our labor of years
may be brought to naught in a moment at the
whim of the British Parliament. The Transvaal
has found the worth of acknowledged indepen-
dence when it conflicts with the interests of the
British money-lender. Australia seems about to
learn the same lesson.

Which Csesar ?—A Privy Council Dilemma.
Quite a treatise could be written on the anomalies
of Privy Council law, but the following VvilL pet-
haps illustrate as effectively as anything thfi sanity
of the P.C. constitution. In one case; Thimble v,
HiLLj decided in 1879, the PriVy Council rapped
the majority of the Judges of N.S.W. Supreme
Court severely On the knuckles for refusing to be
bound by a decision of the English Court of
Appeal which conflicted witli a previous decision
of the local Supreme Court. (The case was with
reference tb the right to recbver a deposit in a
wagering contract from a stakeholder.) The
Privy Council thus laid it down that colonial
courts were to follow the Court of Appeal
on pain of being upset and abused by the
P.C. But the English Court of Appeal has
repeatedly refused to be bound by the de-
cisions of the Privy Council. In a bill of
lading case (Lkask v. Scott) about 1877 it refused
to follow the much-belauded Oassar, and expressly
stated that it was not bound by his authority.
Similarly in 18% in the case of Pugh v. London
and Brighton Railway Co. the Court of Appeal
treated a Privy Council decision, which is binding
on the Supreme Court of Victoria till the P.C.
changes its mind, as open to question. (If the
legal advocate of the retention of the P.C. appeal
wishes to learn how contemptuously its decisions
are treated, not only by the Court of Appeal, but
by the Irish courts, the American courts and even
the single judges of the English bench, let him
look up the case of Wilkinson v. Downton,
decided by the English Queen’s Bench Division in
1897.) The beautiful dilemma evolvable out of the
above would need a Gilbertian opera to do it
justice. The law tells the colonial Court to
follow the Privy Council, the Privy Council tells
the colonial Court to follow the English Court of
Appeal, and the English Court of Appeal refuses
to follow the Privy Council. It’s a lovely com-
mentary on the “ uniformity of law throughout
the Empire ” we hear so much about nowadays.
If a Judge in the colonies follows the P.C. in cer-
tain cases, he will assuredly be wrong, for the
P.C. has laid it down that he must follow the
Court of Appeal; and if he follows the Court of
Appeal in such cases he is also sure to be wrong,
for the law lays it down that he must follow the
Privy Council. The appeal to Cassar ? Welland
good, but xvhich Caesar ?
The “S.M. Herald’s” Tune Changes.
“There was a disposition soon after the war
began to credit the worst of the Boers as foemen.
Their bitter hostility and contempt prejudiced us.
Reminiscences of the former war were revived by
stories of abuse of the white flag, explosive
bullets, rigorous commandeering, and wilful
damage to the property of British colonists.
Later, much was said of the treatment of
prisoners and wounded in the hands of the Boers.
Under the influence of these reports the opinion
began to be formed tnat, brave as the Boers were
and skilful as soldiers, they were hardly a civilised
foe, or else they were fighting not as warriors but as
fanatics, as in a Moslem holy war upon the infidel.
By degrees that notion is being disproved. Our
wounded upon release acknowledge the humanity
of their captors, their readiness to relieve suffer-
ing by all the aid at their command, and their dis-
position to make the yoke as light as possible.
Our men in South Africa even fraternise to some
extent with the enemy in the Sunday* or other
truces of the campaign, and they find these stout
and ready enemies very good fellows apart from
the bitter business that brings them together. So
it was in the Napoleonic wars and in the Crimea.
While people at home abhorred the very name
of Frenchman or Russian, the troops in the
field struck up a rough fellowship with the
foe and learned each other's human side. The
truth about the Boers may be that some of
the abuses which have been declared arise
from their independent style of warfare. The
commandoes, and even the individual burghers,
fight much ‘for their own hand,’ and employ the
arms and ammunition which they are most
familiar with. In such circumstances there is
more scope for the misbehaviour of a few despera-
does or fanatics than in our more disciplined
ranks. Be it as it may, the legend of the Boer as
a sour, harsh, relentless foe is disappearing, and
we shall soon do justice to the better personal
qualities of our enemy, as well as to his splendid
courage and constancy in a desperate cause.”—
Sydney Morning Herald sub-leader , 11th May,
1900.
I»ex and Lazarus.
If a common garden trades-union were to suggest
that an apprentice to a carpenter, who after ordi-
nary working hours or after the conclusion of his
apprenticeship, does a little blacksmithing to fill
in time or earn an honest penny, should be forever
debarred from becoming a member of the carpen-
ters’ union or from exercising his trade except as a
“scab” subject to imprisonment, what a howl of
horror about the heinous breach of the principle
of freedom of contract, and of the law of compe-
tition would be raised, especially by lawyers !
But when the Legal Trades Union, backed up by
obsolete rules of court and a domestic forum of
judges sitting in semi-secrecy for such purposes
only, does the same thing to an apprentice to that
particular trade, it is quite another story. At
least, that is, up to the present, the experience of
young Lazarus, a would-be initiate into the Vic.
legal priesthood. Lazarus, it seems, has served
five years under articles and passing examinations
to fulfil tne conditions .of enrolment as a
barrister and solicitor of this .honorable court.
When he had fimsned his course, but before form-
ally applying to be admitted, he undertook some
private work for a period of months. He
then applied in the ordinary way for admission,
but the body of persons, future rivals of his in his


Erofession, who had the duty of looking through
is application-papers, found that one of these
documents (a sort of certificate of character)
stated that the person signing it had known the
applicant for (say) six years, and that during that
time he had not been engaged in any trade or pro-
fession, “except for the period of 3J months.’
This fearful exception damned Lazarus from the
table of Lex. The horrified “ Board ” rejected his
application. Lazarus appealed to the Judges of
the court, who, still more horrified, pronounced
the “ Get thee behind me, Satan ! ” And as their
decision is final, or alleged to be so in
so many word, Lex would appear to have
got even with the sacrilegious Lazarus.
The effect of such absurd restrictions upon the
poor will be undoubtedly to make the legal pro-
fession an even closer preserve for the rich than,
by reason of the heavy premiums payable for
articles, it is at present. All close corporations
are undesirable ; but close corporations entrusted
with the interpretation of the law, and confined
entirely to the rich, are odiously so. And no
reason on earth, save that of dog-in-the-manger
exclusiveness, can be assigned for a rule which
prevents a poor man, after having gone through
nis apprenticeship, from earning the high fee he
has to pay for his formal entry into mastership.
On the whole the incident shows what pranks
before high heaven can be played by trades
unionism, provided only it is a trades unionism of
the “classes,” and that it can claim the judges of
the land as its committee of management; and it
also shows what poison-stings lurk in our law, and
what throw-backs to the worst days of feudalism
luxuriate in its musty purlieus.

Workmen’s Compensation Legislation.
A democratic lawyer to The Bulletin :
The fact that the Australian provinces are so backward
in adopting with improvements the English Workmen’s
Compensation Act is by no means a good testimonial to
their alleged progress in social legislation, and is, in fact,
attracting attention in England. In a recent paper on the
subject, Rueog, a great English authority on the law of
employer and workman, says : “ Some such system as
that upon which our Workmen’s Compensation Act is
founded is well nigh common to the whole of the Con-
tinent. Germany, Austria, Norway, France, and, I be-
lieve, Switzerland, have it already in full operation. Italy
and even Russia have like schemes in contemplation. On
the other hand, it does not appear to have been yet
accepted by our colonies or by the United States.” Aus-
tralian attempts are mainly in the chrysalis stage, and
will probably result in the distant future in a feeble
imitation of the Chamberlain scheme with all its defects
and omissions. The ideal to be aimed at is, of course,
the absolute insurance by the employer against
accidents to his workmen in every form of
employment from chemical - factory hands to farm-
laborers, and from domestics to clerks, lead-workers,
sailors, and even soldiers. But failing that, great good
can be achieved by a reasonable extension of the employ-
ments specified in Chamberlain’s Act, and by freeing that
Act from its numerous technical defects. For the latter
purpose, every lawyer M.P. should procure, or should be
supplied with at public expense, a copy of the paper read
by Riiego above alluded to and published in several con-
secutive numbers of the English Law Times of this year.
This paper is the most comprehensive and searching
criticisim of the Chamberlain Act up to date, and for
Australian legislatures to copy Chamberlain’s Act with-
out the alterations suggested as necessary by a perusal of
Itu ego’s paper, would be simply flying in the face of ex-
perience and the best expert opinion.

When the Drunk is over.
The attitude of the Labor Party throughout Aus-
tralia re Transvaal War is not a highly edifying
one. The great mass of the Labor members know
and feel that the war is scandalously unjust ;
many of them make no bones about saying
so—in private—to their intimates ; but very few
of them have had the courage to openly say so
either on the platform or in Parliament. Pru-
dential motives undoubtedly underlie this new-
born modesty, but it is really most doubtful if the
policy, even from the standpoint of political ad-
vantage, be a sound one in the long run. . . . The
Jingo drunk soon passes ; the nation is even now
putting down its weary head on the pillow to sleep
off the intoxication; and the morrow (when the
grog-score is to be paid) will undoubtedly bring
second thoughts that may easily cause the men
who had the courage to protest against the war-
fever to be regarded in a very different light. The
final conclusions of the people ( vox populi ) are
nearly always right; the first impulses of the people
are nearly always wrong. The average English-
men of to-day bitterly condemns the American
war; the average Englishman of a little over a
century ago just as strongly approved of it.
Within twelve months of to-day the Australias
will regard the sending of the Contingents very
differently ; within 12 months the men who
opposed the mad business will be respected for
their pluck by many of those who most bitterly
opposed them. Had the various Australian Labor
Parties stood together and given a solid vote in
their respective Assemblies against our meddling
in this sordid crime, the chances are the next elec-
tions yvould have found them as strong as ever
numerically, and infinitely stronger from the
standpoint of morale and consistency. But no!
They tripped and they trembled; they went to
send-off banquets and made glowing speeches
glorifying the butcheries perpetrated by the
legionaries of Beit, Eckstein and Co. ; and so
gained the contempt of every true lover of free-
dom in the community. Many of them will yet
have reason for bitter repentance. It is a remark-
able fact that political ruin and extinction almost
immediately overtook every Queensland Democrat
who stumbled and fell over the disgraceful Q.N.B.
business. The afterthought of the electors con-
demned the dodgers and the dodgers died. And
the after-thought of the sober Australian will
operate in a similar manner j will make him
despise those who truckled to his drunken folly.

The “Apprentice System ” in Africa
and Elsewhere.
More of that furiously Jingo paper, Canterbury
(M.L.) Times
The Transvaal never observed the conditions. Its
existence as a State has been one continuous effort to
avoid its engagements with Britain. The Sand River Con-
vention, to which it owed its being, forbade slavery.
The Transvaal practised slavery, but called the slaves
apprentices.
Apart from the fact that the Transvaal was a free
State for many years before the Sand River Con-
vention, which was merely the first treaty entered
into between it and Britain (it doesn’t necessarily
follow that a country has no existence until it
makes a treaty with Britain], the complaint about
the “apprentice system" is interesting. That
utterly sinful and shameful and objectionable
system certainly does exist in the Transvaal, but
seeing that it also exists in at least two British
States, and the British Government winks at it,
no Briton has a right to put on any very lofty
airs on the subject. In Rhodesia the nigger
is “ supplied ” by his chief, who gets so
much money for him, or pays his taxes with
him, and, as the ‘ ‘ supplied ” nigger has no
more voice whatever in the matter, he is a slave
for the_ time being. And in Westralia the
apprentice system is in full blast. In Westralia,
the nigger, male or female, can be apprentieea
(“indentured”) for a term of years, or various
terms in succession, simply by drawing up the
necessary agreement binding the said nigger, and
getting a magistrate, J.P., or policeman to wit-
ness that the colored person entered into the con-
tract quite willingly, and under no compulsion
whatever. This is a beautifully convenient
arrangement, especially in the outlying districts
where every squatter is a J.P., so that when one
captures a few niggers the other can certify that
they entered willingly into a bond—rushed at it,
in fact —to serve him for a long time without any
wages. And even the pretence of voluntary ser-
vice is hardly kept up —as witness a recently-re-
ported case where a J.P., on a more than usually
intelligent aborigine refusing to be apprenticed,
sentenced him to six monthk gaol as a vagrant,
with the intimation that the sentence would not be
enforced provided he consented to become a slave.
As to how they are treated—some 12 years ago a
wandering person in these parts wrote an account
of flogging, starvation, murder, and all-manner of
ill-usage, which made the hair of Australasia in
general stand up for a moment—and then lie down
again in forgetfulness. And how long is it since
two W. A. squatters were tried, convicted, and not
hanged, for flogging sundry nigger apprentices
(women and children included) within an inch of
their lives, and some actually to death ? The
apprentice system is, in one sense, worse than
ordinary slavery. The old slave-owner maintained
his serfs when they were too young to work ;
under the apprentice system, the nigger shifts for
himself when he is young, and is turned out to
scramble for his own precarious’possum when he
is old. And this slavery exists under the direct
sanction of the British Government, which had
the right to veto the Bill under which the infamous
device was first established—and didn’t. And yet
the apprentice system in the Transvaal is too
shocking for words, and no Briton can contem-
plate it, in the Transvaal, without a shudder.
For goodness sake let us do our own local shudder
first.

Labor Agencies.
When politics are slack, The Bxtx.letin likes to
rise now and then and speak a little curse about
the Labor Agency swindle, and as it is a long time
since the last curse on that over-present iniquity
this appears to be as good a time to curse as any
other. N. 8. W. is aland of quacks, where most things
are allowed to slide, lest by interference some v
ruffian’s freedom to injure his neighbors maybe
curtailed, and the glorious fabric of Liberty be
thereby brought to the ground. So any man who
can get an office and a counter can be a bank or a
life insurance society, and the man who has lost
his billet driving a ’bus can turn doctor or dentist,
and the insolvent shopkeeper can be a kidney-
specialist, and almost anyone can delude the
public by protending to be almost anything, pro-
vided he doesn’t pretend to be a lawyer. If he
does that he is touching the sacred six-and-eight-
ponny Ark of the Covenant, and things fall on
him ; for Parliament is full of lawyers, and their
yearning to save their own branch of business from
the intrusion of boguspracti tioners, when there isn’t
enough Cs. Sd.togo roundthe real ones, ispathetio. In
Sydney, as the biggest city in the land of Let-it-Slide,
the bogus labor-agent flourishes. He needn’t be a
man of good character. He can charge any fee
he likes, and in these times of terrible competition
for work he can get large fees (to be deducted
from future wages) for a very small job. Very
often the Sydney labor-agent simply copies on to
his board “ wanted ” advts. from the morning
papers, minus the address at which application is
to be made, and the applicant, or the 24 appli-
cants, must register themselves with him and pay
a fee before being sent to the prospective em-
ployer. Though the billet is filled before the
agent’s customer gets there, and the employer
gave that special agent no authority to send appli-
cants or take fees, and his tacit pretence that the
billet was put specially in his hands is all a lie,
there is no redress unless the hard-up applicant
likes to spend money and weeks of time on a
prosecution. Also, there is no legal remedy
against the agent who divides the fee with the
employer. The agent’s fee is, say, two
weeks’ wages, and when the two weeks are
up, and the dead horse of the fee has
been worked off, the employer sacks his new hand
as being “unsuitable,” and gets another on the
same terms, whereby the agent has a steady job
and the employer gets men at half-wages. This is
a great and flourishing swindle, and the law takes
no cognisance of it. Nor does it take any special
notice of the worst fraud of all, whereby the
agent gets several pounds as a fee in return for .
providing a good but wholly imaginary situation
at, say, Bourke, whither the fortunate applicant
proceeds at his own expense—the object in send-
ing him so far off being that he may exhaust bis
funds and so be unable to get back and maul the
man in the office. If labor-agents were licensed
and it was heavily punishable for any unlicensed
person to go into the trade, and the licensing law
was enforced (which comparatively few N.S.W.
laws are), a reasonably-efficient remedy would be
Erovided. The fraudulent person would simply
e thrown out of the business, and by much
throwing out it would gradually become clean.
As things are the agent usually makes £lOO by
fraud before Justice falls with great weight upon
him and fines him £5, so even when Justice does
find him very little good results. Victoria now
requires labor agents to be registered, fixes the
scale of fees payable, and fires the swindling
practitioner out—rather gradually, it is true, but
still he gets out in the long run. Some American
states do even better : in New York, for example,
not only are fees fixed by law, and agents required
to be of good character, but the agent who takes a
fee from an applicant must find him genuine work
inside one month or return the money in full. This
is so simple and effective a way of meeting the
difficulty that it may strike even the Australian
politician some day.
Breach of Promise.
It is easy to discover absurdities in Breach of
Promise verdicts, but an absurdity equal to any
of them is to argue that therefore the Breach of
Promise Law shall be wiped off the books. An
engagement to marry is, and should be, a solemn
engagement. If one party desires to retire that
party should reasonably compensate the other, if
the other demand compensation. This applies to
the woman as well as the man. But it is obvious
that a woman is generally more damaged than a
man by such a breach. The man whom a woman
rejects after engagement does not necessarily de-
teriorate in the matrimonial market. The woman,
after being tied up under such an engagement
generally has to seclude herself from matrimonial
pursuits until at least the memory of her lately
engaged’s embraces has died down. She has not
alone been caged by her lover’s promise while
his promise lasted, but has actually been
quarantined by him when he broke it.
Further, the case has this business-like, though
grossly-material, aspect. While a woman’s wage
is so much less than a man’s, marriage is necessarily
to a woman a means of support. The woman who
is jilted loses, in a business sense, her promised
home and clothes and victuals. The man who is
jilted only loses, in a business sense, the expen-
sive privilege of supplying a woman with home
and clothes and victuals. Therefore, while the
woman has a good business claim for damages, it
is doubtful if the man has any. If Smith and
Jones make a contract whereby Smith agrees to
pay Jones £lOOO, and Smith breaks the contract
and doesn’t pay, Jones has a right to sue Smith
for breach of agreement. But if Jones refuses to
take the £lOOO, has Smith a right to sue him on
the ground that Jones didn’t fulfil his end of the
agreement ? Of course, either party has a right
to ask compensation for disappointment and loss
of married happiness. But the Court doesn’t
know that he or she might not have been much
more disappointed if the marriage had happened,
and it has no right to take any account of the
prospective married happiness. There is no proof
that there would have been any.

Whoop !
“Dingo” implores this country to keep its tail
up, and cultivate a judicious enlargement of the
head and a good, loud cock-a-doodle-doo :
A bitter wail has arisen from the Australian Nationalist.
He has watched the departure of the various contingents
sick at heart. He has viewed the wild hysteria of the
public with contemptuous pity. He has raged at the
vapourings of the public man. He has visions of the
Australia of the future, and he firmly believes that the
madness of the moment has swept away all that has been
labored for by the Nationalists of the last 20 years. The
Australian Nationalist is nearly as one-eyed as the Jingo
public man. Busy in his throes, he has not yet had time
to discover that the present war is pregnant with
more possibilities of nationalism than all the talk of the
past two decades. The success of a nation depends, like
the success of the individual, more on bluff and bluster
than on merit; and, until the past few weeks, Australia
has made the huge mistake of trying to live on merit
alone. What merit it had was not even recognised by its
own people. The Bulletin, the organ of nationalism,
sat on all attempts at Australian braggadocio, barracked
one-sidedly and shamelessly for the English cricketers
during every tour, and never failed to uphold the Aus-
tralian as the physical, mental, and moral inferior of the
British. The result has been that all Australia has had
an utter contempt for such of its own products as had
not received the hall-mark of English approbation. Aus-
tralian art and literature were things despised inside their
own country, and the more idea of an Australian being a
soldier was regarded as a joke. It has taken the veldt of
Kensburg to change all that. The Australian who took
time to consider would have remembered that the great
test of war is courage and coolness in times of danger,
and that the records of Australian wrecks, mine-disasters,
floods and bush-fires had supplied enough of t hese for a
couple of bushels of Victoria-crosses. But ho didn’t take
time to consider. The nationalist burst into wild de-
nunciation of the troops as comic opera warriors, and
even the loyal Naicest did not regard them seriously.
Their acceptance was deemed merely complimentary, and
those loudest in their patriotism trusted in their own
minds that the first contingents Would be kept blacking
officers' boots or doing something which wouldn’t Bhow
their hopeless incompetence. What has been the result?
The undrilled Australian youth has shown himself not
only the equal but the superior of English Tommy Atkins.
The compliments of the British generals go for nothing,
for they may have been dictated by motives of policy, but
money talks. A cheque is always the sincerest form of
criticism, and when the British Government is wiling to
pay Australians ss. a day when they can get any number
of men at Is., there is only one inference. Making all
allowance for the unusual circumstances of the present
war, the fact has been established that the Australian is a
first-class fighting man. He was not previously
aware of this fact, and with the discovery he has
suddenly awakened to a sense of his own importance.
To base the value of a nation on its soldiering qualities
is, of course, the height of foolishness, but the Austra-
lian is as foolish as the rest, and, in his illimitable
modesty, aided by the English-born residents and the
patriotic Bulletin, he has hitherto deemed himself of
inferior clay. To-day the Australian knows he is a
soldier, and, like one of her own warriors, Australia itself
is beginning to throw out its chest and strut among the
nations of the earth. The strong and self-reliant Aus-
tralia of the next few years will stand no nonsense from
anybody—not even from England. The spirit of Briticism
abhorrent to the nationalist has no real existence in the
ranks of the troops or the hearts of the people. It is merely
the hysteria of the hour which leads to all great movements,
good or bad. The men of the Contingent went to fight
for England just as much as the Greek athlete raced for
the actual laurel sprig. And it was his troubles what
tree the sprig was picked from. In connection with the
present trouble in South Africa there was a very big pro-
portion of Boer sympathisers until the actual opening of
hostilities sent them over to the British side. A discovery
that the Australian can make as good a soldier as the best
of them has caused Australia’s head to swell—to its
proper size—and without a slightly-swollen head national
sentiment is impossible. The war has also indicated the
physical perfection of the Australian. How often did the
wise men of the Australian papers tell the diffident and
credulous Cornstalk that he was not as good a man as his
father ? How often did a fat, bloated and more or less
blasted person arise in a local legislature and compare
his own shapeless build and fatted deportment with those
of the Australian youth to his own great advantage ? And
yet none of them were stoned or rotten-egged or hooted
’ as they would have been in any other country in the
world. It is years since England has produced a sculler
that could keep the rank-and-file of Australian second-
raters warm. It is even longer since she could send a
pug into the ring capable of holding his own with
the average beer-fighter of this country, and when-
ever casual Australians have arrived at English uni-
versities they have at once jumped into the places
of the foremost athletes. England is a worn-out,
effete and befogged country, whose every grain of dust
has been decomposed human being, and whose City resi-
dents live huddled together, with little to eat and less to
breathe. The Australian, descended from the very pick
of England, is born and bred in the sunshine. His eight
big towns are all coastal ones, and are swept on one side
by the eucalyptic breezes of the bush and on the other by
the fresh sea airs, and are small enough to give everybody
room to move. His climate is such that he uses his house
to eat and sleep in, but little else, and in encouraging him
to live in the open air and breathe oxygen it also en-
courages him to develop his muscles, whereby the
uniforms of the British soldier could not meet round his
chest when he got to Capetown.
Dr. Ashburton Thompson, President of Sydney
Health Board, says about certain slum proper-
ties :
“ Walls one-brick thick saturated with damp, floors
resting on the earth, and so ricketty that you could put
your shoulder through them, and owned, I regret to say,
by a well-known medical man. And the local council,
instead of bringing the owner before a magistrate, has
contented itself with talking about the filthy habits of the
Assyrians, and writing letters to the board with regard to
the alleged over-crowding.
There is a municipal yell in N.S.W. for “more
power.” What for—more effectually to screen the
slum-landlord ? When the landlord has been de-
prived of his monopoly of municipal voting-power,
and aldermen cease to be his obsequious delegates,
it will be time enough to think about giving
municipalities “ more power.” And that won’t be
till property-qualification and plural voting is
abolished.

If we are to credit Gibbon that St. George was a
Cappadocian purveyor of embalmed pig, and
possibly saddles, to the Syrian and Alexandrian
contingents of the Roman army, it is legitimate
to infer that the legend of his killing the dragon is
an esoteric rendering of his success at repealing an
Adulteration Act of the period If so, there is a
great need for dragons in Australia, and we don’t
know that bas-reliefs of dragons killing St.
Georges wouldn’t appropriately and suggestively
grace every Senate House in Australia. Beer,
pickles, sausages, jam, wine, meat, bread, groceries
of every feather, milk of every aqueousness, fruit
of every flavor from paris-green to tobacco-juice—
even white oyster soup—and all adulterated.
Faugh ! “ slinks,” salicylic acid, grains of para-
dise, anthracised beef, cancerous pig, alum
(so much so that bakers may be dubbed
the almuni of industry), poisoned lollies
for our babies, rotten tinned-fisn for South Sea
Islanders, Canton tea - shop floor sweepings
mineralised into saleability, typhoid - sprinkled
oysters—such are of the stock in trade of the St.
Georges of the modern popular armies. They
must be got rid of, these Cappadocians, even if we
have to canonise them afterwards. But they
never will be got rid of unless all parties combine
to have a sweeping and comprehensive Adultera-
tion Act placed on every Australian statute-book.
This is no party question; it affects all consumers.
It’s no good pottering now at wine, now at meat,
now at milk. All deleterious adulteration is
criminal, and should be legislatively declared so :
all adulteration is cheating and should be rated
accordingly. Wanted, several dragons warranted
to extirpate the myriad St. Georges who are
infesting the community.
Police-Superintendent Brennan’s report to In-
spector-General Fosbery on the present N.S.W.
method of killing mongrel and mangy stray dogs
is a self-complacent and highly policeman-like
document. A raid was made in Sydney during a
few days lately, and of 2026 dogs captured 1761
were destroyed “on the same principle as cattle
are at the abbatoirs—the force is applied at the
base of the skull, known as the foramen ma</num.”
Had the report stopped here the community could
have gone on demanding the long-promised lethal-
chamber, in the belief thepoliceauthoritiescon-
curred ; but the report remarks that “ the various
experiments made for killing dogs inapainless man-
ner by scientific processes, by aspl ivxiation and other
chemical agencies have failed, besides being ex-
pensive and, finally, “This method (the abat-
toir one) is the least painful and cruel that can be
devised.” If the police be allowed their way,
that report will kill and bury the present
quickened sense of horror at a needless barbarity.
The abattoir system passes for cattle, as those are
food animals ; were they not food animals the
method of killing would be as monstrous in their
oase as in that of stray dogs. But with the
proved efficiency of lethal chambers in European
and American countries boforo the public the
alleged fact of thoir failure in Sydney merely tes-
tifies to the blundering bungling of those who
managed the local experiments. Mr. Brennan’s
assurance that breaking the base of a dog’s skull
with an iron bar “is the least painful and cruel
method that can bo devised ” reveals the self-
confidence of the solemn bobby frowningly front-
ing the science and humanity of the age.

♦ — :
The Lyne Govt, promises to reform the rotten
Sydney Corporation by extending the franchise
and making the Mayor elective. That is better
than the nominee-Commission idea, which held
suggestions of job ; but why not adopt the
Greater Sydney scheme while the Govt, is about
it ? Sydney can’t afford to abandon the rate-
payers’ right of election in favor of Ministerial
nominees, because light filters into the misdoings
of an elective body sooner or later, while into
those of a nominee body it always filters later—
and the devil you do know is better than
the devil you don’t. But the newspaper
clamour for the abolition of Sydney Corporation
has paved the way for the reception of the Greater
Sydney idea; and the advocates of that idea
should set to work to urge it more vigorously than
ever—with the English County Councils for model
and example. The great essential in all electoral
reform is to widen your franchise —increase your
electorate. Small electorates breed small repre-
sentatives ; big electorates may not breed big
representatives, but they breed bigger. The man
with 10,000 voters at his back has less to fear from
“ influence ” than the man with only 1000. Hole-
and-corner methods live in a hole-and-corner con-
stituency : they die in space and sunlight.

Sydney Telegraph, which runs the N.S.W. Govt,
just at present—and is posted free all over N.S.
Wales at the expense of the tax-payers—has been
bubbling over with new ideas lately, and the pro-
cess of converting the Telegraph’s new ideas
into active N.S.W. politics is being watched with
interest by people who know the Tele-
graph’s board of directors. One plague
morning lately the Telegraph aroused its
readers with the conviction that a British
hygienic expert by the name of Simpson must be
imported at once, at a fee of £5OOO or so, to con-
duct the fight against the filthy Sydney property-
owner and the bubonic Sydney rat. Somebody,
alleged to be a person of vast wisdom—name
not given, so you took the Telegraph s word
for it —had alleged that only Simpson could
save us in this unparalleled crisis. Next
day and the day after letters dropped in
from “Banker” and “Citizen” and “Public
Opinion ” and similar gentry —names not given—
urging the community to take Simpson for that
plague feeling. Then a Telegraph writer
—name not given—was bidden to point out that
Simpson’s claims to come to Sydney immediately,
and do such vague things unspecified as might
seem good to him, were claims which no city
could afford to disregard. Then, very suddenly,
Simpson dropped out of the Telegraph alto-
gether. Why? Has Govt, bitten sub rosa —or
wouldn’t Govt, bite? What has become of Simp-
son ? The Telegraph readers call with one vast
voice for answer.

American Republic are heart and soul with the
Republics of the S’Africa. It is cabled that only
one member of the New York Municipal Council
dissented from the proposal to officially welcome
the Boer peace delegates, and the burden of his
song was that to show sympathy with the visitors
would be to exhibit ingratitude to England for her
display of friendship towards the U.S. in the
Yanko-Spanish war. As a cold-blooded matter of
business Uncle Sam does owe England a good
turn. She stood by while the Yankees rounded
off their tobacco monopoly by stealing the Philip-
pines from Spain; as a fair deal it is now for
Uncle Sam to stand by while England rounds off
her practical gold-monopoly by stealing the glit-
tering Rand.

Rev. lan Maclaren, the famous Scottish author-
preacher, is not afraid to speak the truth about
the war:—
From the highest unto the lowest, priests and people
together, we have been growing mercenary, worldly,
materialistic. Still we grew richer and richer and one
could not tell how we were to be punished, for with our
riches we built iron ships and boasted of our security.
Is anyone so blind now as not to see how our greed of
wealth has brought us into our present state and has led
to the death of so many brave men ? . . . The immediate
occasion of this disastrous war was the desire of a pastoral
people to retain the control of their own country and the
determination of a handful of mine-owning millionaires
to seize it for their own ends. They were not them-
selves brave enough to fight and now they are not
generous enough to being as mean now as they
were cowardly before, but they were cunning enough to
induce a gang of criminal adventurers to make that Raid
for which we are all now paying, in sorrow or in blood,
and, in the end, to set by the ears our great Empire and
this little nation of country folk. Who had been so
eager for war as the baser herd of gamblers on our Stock
Exchange, who are the disgrace of an honorable business ?
Who were so ready yesterday to sing patriotic songs and
drink patriotic toasts, and who but they have had the
heart to play upon the feelings of the nation in their most
sensitive hours by lying rumors of victory, so that
whether we laugh or weep those brigands may reap their
ill-gotten gain, won at the expense of men’s blood and the
nation’s regret ?
Which is just what everyone thinks in his own
heart, but how many have the nerve to say so ?
