Elizabeth, in her character as the Great
Flossie of English History, gave place to “ School
for Scandal” at Her Majesty’s last Saturday.
This scholastically-entitled drama belongs to the
class wherein the characters are named according
to their qualities, our ancestors evidently being
unable to comprehend that Lady Sneerwell would
sneer unless her cognomen in the programme
acquainted them with the fact, and allowed it to
soak well into their 18th - century intelligences
before the curtain rose. The drama is really two
plays badly joined together, and the last act was
written while thefirst four were being rehearsed, the
manager having locked Sheridan in his room and
deprived him of his daily beer till the play was
written. Nowadays it is the playwright who locks
the manager in a room till the play is read. At
Her Majesty’s the drama was given with most of
its 18th century spiciness, said spiciness being
bluer in language but less blue in ideas than the
modern variety. A glossary of old-time slang
should be handed round the gallery, which, other-
wise, cannot be expected to identify the old-time
macaroni with the modern mash. Also, the top-
lofters choke perplexedly over their peanuts while
wondering why the frugal Sir Peter should object
to his wife driving a pair of white cats. Cats are
always cheap.
* * * *
Nance O’Neil is too large for her part,
and she gambols in a serious elephantine manner,
the shadow of Elizabeth being still heavy on her
soul. Her dressing might be more tasteful, and
she loses her reputation in an unemotional lop-
sided hat, large enough to render the famous
screen quite unnecessary. Kingston is much more
convincing wearing the white silk of a gay
Lothario than in the tweed garments of a modern
Angel Clare. Becks’ Sir Peter Teazle howls like
a lost dog when chaffed about his wife’s goings-on.
Otherwise there is nothing original in his imper-
sonation. The present scribe is not sure if even
the howl is original, or whether it was the regular
custom of high-class gentlemen of the 18th century
to make observations in dog language when their
wives were found behind screens in bachelor
apartments. Nance was billed to fight against
her avoirdupois and struggle manfully with her
too-majestic figure in the cause of Frolicsomeness
for six nights only, after which she will figure in
“ Oliver Twist.” It is not true that she is to play
the small, good boy, who gives his name to the
production.
* Sf * *
The Lyceum still continues with its tale of
the Soldier and the Man who is neither one thing
nor the other. He isn’t a soldier because he dis-
obeys orders, and he isn’t much of a man because
whenever trouble comes to him he forthwith takes
to drink, and can only be yanked back to the
dingy kitchen and domestic tea-cup by his wife
threatening to do just as he does, whereupon he
sees how awful two of him would be in this dark
and dreary world. Moreover, when he is saved
from the executionary bullet by the faithful
friend-fellow-soldier, he never takes the slightest
notice of it but tells his pa that nobody was good
to him. Probably he knew the value of his own
life best.
• * • •
“ East Lynne,” the wettest play on re-
cord, was given in its newest and dampest at the
Criterion on Saturday night. According to Dam-
pier, it is the tale of a genial, clumsy idiot named
Carlyle, who hadn’t the sense to tell his wife that
he only flew about with the other girl as a lawyer
and for the purpose of saving her brother’s neck.
The wife is a plump lady who is somewhat too pre-
cipitate, and when the villain tells her that he can
see Hubby hugging the other girl, believes it in-
stead of having a peep herself—which is very un-
womanly. It is a tale belonging to the great
Moral Ages of this century, as is seen by the vir-
tuous conduct of the genial idiot aforesaid
when he allows his divorced wife to die
standing up without any assistance, and will
not contaminate himself by touching her till she
is practically dead, when he holds her up with
great gentleness. Perhaps this trait was much
admired when “East Lynne” was first staged,
long ago. The curious point about the Lady
Isabel drama is that whereas, now-a-days, her
return to her husband is looked upon as a beauti-
ful and pathetic incident much to be commended —
by the fair authoress and old-time readers it was
regarded as a thoughtless, if not a rather ill-
bred proceeding which reflected somewhat dis-
creditably on Lady Isabel’s education in the rules
governing the Naicest.
• * * »
The long, difficult, moist part of Lady
Isabel is laid on the ample shoulders of Miss Lily
Dampier, and the part of her pleasant-mannered,
crass husband is supported by Alfred Rolfe, while
villain Levison stalks abroad in the person of
Benson North. Miss Dampier’s voice strongly
resembles Papa’s, who, by the way, stands out
of the caste altogether. Miss Lily Dampier is
somewhat stout and the average man can quite
understand the lawyer being vastly interested in
sad, simple, sister Rose who is so anxious to
save her brother’s neck. For in this play Rose
Dampier speaks with her own natural voice, which
is a great deal more pleasant than the stage article
she used to wear in “ Robbery under Arms,” while
Lady Isabel uses her special dying voice all the
time, except when she rises to real tragedy at the
death of her little boy. In that death-scene Miss Lily
is hideously real, and her utter abandon, her mad
desire to be recognised and called mother by her
own child is terrific—and then the sudden chilled
silenceasthe consciousness that he is dead suddenly
smites her is heart-breaking. Miss Clements and
several other people do good work, and at the
finish each person is observed to rise up softly and
instinctively reach for a spectral towel as the
vision of angels and baby boy is wafted aloft out
of sight.
* * * Mf
“ The Tyranny of Tears,” Haddon Cham-
bers’ new drama now making its debut at the
Royal, purports to be a lecture to women on the
futility of trying to over-rule their husbands by
Niobe methods; but as this particular tear-
distiller generally does manage to get her own way
by this process, the moral seems, somehow, to have
t?cme astray. Likewis 3 there is a calm sensible
girl in the drama who never receives anything
from life till she also starts a little emotionalism,
whereupon she gets a proposal and another man
to be a brother to her, and various other incentives
towards future indulgence in the masterful tear.
There is a further valuable moral, inasmuch as
when the tear-spiller and his wife separate by
mutual dissent, she comes up next day spick and
span from hat to heel, whereas he meanwhile has
taken to late hours and champagne for breakfast,
and tan boots and other dissipations of a like
nature. Altogether the drama is one of Brough’s
best and most charming ; and as a tear-shedder of
the first water Miss Grace Noble, who acts its
showery heroine, cannot be beaten.
* * * *
Mrs. Brough acts with her usual versa-
tility as the sensible, snappy girl who is one of the
usual large, poverty-stricken, many-daughtered
family that used the inked string in their shoes
and handed down the family frock from generation
to generation and wore the ancestral boots by
turns. The doubt as to whether the kiss she gave
the married man’s photo, was motherly or not will
probably be the psychological question of the day.
Miss Grace Noble, as before hinted, did well as
the womanly woman who rather goes back on her
womanliness by listening to reason in the last act,
and celebrating her weep on hubby’s kindly chest.
For the rest —Dartrey evinced his usual genius for
acting the polished and disreputable rake, and
none of the other members call for a single curse
from the public in general or this well-satisfied
scribe in particular.
* * *
The only new features at the Tivoli are
comedian Billy Maclain and songstress Madame
Cordelia, both late of the M‘Adoo co., which has
mostly gone back to the Swanee River, or there-
abouts. The worthy Billy’s larynx is supposed to
be fitted with a special squeaker, for the dark
comedian can make more remarkable noises than
any other mummer this writer ever struck. The
Martinettis now indulge in “A Duel in the
Snow,” the setting of which is very effective, and
if the Martinettis didn’t waste so much time
bashing each other on the various physiognomical
points and were content with stabbing the worthy
Dr. where the selectors brand calves, it would
be much more amusing. Sixteen hours of bashing
on the proboscis and on the point gets wearisome.
However, there is one thing Paul Martinetti can
do thoroughly, and that is—Die. It is worth
going a long way to see the white-faced gentleman
give up the ghost, and if ever this scribbler should
abjure the tynewriter for the sock and buskin he
will hunt up Paul Martinetti to learn how to die
artistically.
Melbourne Alexandra, now deceased, was
a cold inhospitable place where the ghost rarely
walked with vigor. It had many bad seasons and
few good ones during the 14 years, or so, that
have seen its rent decline since Miss Julian Syd-
ney and Captain de Burgh tried their luck in the
new building. The present writer will never for-
get the first night of the Aleck. There was a
powerful odour of paint and a strong foreboding
of failure in the crowded auditorium that night.
Miss Sydney spoke a prologue, and referred to
herself as a weak woman who was going to do her
best, &c., but the lady couldn’t have been as weak
as the three-act farce she was introducing to
notice. Robert Inman, Oily Deering, and young
Poulton were important items in the farce.
Their ill-fated efforts have somehow lingered
in the present scribe’s memory, together with the
smell of the paint, and he takes this suitable
opportunity to state that he thinks they acted for
the best. Let bygones be bygones. When the
first Alexandra season was white with frost, the
management called Phil Day to the rescue. Great
Phil Day, the original Australian Jacques in
“Silver King,” and the immortal Bosco Blithers,
who used to “call about a hat.” Poor Phil was
paid (or owed) a big salary to play the star part—
written for J. L. Toole—in “Uncle Dick’s
Darling,” but the magnet of his name
couldn’t draw much money to the Alex-
andra.. Nobody did any good there till
Martin Simonsen came along with a capital Italian
opera co. and an enthusiastic little crowd of sub-
scribers, who had found the money to give the
spec, a fair start. The co., headed by Ciuti, Laz-
zarini, and Tagliavia, caught the musical taste at
boom-time. Melbourne’s renaissance of Italian
opera lasted for 13 weeks, and put more than
£4OOO into Simonsen’s pocket, and, at one place
and another, finishing up with a return visit to
their first camp, he kept the same artists together
for 12 months. Simonsen’s third Italian co. (his
second co. came to grief and wrangles at Melb.
Opera House) was also produced at the Aleck, not
many years ago. It was there that Signora Cut-
tica, delightful singer and charming artist, made her
first Australian appearance, but Melbourne wasn’t
taking any shabbily-mounted opera. Poor old
Simonsen suspended payment once again, and
W. and M. took over the tuneful Cuttica contin-
gent. Henry Bracy made some money at the
Aleck with his “Beggar Student” co. ; likewise
Dampier had more than one prosperous time in
the dingy barn. His best time was when every
woman in Melbourne had a mind to see Lily
Dampier play a long, lugubrious part in “The
World Against Her.” Dan Barry could some-
times make business lively at the Aleck, and
brief amateurish ventures, extending over a season
of one night, or a week or two, were frequently
lucky. But the showmen, Bland Holt among
them, who shook the dust of the Aleck from their
feet, and kicked themselves for having tried to
improve its character, were many and various.
Farces, comedies, melodramas, opera—light,
medium, and heavy, musidal comedies, high-class
plays, also circuses and wild beasts have failed
ignominiously at the late unlamented establish-
ment. As often as not the failure served some-
body right.
* • » *
J. C. Williamson’s talk, last, year of retiring
from Melbourne management and going to
England and America and confining his enterprise
to Sydney, &c., &c., (all of which was taken with
grains of salt by people who were watching his
diplomacy) seems to have ended in a determination
to do exactly what he said he would never do
.again— viz., divide his interests equally between
Melbourne and Sydney. He booked a three
months lease of Melb. Alexandra in the first place,
but as soon as he found ex-partner Musgrove de-
termined to run the Princess’s in person,
Williamson (at his new house) took hold of the
“Aleck” property and made up his mind to
“maintain the traditions of the theatre with
which he had been so long associated in Spring-
street.” Melbourne is glad to find that J.C.W.
was only “spoofing ” when he bade the city adieu.
** * *
“ The Gates of Bondage,” the last drama
Eut up at the Alexandra, was in some respects a
etter-acted version of “The Deemster” than
Wilson Barrett gave us under the title of “Ben-
my-Chree.” Undoubtedly the Woods-Williamson
crowd should have started business with “The
Gates,” instead of the dreary patriotic piece of
foolishness in which they first took the floor.
Woods, as Dan Mylrea, looked very like a real
demoralised outcast when he got cut off from
Manx society, and was left to work out lonely sal-
vation on a fish diet. Also, he made a good impres-
sion throughout the duel scene, and the duel itself
surpassed all previous death struggles at the
Alexandra. Godfrey Cass’s furious attack on
Woods was a fine, blood-lusty example of nimble
histrionics. The young man got worsted in the
end because the drama had to turn upon his
corpse, so to speak, but The Bulletin hopes to
see him playing indestructible heroes in the near
future. Cass, in kharki, versus the Enemies of
the Empire would make a stirring fight.
** * *
Her Majesty’s Theatre (late Alexandria)
will be opened by J. C. Williamson, on Saturday,
with a revival of “ Pinafore.” It goes without
saying that the house will be opened in first-class
style, and as regards “Pinafore” everybody
knows it for a satirical, whimsical, nautical opera
wherein the gallant captain says “ Dam my ; ” and
the royal ballerinas darice a hornpipe of the very
finest quality.
*# * *
“ What Happened to Jones ” is happening
for the fifth week at the Princess’s, and the vital
energy of that neatly - balanced joke has not
abated. Arnold’s show travels with the joyful
consciousness of having caught on. In the lan-
guage of Lohr, it is “ All right ” ; also, “ it would
disturb the serenity of an owl.” And a further
matinee of “ Jones ” is announced for this Satur-
day (19th).
* *
“ The Ladder of Life ” at the Royal is
another cause of large audiences. The Gordon
Highlanders have carried all before them fora
good many nights, but their gallantry doesn t
appear to pall upon the patriots in front. They
go on charging for England while the management
charges for admission and whistles God Save the
Queen.

At the Bijou things move along peacefully
Amann is giving them Cronje, Henry Parkes,

Irene Franklin warbles her “ Trade Mark ” with
kind attention in the same fetching little way.
** * *
It is hard to account for the queer bursts
of apathy and enthusiasm on the part of the
Melb. public in matters theatrical. Just now
there is a rush to all the available houses ; a few
months ago only one could expect to have its seats
decently covered for six nights a week; a month
or two ahead a dread emptiness may again freeze
up dramatic enterprise. General prosperity is
not responsible, as that remarkable veteran is not
more obvious now than he has been for two years
or so, and the butcher, baker, and candlestick-
maker are sending up doleful complaints to high
heaven, bewailing the sickness in trade, and the
belief that money lias vanished from the face of
the earth.
A Shylock Come to Judgment.
Sydney Telegraph, which desires to see the
powers of the Privy Council unrestricted, and
points out that the Federal Supreme Court can-
not possibly satisfy the British money-lender,
talks thus in a leading article (May 15) regarding
compulsory arbitration in Maoriland :
It has been said that the employers were ready, in the
event of this happening (i.e., an adverse judgment given
by M.L. Supreme Court], to carry the matter to the Privy
Council, but it is extremely improbable that they will
take that course. Nobody doubts that it was the inten-
tion of Parliament when the Act was under consideration
to so frame it that the Arbitration Court should have the
power given it to favor the claims of union laborers to
employment as against non-unionists. And even if it
should be held that this has not been done with sufficient
certitude in the wording of the measure the remedy is
one which Parliament can supply in a few hours. . . .
It would, therefore, he quite useless for employers to
move the Privy Council. Before that tribunal could
speak Parliament can carry through an amendment of the
Act which would render any question of interpretation
impossible.
The British money-lender and his cherished appeal
are on precisely the same footing. If the Privy
Council’s interpretation of an Australian law is
likely to be more favorable to the British money-
lender than the Federal Supreme Court’s inter-
pretation, then—as the Telegraph points out —if
the Federal Parliament desires the Australian inter-
firetation to prevail, “the remedy is one which Par-
iament can supply in a few hours.” “It would,
therefore, be quite useless for [the British money-
lender] to move the Privy Council. Before that tri-
bunal could speak Parliament can carry through an
amendment of the Act, which would render any
question of interpretation impossible.” Precisely.
So the Telegraph is confuted out of its own
mouth. The British money-lender’s interests are
said to be bound up in the power of “ superior in-
terpretation ” possessed by the Privy Council.
But, while Australia retains the right to make its
own laws, the money-lender’s privilege is moon-
shine. For, as the Telegraph judiciously points
out, though the Privy Council may alter an Aus-
tralian interpretation of the law, Australia can go
behind the Privy Council and alter the law.
Melbourne has got her first authenticated
case of bubonic. —
The repeated allegations as to the use of
dum-dum bullets by the Boers are, perhaps, ex-
plainable by the statement of the eminent London
surgeon Treves (recently at the front) that,
although at 1500 to 2000 yards, the Mauser bullet
Eenetrates like a needle, it will, at 500, shatter a
one to fragments.
Cronwright-Schreiner, Olive Schreiner’s
husband, is among the best of living authorities
on angora goats. He breeds them extensively in
S. Africa, and knows their ways thoroughly.
Which probably accounts for the reported fact
that he was not nonplussed when Jingo hoodlums
recently bellowed and cavorted around him.
Roeburne (W.A.) town council recently
got itself into a flutter over the report that one of
its members—-ominously named Kruger —had de-
clared that he would like to fight for the Boers.
Pressed for an official explanation, K. mildly re-
marked that he had only expressed his agreement
with Herbert Spencer, John Morley, and a few
other obscure dunderheads, in the opinion that
the present South African war is an unjust one.
The “ loyalty ” of some of the councillors, however,
was too tender to stand even this ; and, as Kruger
couldn’t be kicked out, they kicked themselves
out by refusing to sit with such a seditious mis-
creant.

Messrs. W. & .T. Lempriere, Mining Machinery
Merchants, 506 Little Collins-street, Melbourne, report
that the Luhrig system of concentration is gradually es-
tablishing itself in the principal mills of Australia. The
latest Luhrig vanner has a large capacity, and owing to
important improvements the life of the belt, &c., is greatly
increased. Vanners and spare parts are stocked in Meib.
and prompt delivery is thus assured. A small testing
plant is available near Melbourne. Upon receipt of
necessary data, estimates for complete plants, including
batteries, rolls, trommels, jigs, classifiers, vanners, &c.,
will be proceeded with by competent engineers.*

Gilfillan and McCreery have removed to more
commodious offices in the Stock Exchange Buildings,
Melbourne. They have enlarged their mining laboratory
and ore-testing works and are prepared to test parcels of
ore to ascertain the most successful and economical
method of treatment.*
• * * •
The Wilfley Concentrator, which was lately in-
troduced to Australia by the Austral Otis Engineering
Co., Ltd., is meeting with much success, users describing
it as far and away the best machine of the day, and the
CO. is kept busily engaged in turning out the machines to
keep pace with the orders. The range of adjustment to
suit various classes of ores is such that the machines are
being successfully employed to concentrate on tin, copper,
silver and gold ores, and the machines are now working
in all the colonies. The Central Silver Mine, Broken Hill,
after consideration of all existing plants and careful ex-
periments, have adopted them and are putting them in.*
Cyanide, caustic soda, chloride of lime, and all
mining and assay chemicals and apparatus, can be ob-
tained of Felton, Grimwade and Co., Melbourne, who
have issued a very handsome illustrated catalogue of all
mining requisites, price Is. 3d. post free.

S. Australia’s very latest gold-prodigy is at Tar-
coola, and, as there is a wealthy M.L.C. deep in it, it is
being boomed rather more even than the fizzy province
has boomed its thousand-and-one other prodigies. Tar-
coola is 274 miles from Port Augusta, and prospecting can
only be done satisfactorily and safely by camel parties
Of course, the usual absurd assay-results of a few penny
weights of gold-specimens have been published.
Mining report: “Prince Lyell, Tas.—Tunnel in
398 ft. ; country hard, mineralised quartzite, copper
pyrites; bad shooting.” No doubt about the Prince
being a bad shooter. Never made a hit thus far. Sellers
6d. Its royal relative is down to 2d. or so.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
According to N.S. W. W orks Minister O’Sullivan,
who went up to Broken Hill to announce it, the Govt, has
made up its mind to propose a lme from the Hill to Cock-
burn (the terminus of the S. Aus. system), by way of
Pinnacles and Thackaringa. This means competition with
the Silverton Tram. Co. The latter’s Act puts purchase
almost out of the question ; for the Govt, may only take
over the line on paying compensation based on the co. s
current profits—at present nearly £lOO,OOO a year, lo
pav a million and three-quarters for 32 miles of narrow-
eauge line built over flat country is not to be thought of;
in fact, if it were paid it wouldn’t be much advantage to
Broken Hill, because the rates would have to be kept
ridiculously high in order to pay interest and provide a
sinking fund on so huge a sum. It is important to re-
member that the co. was not granted a monopoly ; it took
all the risks of competition just like an ordinary trader,
though so far it has had the phenomenal luck to keep the
field to itself. Premier Lyne, it is well known, has
scruples about disturbing the co. His idea is that it came
along when the future of the silverfleld was uncertain,
and now it ought to have consideration for accepting the
risks it did ; also, seeing that a basis for determining com-
pensation was laid down, the State ought not to dodge
that by running a line alongside the company’s. But,
apart from everything else, there are several very fair
mines at Pinnacles and Thackaringa, now handicapped by
heavy cartage—one of them alone has paid £16,350 for
freight—and the question which the Govt, had to decide
was whether these were to be left undeveloped and unas-
sisted for fear of affecting the monopoly which the Silver-
ton co. has enjoyed forl2 years.
N.S.W. figures for last year, as gathered finally
by the Mines Dept. : Silver and lead, £2,070,667 ; gold,
£1 761,815; coal and shale, £1,326,798 ; copper, £399,814 ;
opal, £135,000 ; tin, £98,428 ; coke, £77,130 ; diamonds,
£10,341 ; and the rest not worth talking about. The
year’s total was £6,167,657, which was £1,290,659 better
than the ’9B figures. Gold production—a lot of Queens-
land gold is counted in—gained most; it sprang from
340,4930 z. to 496,1960 z. Silver and lead were £366,602
better (these figures are always only rough estimates), but
much of that was due to the big rise in prices. The same
with regard to copper (from £118,927 to £399,814), and
tin (from £60,000 to £98,428).
* * » •
Cobar (N.S.W.) : The mines of this camp suffer
from some disabilities which it would be easy and profit-
able for Govt, to remove. The 20 years’ working of the
big copper-mine by reverberatory furnaces was the means
of cutting out all the wood for miles round. Conse-
quently, they have to put up with the poorest and dearest
wood in N.S.W. By a job of the dirtiest description,
when the rail was extended to Oobar it was absolutely
taken round the mineral belt instead of through it, which
is the natural and shortest route, and the terminus landed
a long way from the town instead of in it. Cartage from
railway to mines averages half-a-crown a ton. It is the
best-paying branch-line in N.S.W., but has never re-
ceived the benefit of through-rates. Lithgow coal landed
on the mines costs 225. 6d. per ton. Only one mine uses
it as fuel, but if the rate from Lithgow were reduced 4s.
or 6s. a ton they would all use coal instead of wood ; and
if the railway itself were extended to the mines it would
be one of the best-paying short lines in the province. The
mining co’s would extend it themselves if allowed
Cobar-Chesney: Some hundreds of tons or 10
and 12 per cent, concentrates have been sent away from
this mine, but it is doubtful whether any profit is shown
after railway and treatment charges. The loss, too, in
concentration is greater than anticipated, but this will, to
a large extent, be got over by additional plant for catch-
ing concentrates now running off with slimes and tailings.
It has an enormous body of 3 and 4 per cent, concentrat-
ing copper ore with a little gold, and it would be extra-
ordinary if it cannot be worked at a profit while copper
keeps its present price. There is not the slightest doubt
that it can, but to do so means a large daily production,
and that in its turn calls for a heavy expenditure.
• * » *
Great Western, Cobar. This little mine con-
tinues to “ gouge ” out a few tons of ore a week, containing
f;ood gold, but mixed with so much metallic copper that
ittle or nothing can be got from it by amalgamation,
while if the ore is sent away for treatment the whole of
its value is eaten up in railway and other charges. The
present manager—and it has had many—has made an
excellent job of straightening up the very crooked old
ram’s-horn shaft, and has got on to a shoot of good ore,
which he has no means of treating locally. As it is,
there seems no hope for the show but to make the few
remaining calls and then reconstruct.
• * * «
The Occidental, Cobar, after manyfyears’ work-
ing in a tinpot way, has got itself in shape, for which it:
has largely to thank the cyanide process. By the profits
made out of its stacked tailings, as well as those cur-
rently coming from ore crushed, it has paid a few divs.,
increased its stamps from 10 to 30 (10 more are going up,
and 50 are to be at work by Xmas), built a large cyanide
plant, and is now sinking a big main shaft so as to get at
the tens of thousands of tons of ore around the old one.
The future seems absolutely secure for many years to
come. Less than three years ago the co. was willing to
sell the mine for £20,000. An option was given, a sub-
stantial deposit paid, and the crowd who put it up in
London seemed to have forgotten all about the balance,
which only represented about a year’s work on the mine.
They missed a mining bargain, for which the Occidental:
shareholders are not sorry.
* * * ♦
Cobar Gold Mines (mainly owned in London)
is in great shape for long-continued divs. ; it is the best-
opened-up, the most economically-worked and perman-
ent-looking gold-mine in N.S.W. Its returns have got to
be guessed at, but it is easier for a man as rich as Croesus
to conceal his affluence than it is for a gold-mine to con-
ceal the fact that it is doing well. Everything around
the O.G.M. above and below ground bespeaks the fact
that it is all right. None the less, it is time that it was
compelled, with all other mines, to declare its monthly
output. That is compulsory in Vic., and why not in
N.S.W. ? It is a Crown tenant, and surely the Govt,
landlord ought to know what is being got out of his free-
hold. The last balance-sheet shows costs of breaking,
raising, crushing, and cyaniding under 12s. ft ton, and it
is easy to see that since that time a further reduction has
taken place. An enormous deal of automatic work is
done at this mine. The ore-shoots are so arranged that
the ore gravitates to them to such an extent that there
are not more than half-a-dozen shovels in the mine to
keep up an output of 1500 tons per week. When the
stopes are finished they are filled with tailings. As
altered, the 100-stamp mill is doing good work, and the
cyanide plant is one of the finest and largest in Austral-
asia. Already upwards of 20,000 tons of slimes have
accumulated awaiting the determination of the manage-
ment as to the system of handling, but whether by filter-
press or agitation, it is reasonable to assume that their
treatment will show a profit of certainly over 10s. per ton.

Milbum Creek copper mine (N.S.W.) now
owned by Melbourne people, promises to develop into a
valuable property. It was altogether too defiled a thing
for the saintly souls of Sydney to touch.
* * * *
Burraga copper-mine, N.S.W., now known as
“ The Lloyd” Copper Co., is rapidly getting into shape.
Already the production has nearly doubled since the
mine was taken over in August last. Mining costs have
been reduced, and thousands of tons of ore have been
placed in sight by development, a thing quite unknown
before. A new hauling-shaft has been brought up
through the old workings and partly through new
ground, a big concrete dam has been put in, several new
reverbatories built, two 120-ton blast-furnaces with hot
blast stoves are building, an enormous flue and a, 200 ft.
chimney-stack are going up, and every other provision is
being made for the expansion of this big and long-worked
mining concern. Yet the mine was refused not long ago,
by so-called copper and other experts, for less than half
the sum paid for it last year, when those who bought it
got a bargain. In fact, about half the money asked by
the veteran Lloyd three years ago for the mine was repre-
sented, in actual copper, in the bottoms of the old rever-
batory-furnaces at Lithgow and on the mine 1
* » * •
Yiotory, Forest Reefs (N.S.W.), about to be re-
constructed. Company has applied for Govt, aid to the
extent of £5OO, and will get it, not because M’sP. figuro
largely in the shareholders’-list, but because the co. is
deserving of it for demonstrating the asininity of Ballarat
and Oreswick mine-owners, who will use puddling-
machines instead of heavy-weight stamper-batteries for
treating liquid mud.
Of N.S.W. dredging schemes it is reported that
“ investors are gradually realising the great possibilities
of substantial profits being secured in the purchase of
these shares at the present low prices.” Tub Bulletin
ventures to prophesy that not six out of the numerous
companies which have been floated will return profits
equal to the “present low prices.” When pioneer
Garland's dredge started on the Macquarie the scribbling
wiseacres were sanguine that there would be results
which would create an unexpected N.S.W. boom. But
solemn —and most significant-silence was maintained as
to Garland’s results and then the would-be boomsters
shifted their ground to the Turon. That scheme seems
to be daily receding from golden points towards which
the public were assured it was ever approaching. It has
been a hideous failure as far as realisation of promoters’
anticipations is concerned. Nothing abashed tho boom-
sters now travel off to Araluen. It is quite probable that
some of the companies about to operate there will earn
divisible profits. Should this eventuate the long talked-
of boom will come. The ultimate results will be a few
successes and a hundred failures; much boodle for the
wise men who float dredging cos. and much weeping and
wailing and gnashing of teeth for the fools who put up
the money.
• * * *
Hillgrove (N.S.W.) in a sorry plight. Baker’s
Creek battery, for the first time, has run ahead of the
mine, and is now idle awaiting further mine develop-
ment ; Eleanora repeating its history—once more being
pig-rooted by tributors ; West Sunlight closed down ;
Cooney Co. has so far failed to locate Smith’s reef in its
property. The only gleam of sunshine seems to be at the
Sunlight, which is getting better returns.
English advices to some Broken Hill directors
are that a substantial rise in silver is to be looked for.
The talk is of as much as 6d. an oz. Apparently there is
expectation of it, too, in the U.S., for recent papers say
that abandoned Comstock mines are being got ready
again. The big lead prices have also set the Colorado
and other camps booming again. As to the silver the
explanation is that there is likely to be big buying for
minting. The world's stocks are, however, appalling.

Nothing at Broken Hill has improved more

* * • •
Odling’s magnetic process, which the B.H.
Prop, is going to try for the separation of the particles in
the sulphides, and for which it is claimed that it can get
out the rhodonite, has been developing for 11 years. Mr.
Odling’s first machine was designed to get rid of the some-
what similar and equally exasperating pyrrhotine in the
Pinnacles ore.
» m • •
The Wifley concentrator will soon be humming
all over Australasia. Over 50 already at work and under
order for Broken Hill. The machine has a distinguishing
feature. It does not attempt to draw a line between
clean concentrates and clean tailings, but allows a wide
margin, and puts that portion back on to the machine
again—a simple but sensible idea.
* * * m
G. H. Blakemore, the brainy young metallur-
gist, who for some time ran the Illawarra works and lately
read an on-the-whole capital paper to the N.S.W. Chamber
of Mines on Government Metallurgical Works, goes to N.
Queensland in charge of the Ennisleigh. Mr. Blakemore
has for some years been veteran John Howell’s under-
study. His opinion of Govt, metallurgical works is that
they ought to be purely experimental, and that the staff
ought to earn its salary by solving the heaps of problems
that are lying round on tailings-dumps, and in abandoned
mines.
* * * *
Departed from Sydney last week, “ unwept,
■unhonored, and unsung,” Deep-levels Bouchier. All
mining folk agree that a more gentlemanly, straightfor-
ward man than Mr. Bouchier never appeared on the Aus-
tralian scene. Pity ’tis that his luck was not equal to his
pluck. Had it been so, on his departure, he would have
had to submit to the sickening slobber which attends
success.

* • • •
The Critio’s (Adelaide) London correspondent
■describes big Henry Bratnober as the man who condemned
the goldfields of Westralia, and thereby lost the Roths-
childs the chance of acquiring some of the big Kalgoorlie
properties—adding that, while he was wrong in his
estimate, so were other great mining authorities, such as
Dr. Schmeisser. It is the newspaper-man, and not the
Bismarckian mining-engineer, who is wrong. The mines
that Bratnober condemned in Westralia have, almost
without exception, since condemned themselves. Because
he happened to go to Hannan’s, then mainly a dry-
blowers’ camp, it is declared that he condemned the great
mines of Kalgoorlie. The mines he saw at Hannan’s were
those that are no better to-day than they were then. The
Great Boulder and others around it were prospecting
quartz veins, mostly valueless, and knew absolutely
nothing of the existence of the rich formations they are
now working. Bratnober, like the writer, may have
passed over them on his way somewhere else, but he did
nothing more, for there was then nothing more to do. Of
the few mines that impressed him as having any chance of
permanency was Menzies’ find, now known as the Lady
Shenton, for which he made a good offer when it consisted
of a few shallow holes sunk here and there on the reef,
and the history of that surface-show has only served to
prove the correctness of his judgment.
* • * •
The Associated G.M., Kalgoorlie, recently
started to give its monthly costs with its monthly pro-
duction, and on each occasion the former exceeded the
latter —on the last, to such an enormous extent that the
Kalgoorlie newspaper mining-hummer declared that the
costs have been exaggerated. Well, as a rule, it is all the
other way. What possible motive can there be for
exaggerating costs unless it is to “bear” shares? It
would not be surprising to hear an accusation of this kind
made against some persons prominently connected with
the mine, which, despite the heavy fall, still has a market-
value of, say, £1,750,000, although it is officially declared
that its last month’s working was done at a loss of
over £lO,OOO. The London experts who lately ex-
amined the mine say that comparatively little pay-
ore is visible, and that the gold-shoots shrink to
littleness at the lower levels. Not a word said
anywhere in favor of the mine, which stands at this value
in the face of these statements and a continued monthly
loss. What is the of this thusness ? Shares are
oversold to the extent of hundreds of thousands, and
scrip cannot fall to its intrinsic value while so oversold
and while hundreds of people knowing the true condition
of things continue to shoot at them. This is the, position
as clear as noon-day sun, known to hundreds of people on
the field and elsewhere and yet nobody points it out.
• * • *
Thanks to cyaniding, some Kalgoorlie mines’
results show improvement in the per-ton yield. Great
Boulder, for instance, averaged, in ’9B, 2oz. ldwt.
3gr. ; and in ’99, loz. 12dwt. 7gr. ; but in April the return
was up to 2oz. 3dwt. 3gr. Still many of these yields are
stated unfairly. The Boulder’s figures for last month are
given in most lists —“ stone treated, 3448 tons ; g°> c L
74350 z. ; averages, 2oz. 3dwt. 3gr.,” while as a matter of
fact 37890 z. of this total was won from tailings accumu-
lated any time these past five years. It would be fair
enough to include the cyanide results from the tailings of
an equal quantity of stone ; but in this case nearly two
tons of tailings went through for every ton of stone.

Kanowna, W.A., suffering from the re-action
of the deep lead rush, still has hopes. White Feather
Main Reef has paid regular divs. the last few months,
with more in sight. April average, lOdwts. Much is ex-
pected locally from the Kanowna Consolidated, an Eng-
lish co. floated last year to work certain lsases at the head
of the famous Q.E.I). lead. This co. has three reefs, now
being opened up to 300 ft. ready for stoping—-average
value reported as 16dwts. To show their bona-fides it is
told that manager cabled ’Ome one day for winding plant,
and received return cable next day saying plant had been
bought and shipped. Which ought to be a record.
Adjoining lease —the Ballarat —worked by tributors for
some time past for a return between 2oz. and 3oz. per
ton, is now in English hands. And the Robinson still
another English co.—which has existed on public crush-
ing for many months, has started to mill its own ores
again, expecting a lOdwts. return.

The Bulletin wishes to know who are to be
prosecuted in connection with the Associated fiasco: the
directors who, but a few months ago, stated, in an official
document, that there were 750,000 tons of payable ore m
8i <r ht, or the experts, who now state that the available
quantity of 32dwt. ore—unpayable—is only 33,000 tons?
Recent disclosures in connection with some
W.A. mines surely justify the assumption that in mining
parlance “blunder” and “plunder” are synonymous
terms.
* * * *
In Westralia they still float companies one day
and make calls the next. Scrip in the La Tosca Co. was
issued April 12. Before the end of that month the direc-
tors met the shareholders, “ shouted ” for all hands, and
made a 3d. call. And that co. probably has a “ capital ”
of £50,000, at the very least.
• * • •
The Windsor Consolidated —Zeb. Lane s first
big “ float ” this trip—was lately “ offered to the public ”
in London. This is what “ offering to the public ” meant
in this case Capital, £150,009, in 150,000 shares. Ven-
dors take 100,000 shares. Vendors also subscribe for
10,000 shares. Also, vendors underwrite 30,000 shares.
Likewise, vendors have the option to take the other
10,000 shares inside 12 months. Total, 150,000.

One thing “ sticks out a mile”: The English
companies are about to try and reduce miners’ wages in
Westralia. “ Wages are a great deal too high,” says the
Associated expert’s report, “ to ensure the mines paying 1
the hours of labor are too short, and too many hands are
employed.” It will mean a lot of trouble.

* * * *
What trustful fools Australians are to invest,
under present conditions, in mines absolutely controlled
and faked by a lot of shady London stockjobbers 1 What
on earth does anybody here, not right in the ring, know
about the real condition of any of the big Westralian
mines, for instance? Sooner or later the Australian
public will see that every mine leased by the State to a
private person is regularly inspected and publicly
reported on by a trustworthy and well-paid Govt, official,
and in the case of very important mines the reports will
come at short intervals. Why the Australian public
should allow packs of foreign sharpers to get possession of
our (the State’s) mines and use said mines as instruments
of confidence-swindle and daylight robbery is indeed a
mystery. Why does the law discountenance the three-
card trick and “ under-and-over ” ?

It appears to be a solemn fact that Major (or is
it General) Collins, erstwhile of Broken Hill and other
places, who now runs the Lake View Consols, gets
£lO,OOO a year salary. He showed his agreement in
Svdney the other day, an imposing document weighing
about 21bs, and fairly bristling with signatures and seals.
Queensland Mining Co. stock had a brief boom
on Melbourne ’Change last week all along of a forged
telegram purporting to come from Elder, Smith & Co.,
Adelaide. The wire sent late on Saturday afternoon (sth)
said : “ Anglo-Continental Syndicate, London, appointed
us their agents, resume negotiations purchase Queens-
land Mining Investment props, same terms as before,
allowing equal rights new issue; they guarantee com-
pletion immediately. Writing Monday.” Shares har-
dened on Monday, and next morning they soared up-
wards to 6s. and 7s. —not much of a soar considering they
had touched 30s. at the wayback time when the Anglo-
Continental G.M. Syndicate came nibbling round the
bait. A mysterious circulation of “ confidential ” memos.
re the alleged London offer seems to have done more
harm than good for the schemers. It isn’t usual for
people in the know to diffuse their knowledge around the
room on bits of paper ; consequently, not. more than 3000
shares changed hands at the advanced prices. Meanwhile
theMelb. manager had wired to Elder, Smith & Co., and the
cat was fairly out of the bag on Wednesday. At time of
writing, sundry detectives were under orders to find the
unknown author of the cronk wire; likewise the philan-
thropic person who put those bits of paper around.
Somebody seems to have been very anxious to quit his
interest in the Q.M. Co.

A dispute between'the men of the newly-
formed squadron of Sydney Lancers and the
powers that be is exciting some interest in ama-
teur military circles. The troop had decided on
one of their members as a certainty for a lieuten-
ancy in the regiment, but he has been passed over,
and the lieutenancies given to Messrs. Lyne and
See, sons of the Premier and Chief-Secretary re-
spectively, the third going to one of the Darleys.
How; exactly is history repeating itself in all the
provinces ! The men much resent this, as they
object to be governed by officers appointed by
mere influence. As a protest, when they were
asked to form a camp in connection with the
Queen’s Birthday manoeuvres only three of the
squadron volunteered. An effort is being made to
conciliate them, but at present the men are firm,
and the camp will have to be held without them.
* * * *
A so far unreported incident in connection
with the Victorian Bushmen. While camped at
Mentone, a friend of one of the men was on his
way to the camp when he met four privates and
an officer—all full of patriotism and liquor. The
officer said, “Here comes a d Boer. Have a
shot at him ! ” and promptly followed his own
advice by firing a revolver at the visitor. Fortun-
ately he was in no condition to see straight, and
THE BACKBLOCKS FAMILY IN TOWN.
“ 'Ere, mother, ’arf time! What about the twins?”
the bullet struck the footpath ; but, glancing off,
it struck the victim in the stomach, inflicting a
nasty wound. He was taken to hospital, and has
just come out still feeling the effects of the shot.
But how did it happen that the officer was sup-
plied with ball cartridge ?
• * * *
\Vhen late Yic. Gov. Brassey was leaving
Australia, the Queenscliffe forts (Port Phillip) had
orders to fire a 17-gun salute when Brassey’s
yacht, the Sunbeam, should be passing, but it un-
fortunately happened that, about the expected
time, a collier put in her appearance, and duly
received the salute. Then, when, half-an-hour
later, the Sunbeam did come along, she got no
recognition, because the officer in charge was out
of barracks, and there was no one to authorise
more powder-burning. Again, when the Bush-
men’s Contingent was leaving, great preparations
for a send-off were made at the same forts in the
way of colored-lamps, limelights, bonfires, and so
on, which, again, were all wasted upon the wrong
vessel. And, yet more recently, when the Aus-
tralian Imperial Regiment was leaving on board
the Victoria, the little demonstration from the
forts was brought off just 14 hours too early. The
troopship left about 10 o’clock on the Wednesday,
and the display took place about eight the night
before. Now if, in times of peace, the eyes of Port
Phillip’s fortress cannot tell the spick-and-span
Sunbeam from a grimy collier, what may be ex-
pected in time of war, or what chance will there
be of damage to an enemy, instead of to friendly
passers-by ?

Ulhce declines to accept an invitation to be pre-

(lead from infectious diseases. Moreover, because

* * • *
At Goulbum Anglican Synod the other
day Rev. P. (J. Smith denounced the title
rector, describing it as “an empty, vain,
ambitious sound, with no sense or meaning in
Australia.” But why did he again and again call
the Bishop sitting before him “My Lord ” ? If
rector’’is vain as applied to a Minister in Aus-
tralia, Lord” is much vainer, for the former’
only connotes clerical rank while the latter impu-
dently annexes a lay dignity. If names or nick-
names are worth arguing about a line of consist-
ency should be drawn.

Text for a Maupassant. “W.F. H.” to
The Bulletin
I was idling about in the railway-yards when a man,
employed in shunting, fell under the wheels of a moving
truck, and had his right leg and hip hideously, hopelessly
crushed. He was picked up and laid on some coats spread
on the platform, and a doctor soon came. The poor
shunter was suffering intensely, but, after the surgeon
had examined him, he coolly said, “ Doctor, how bad is
it ? “ Very bad indeed.” “ Shall I live ? ” “ Not more
than, perhaps, 10 minutes. You are bleeding to death.”
1 What time is it, Jim ?” asked the man of a fellow-em-
ploye. “11.55” was the answer. “The children will
bring my dinner at 12 sharp. Some of you go and stop
them. They mustn’t see me die. Poor, motherless
children orphans now 1 ” I went to the door, with
others, and we were just in time to stop a b.oy of eight
and a girl of six from coming in. Each had hold of a
basket, containing father’s dinner, and they were smiling
in anticipation of the greeting they would receive. We
sent them away with a story about their father having
gone home, and the eyes of both were filled with tears of
disappointment. The echo of their footsteps could still
be heard on the pavement when the father breathed his
last.
• • * *
There is one sleepy town in Victoria, and
one of the oldest in the province, which deserves
to be infamous for the startling num-
ber of idiots, miscellaneous cripples,
and incurables afflicted with malignant
hereditary evils it has produced. The
town is the abiding place of many
wealthy families, and it is shocking to
note how frequently physical and
mental diseases of a transmissible
kind appear in these families. A large
percentage of thorn produce idiots,
and an impartial census disclosing the
town’s unenviable productiveness in
this respect, and in the matter of
scrofula cases and human malform-
ations, would shock Australia. There
is a good deal of intermarriage
amongst the “old families” of the
town and district, and the “best
set” prides itself on being quite the
“ naicest ” in Victoria.

Not generally known that the
bronze figure of Mercury over the Age
office was originally imported for the
Commercial Bank of Australia.
When the ’93 collapse arrived the
Commercial’s new whited sepulchre
in Collins-st. was not out of the hands
of the contractor. In the light of the
’93 exposures, the erection of the
statue over the bank would have been too cruel;
so Mercury la,y in the bank vaults until David
Syme bought it at a verra low figure, ye ken.
• ♦ * *
An interesting prison item :
Continuous agitation has secured the release from
Pentridge of the notorious negro, “ King,” in a manner
carefully hushed up by the authorities so that attention
might not be directed to the case. King originally got a
sentence of 12 months, but this was pronounced nearly
nine (9) years ago, and he has been in Pentridge ever
since—he says, through the ill-treatment of the warders ;
but they say, through his mad fits of violence. From
time to time King’s case has been taken up by Marshall
Lyle and others, who held that enquiry should be made,
but the Govt, refused to move, alleging that the convict
was a criminal lunatic of the worst type, and that his
liberty would be a standing menace to human life. ThiJ
is the man they released the other day, long before the
completion of his accumulated sentences, and whose dis-
charge to freedom was accomplished in so secret a man-
ner that it was only owing to a casual row between King
and another darkey over, a white woman (of course), that
the fact was made public. King was not discharged in
the ordinary way, through the police. He was made the
subject of a special order of the Governor-in-Council, a
process usually reserved for letting loose relatives of poli-
ticians, persons of influence, and criminals of a Naice
type. As an obscure and inferior nigger pugilist, King
fits none of these types, and there is consequently some
other reason for the extreme secrecy observed in his case.
The inference left is that his release prevents what might
prove a very awkward enquiry, for it was bound to come
in time, and enables the Crown to turn round next time
the case is mentioned and cry, “ Why, we released the
man long ago—behold ! what a merciful Government we
are.”

Victoria’s new .County Court Judge
Johnston bids fair to emulate N.S.W.’s three-
years-for-ninepenco Gibson. The other day he
gave a previously-convicted man two years “hard’
for stealing a bottle of beer. Also he belioves in
the savage “ solitary system,” even to the extent
of giving a prisoner nine days’ starvation in three
months, notwithstanding it had been put forward
that accused suffered from an injury to the head
and was at times irresponsible. The career of
Judge Johnston should be worth watching.
* » * *
The Collingwood (Melb.) ,T.P. strives to
emulate his brother beak of Carlton in his gentle
treatment of the larrikin. Recently two members
of the gang known as the “ Fitzroy murderers,”
apparently out of pure devilment, severely bat-
tered an unoffending citizen at his own door, and
the Bench, after fining the delinquents £lO and
£5 respectively, mulcted complainant’s son, who
had apparently been defending his father from a
cowardly and unprovoked attack, in the sum of
£2 for allegedly assaulting his assailants. It really
looked as if this were by way of apology to “the
push.”

The scare has its uses. On the day when
the Age burst forth with “ The Plague in
Melbourne ” (large type) the municipal price for
dead rats rose 50 per cent—from 2d. to 3d. And
the rat-catchers as a rule are deserving citizens
who wanted that extra penny very badly.

There was quite a curious set of coin-
cidences in the cases of two young women
who recently suicided in Hobson’s Bay (Vic.)
within a couple of days of each other.
Both bodies were found by small boys.
Both women appeared about 25 vears of age, and,
besides being so similar in features as to pass for
sisters, were similarly dressed. Friday’s victim
had a gold ring and an artificial tooth in her pocket,
wlnle Monday’s had a gold ring and a set of teeth
in her right hand.
* * * *
The case of that plague-stricken Sydney
Dago fruiterer, whose place the police had to
break into early in the morning, calls for much
consideration, because up to 8.30 the previous
evening the public had been buying its penn’orths
ot milk at the shop. An over-ripe banana or a
specked apple is the bait many of these men base
their milk-trade upon. While genuine dairymen
must have cemented dairies, away from all dwel-
ling houses, the diligent Dago is allowed to keep
his milk-supply in the general bedroom if he likes.
And he often does like.

• * * •
Chief Justice Madden has been enjoying
another waxellent time at Melb. Divorce Court.
In the course of a day’s work that painfullv-moral
lessoner summed up three respondents in his best
abusive language. The men—two of them —were
“a dissipated and selfish scoundrel” and “a
thoroughly unredeemed scoundrel” respectively,
whilst the one female who hurt his Honor’s feel-
ings was “a wilful and indifferent wife, and a
mercenary and poor-spirited woman.” Judge
Madden’s genius for discovering all the rights and
wrongs of a divorce story in half-an-hour is likely
to bring trouble on him one day. He rushes at
an opinion like a bull at a gate. Divorce-
cases, as a rule, turn only one side to the
light, but His Honor, being a wordy gentle-
man with no imagination worth mentioning,
ignores the possibilities of things that don’t come
under his observation. Sooner or later some tricky
matron will bring Chief-Justice M.’s penchant
into awful disrepute, if he doesn’t watch himself.
Virtuous indignation is a game that two can play
at. If ever the dandy positivist should happen to
make a mistake in his “scoundrel,” and the
libelled party should happen to be very muscular
and very confident of his ability to prove his
innocence after he has taken the law into his own
hands—well, perhaps it will go hard with
Madden’s shiny boll-topper in the first place.
* * * *
A high-toned old Maorilander, who held
among other offices that of district registrar,
used to relate with much gusto how his dau-
ghter got the better of him. She had the
bad taste to fall in love with an employe at
a large meat-works —the old man used to
call him a “ butcher,” but he was not quite
that. Such a union was totally against all
the family traditions, and the father re-
fused his consent. Tears and the other
resources of love-stricken daughters to
move stony-hearted parents were resorted
to in vain. She might marry almost
anyone she pleased except “ The Butcher.”
For some months nothing was heard of the
matter, and peace, apparently, reigned
in the household ; but one day the couple
appeared at the registrar’s office, and
calmly stated that they desired to be mar-
ried ! The girl had, in the interval, be-
come of age, and the registrar (her father)
knew that his consent was unnecessary, so,
calmly sitting down at his table, he asked
the customary questions as to ages, birth-
places, &c., lending no assistance and offer-
ing no remark as he heard his own and his
wife’s names mentioned as parents of the
bride. After the papers were all filled in, and
he had without tremor proclaimed them man
and wife, the climax came. Human nature
could stand out no longer, and, dashing
down his book, he kissed the bride ana
blessed her. 4

“ Selborne ” to Melb. Argus :
During the last eight years 400 solicitors have been
admitted to practice in Victoria. Whilst the number of
solicitors has nearly doubled during that short period
there are to-day scarcely half as many men practising as
barristers only as there were in 1892. Moreover, the
amount of work available is far less than before.
Probably the Vic. Bushman corps of the future
will be composed almost entirely of clientless
attorneys. There was a leaven of learned unem-
ployed in the last Contingent. Dodging around
kopjes for 1335. per week (that’s what a lieutenant
gets), or even 355. per week, is “adoosid sight
easier profession than the Law,” as Major Pen-
dennis would have .said nowadays.

Compulsory slum-inspection at Christ-
church (M.L.) has revealed strange things. A
woman, over GO, was found bedridden with rheu-

The old husband tottered round collecting bottles
for a living. The rent was 3s. a week, and the
landlord played-up if it got behind.

* * * •
Judging by the recent Melbourne Chinese
procession there are more half-caste girls in Mel-
bourne than is supposed. Numbers were carried
in the procession, while the windows of Lilly
Buck - street swarmed writh them and their
brothers. Only here and there was a full-
blooded Chinawoman to be seen. Many of
the girls in the procession lacked any ap-
parent Mongolian strain, having blue or
grey eyes and brown hair.

rule of N.S.W. Education Dept, which
condemns metropolitan teachers, to spend

of 15s. a week; alternatives, leave the

* * • •
In the four principal M.L. cities there is
considerable variation in the market rate of rats.
In Wellington the price per head, f.o.b. the
destructor, is 3d. ; in Auckland, 2d. ; in Christ-
ohurch Id., and in Dunedin 4d. Notwith-
standing the high rate ruling in the Empire city,
supplies do not come in at all freely, the latest
market report indicating a total of about 1000 rats
offered for sale. The supplies come principally
from school-boys, who, deliver the goods chiefly
during lunch hour, carrying them to school in the
morning in their satchels, along with their books
and their sandwiches.

A police-item:—
“Hoodoo” : Was recently informed by a young woman,
who had been long employed in a big Sydney boarding-
house much affected by policemen, that the average
“ copper’s ” manly chest consists mostly of bath-towel !
the Dig, thick, Turkish kind. She had noticed the super-
fluity of these on the premises, and had watched until
she had discovered that Robert was in the habit of placing
a folded-up towel in the chest of his jumper or tunic.
These towels are springy, yet firm, and .make an ideal
padding. This explains why the bobby in a straw hat
who saunters into the pub in advance of the sergeant de-
ceives Bung. Such a slouch —reduced in proportions by
a big bath-towel or two —doesn’t at all suggest the well-
set-up copper. In fact, for height, weight and shape, the
Sydney shop-noblemen beat the police bad. Take a run
round" the rag-shops and see 1
But The Bulletin doesn’t believe a word of it.
The medical test for entering the police is most
exacting, and the average N.3.W. bobby is quite
as good a man as he looks.

To a Sydney decorator’s shop, lately, a
clerical big-wig sent a plaster statue of a saint
{intended for presentation to an up-country
chapel) to be painted and ornamented ; and about
the same time there arrived a bookmaker’s bag to
have a name written on it. Next day the cleric
walked into the workshop unannounced and nearly
had a fit when he saw, hung round the effigy’s neck
(by one of the hands, in the principal’s absence) a
leather handbag, inscribed —
IKEY MOSES, CASH FIELDER.
REGISTERED BY CITY TATTERS ALL’S CLUB.
'That shop will get no more church-work, and the
wag who sacrileged is looking for another job.
• * • •
Though bubonic-fever has so far attacked
hardly any “ well-placed ” persons in Sydney
there is no end of thinly-concealed funk among
the well-fed and well-washed. In one big business
house it used to be the custom of the senior
partner to inspect the premises from basement to
topmost storey once a week, while the junior was
continually pottering round to watch the hands at
work. But since the advent of the plague, the
furthest that the senior will trust himself is to the
edge of the lift-well, and he gets away from that as
quickly as possible, while the junior wont even
budge outside his office door.

N.S.W. State-school teachers have a diffi-
culty to contend with from which their brethren
in the other provinces are free —viz., the collec-
tion of the weekly “thrum.” An important
point in the estimate of a teacher’s efficiency is
his capacity for collecting and “ returning ” fees.
On the form for reporting defaulters are the
worc is—“ A teacher who steadily insists on
weekly payments ought to give the Department
little or no trouble in regard to debts for school
fees.” No book for fees-account is supplied by
the Dept.; and, as said account is therefore kept
in an unofficial record, the dry details are often
enlivened with amusing obiter dicta. On the front
page of “Fee Book” in the Infant Dept, of a
large N.S.W. public-school is inscribed
If there were two pen’orth of public
spirit in Melbourne, subscribers to the Queen Vic-
toria Diamond Jubilee Infectious Diseases Hospital
would be howling by this time for an account of
the committee’s stewardship. Two of the 12
citizens who were long ago appointed to safeguard
the cash and turn it to hospital purposes, have
died and left no sign. The remaining 10 might
as well be dead for all proof they can show of
their fitness to carry out the work entrusted to
them. Thus far they have achieved nothing ex-
cept the loss of a few hundred pounds in a legal
contest with a firm of Melbourne architects. The
architects sued the committee, alive and dead, for
damages on account of a breach of contract.
When the evidence had begun to reveal job-
bery a sudden silence fell upon the proceed-
ings, and the plaintiffs, it is understood, got
£3OO by way of compromise, each side paying its
■own costs. Do the committeemen intend to pay
this £3OO and exes, out of their own pockets, or is
the loss to be a charge against the belated Hos-
pital ? ___

A TALE OF TWO STUDENTS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ To an Assistant, holding a Second-Olass Certificate, in
a School of the Third, Fourth, or Fifth Class. Male £136
per year, Female £IO4.—N.S.W. Public Service Act and
Regulations.”
Miss Katie Magee
From her infancie
A public-school teacher decided to he.
So she pored over books
(And spoilt her good looks,
And before she was twenty had wrinkles in plenty).
But she carried away
All the honors, they say,
And came out of training a brilliant 2A.
Her fellow P.T.,
Jack Connors, said he
Was dashed if he’d study like Kitty Magee.
So, while Miss Magee worked,
Jack idled and shirked,
And while she paled with study he grew fat and ruddy.
But by spasmodic crams
Before his exams.,
It fell out that he, too, contrived to squeeze through—
With a paltry 28.
And now this is the question that flabbergasts me :
Why, for the same work,
(That he still tries to shirk,)
He is now getting clear thirty-two pounds a year
More than Kitty Magee ?
(Ah, the ways of “ the Service ” are too deep for me !)
Eh ?—Get out! it can’t be !
Well, by George, were I she,
I’d don them at once if they’d add to my riches !
And, by gad, I am glad
I was born a lad,
Since I see ’tis not “brains” that is paid for but
“ breeches ”!
GLENGARRIFF.
“No Irish. Need Apply!”
“ An Irishman ” to The Bulletin :
Editor Bulletin, —Did you read of the heroic deeds of
Irish soldiers serving for England in the-Transvaal ? Did
you read of England’s reward—Queen Victoria’s visit to
Dublin ? her toleration of the Irish soldier’s shamrock on
St. Patrick’s day? Lord Salisbury’s avowal at the Prim-
rose League banquet that the lesson of Home Rule in the
Transvaal was that for Home Rule, in the cant phrase of
English prejudice, “no Irish need apply”? Did you
mark the consistency with England’s treatment of Ireland
all down the centuries—for her patriots, irons; for her
services to England, irony ?
* * * *
Proud England ! heiress of empire, nursing her massed
SHE HAD PROVED HIM.
MAIDEN LADY (in tram, to young lady on her
right): “ Excuse me—but are you going far?"
Y. L. : “No, not far ; but why?"
M.L. (in a whisper): “Well, I don’t like you
being left alone with that horrid-looking man.’
Y. L.: “ Billy, we better git out —the ole girl’s
a-frightened of yer ”
wealth of factory and njart, counting her ships on every
sea, flaunting her flag in every port: sad Ireland 1 outcast
of nations, her harp broken and her garb a widow’s weeds,
a desolate figure facing the wild Atlantic, and stretching
anguished arms across the water to her expatriated sons :
what a contrast 1 England, a queen’s throne ; Ireland,
her footstool: England, “ the home of freedom” 1 Ireland,
the refuge of tyranny—her enterprise thwarted, her
industry destroyed, her religion contemned and punished
by her potent neighbor : what a contrast! England, con-
queror by the sword ; Ireland, the conquered—the ruin of
her liberties consummated by bribery : arrogance and
dependence, prosperity and stagnation, the pride of
success and the bitterness of despair—zenith and nadir pf
national fortunes side by side : what a contrast!
* * • *
And the pikes of Ireland are now the defences of Eng-
land ; Irish regiments are sent in the van of battle, under
the British flag, to attack the burghers of the Transvaal;
Irish blood is recklessly shed to extend the British em-
pire in Africa! What mocking smiles curl the lips of the
World-Spirit, watching! That the freedom of those
little Dutch republics should be overthrown for Britain
by the hired soldiers of Ireland ! Ireland ! still bleed-
ing from the English wound ; with the collar and chains
of the English captor still round her neck and limbs—
surely this is the utmost depth of satire which the
Human Comedy can offer.
* » •
But Iceland gets her reward—to the last doit and
tittle of her earning. Queen Victoria deigns for the
second time in her long reign to consecrate Irish turf
with her footstep. Glorious privilege! benefaction queenly
indeed ! More—and the splendor of the bopn may well
dazzle Irish eyes—she, Queen of Great Britain and Ire-
land, Empress of India, Defender of the Faith, whose hand
holds the orb, sign of her power over this world, beneath
the cross, token of her prerogative in the next—she, the
Illustrious, the Exalted, is serenely pleased, in the
ninth decade of her life, to acknowledge the Irish valor
shown in her service by permitting the Irish soldier to don
the national shamrock on his national holiday ! Queen
Victoria tolerates the shamrock. Honor unparalleled!
favor infinite 1 Yet she grants it freely and graciously,.as
becomes her noble lineage—she, the Fountain of honor
and favor—as if her mere permission to die for Her Flag,
Her Empire, Herself, were not hopor and favor far in ex-
cess of any Irish desert!
» • • *
Blessed power of princes and rulers, to confer All in the
guise of Nothing, and to pay with a simple sentiment for
blood which would otherwise be priceless ! For mark the
admirable proportion of bounty to merit! Removal of
Irish religious disabilities—protection of Irish trade—
Home Rule for Ireland—such gifts, from England, would
be indeed a base material return for Irish lives lost ip
England’s service. The exchange would smack of the huck-
ster shopkeeper, pawnbroker. But that, when Irish
soldiers are slain, Queen Victoria should visit Dublin—
how royally adequate 1 That, when her arms triumph by
the courage nourished on Irish soil, Queen Victoria should
permit the shamrock to exist—how historically suitable !
Never, henceforth, can an Irish emigrant draw his last
breath of Irish air without remembering that it is an at-
mosphere which has been twice breathed by Queen Vic-
toria, and taking the memory in full quittance for his
exile. Never, henceforth, can Famine dig an Irish grave
w-ithout the mourners’ grief being calmed by the thought
that the shamrock in the fresh-turned sod has received
Queen Victoria’s notice and approval. Yes ; the Irish
assailants of freedom in the Transvaal, on behalf of Eng-
land, have won for their country a reward fully befitting
their deed.
* • * • *
I f And, as if Queen Victoria’s royal largesse were not a
far more than sufficient equivalent for Irish blood, comes
Lord Salisbury, the haughty head of the House of Cecil
and the English Tories, Grand Master of the Primrose
League, to add his meed of recognition of the Irish ser-
vice to England. The lesson of the Transvaal, he
told the assembled Primrose Knights and Dames,
should be learnt by heart for Ireland’s sake. Let
them see the result of giving Home Rule to the
Transvaal, and beware of restoring Irish liberties—let
them beware ! Round about the Modder and the Tugela
river, in many a hasty grave—all along the road of English
conquest in Africa—lie the bones of Irishmen who fell
with the foremost in the endeavor to rivet the English
bonds upon a little nation of Dutch freemen. And this
is their surpassing reward England’s Prime Minister
declares that the war which cost their lives should teach
England to rivet her bonds more firmly upon Ireland!
What news for Emmet and Curran, for Grattan and Davis
and Mitciiel, meeting their African dead in the Shades !
The Boers have mastered the art of brew-
ing their national temperance drink, and they
show no toleration for “swipes.” One of the
officers on duty at Capetown railway-station when
Cronje arrived wrote to Tay Pay O’Connor, in
London : “ Cronje was asked if he would like any
refreshment, whereupon he promptly demanded
champagne. He got coffee and bread-and-butter,
as provided by Logan’s refreshment-contract.
They have repeated to Logan his opinion of the
coffee. Logan is more loyal than ever ! ”

More desperate sieges await Baden-Powell.
Five-hundred pretty maidens are blushing in am-
bush at Capetown to capture and marry him,
whilst several thousands will summon the dashing
Colonel to surrender on his arrival in London.
And every member of these lovely brigades will be
wearing the P.D. corset of a blameless figure.
Will the long-time hungry warrior and ever-faith-
ful admirer of the female form in its P.D. setting,
be able to hold out against such odds.**
In Adelaide, ex-politician J. C. F. John-
son sent an ancient moke named Bugler round the
province to be auctioned in aid of the Bushmen’s
Contingent Fund, and £2500 was realised by the
scheme. But now, when some respectable trades-
man, wanting a horse, bids £l2 at the real auction
and gets the animal, there are letters in the papers
appealing for money to buy him back and present
him. (alive] to the Zoo; and the “great demo-
cratic ” daily of the place appeals for subscriptions
to keep him in “ dainties ” for the rest of his life.
And this, ye gods! is gravely referred to as “a
patriotic movement.”
