A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, May 15, 1900.
My Deak Myee,—
The one feature that characterises everything
that gathers people together now-a-days is posi-
tively, preponderance of Woman. Petticoat and
froufrou on the stairs. Shoulders, scrags, undula-
tions of heavy white flesh in the theatres. Billows
of lace; billows of chiffon, invented by Worth—
in Heaven—for the resurrection and life of every
dead gown, or worn-out opera-mantle. And here
and there, rising out of the sea as it were, one
little black rock with a big white speck on it—a
man, in a black suit, with a white shirt front.
How the billows beat! Chiffon and lace, how they
surge round him and over him! Verily, the
theatre party that can scrape up three men for
twelve women keeps the eye of the whole hungry
houseful of feminines fixed, in a furtive painful
stare that really threatens to turn the head of our
Johnnies whose collars won’t interfere with the
operation.
Naturally, nobody has dared to give a ball yet.
There is one on the tapis though, and, naturally,
men are getting that one up. The University,
plucking up, and bracing itself behind the assump-
tion that the cropped and spectacled girl-grads,
might easily be mistaken for men, have fixed up
their annual ball. It comes off in Paddington
Town Hall on 28th June, under top-loftiest pat-
ronage. No imagination is needed to predict that
this will be about the most brilliant hop of an
utterly unhopping year. Ball-giving is one thing
the Alma Mater does teach its infants to carry out
successfully.
Speaking of men, a new one, of superlative
breed and looks, has arrived in Sydney. His
name is Hon. Charles Harris, of Malmesbury
blood, and he has come along with the Ladies
Mary Lygon and Susan Gilmour on a long visit to
our Govt. House. In fact, he is going to play
assistant-secretary to Wilfrid Smith, and help him
send out—well, I hardly know what.
When a Gov. goes away into the backblocks he
collects experiences. Also, the backblocks collect
experiences. A case, in which the Hush got the
funniest collection, was so graphically described
r? T 1? ,^ e other day that I must pass it on.
Couldn t I see the picture ? It was Lord—but
that is telling. Anyway, he and his aide went to
dinner on a way back station. Domestic arrange-
ments at this station had their limitations just
then, and the squatter and his wife decided that
they wouldn’t make any “ fuss.” They never did
have waiting-at-table. They wouldn’t have wait-
mg-at-table now. His Ex. would see them as
they were ; with a little extra polish on, of course.
So they sat down to dinner, six of them. The
Gov. and his aide, the squatter and his wife, the
squatter’s two daughters. And they had no wait-
mg-at-table. But “passing the plates” was a
terrible trap to the Gov. and that aide. In they
tumbled, headlong. Sometimes they passed their
own plates on round the table, sometimes they
kept other At last they thought they
had grasped the situation, and started passing up
“on their own.” They passed up their plates for
butter. Thej passed them up for jam. They
passed them up for fruit. They passed them up
for cheese. “ Will you have a little pepper with
your melon?” Up went his Ex.’s plate. On
the slightest provocation, they passed their
plates, and re-passed their plates. The dinner
resolved itself into a wild jumping, and jerking
and hurrying and scurrying of plates, backwards
and forwards, round and round the table. The
Gov. and his aide aged visibly, in their anxiety to
do the right and bushy thing. The squatter’s
daughters crammed bread down their throats
in place of handkerchiefs. The squatter’s wife
kept driving out an invisible dog. The squatter
kept hurting his bad toe against the leg of the
table. What the plates thought of it never trans-
pired. But His Ex. was seen to wipe his brow
ere he lit his cigar that evening. And, to alter
the words of a song (and remove suspicion from an
entirely innocent quarter), they’re both in England
now.
Apropos of table manners, a drab-faced travel-
ling youth dropped in for dinner at a station
lately and made himself at home by telling his
hostess how “ This ’ere ’orse-radish ain’t fit for no
blanky ’orse to eat.”
Such absurd affability rather reminds me of the
parson who comes to dinner on Sunday, unin-
vited. When the covers are lifted he sees that
the beef smokes and a grey steam arises from pota-
toes and peas. He rises, too. His steam gets up
with him. In a genial, visiting tone that comes
quite natural to parsons he remarks how “The
curse of God rests on that beef, and on those peas
and those potatoes.” By way of a little extra
merry conversation, he tells the lady of the house,
“ I am scawndalised to see your hoosehold cooks
on the Sawbath.” And then, overcome with his
own delightful manner, he accuses his host of
failing to bring his family up to God. Which, if
he means cold roast beef, is certainly the newest
nomenclature, is it not.
Have you heard of the new engagement ? Miss
Burdekin, the pretty, doll-like, eldest unmarried
daughter of the late Sydney Burdekin, and Mr.
Alec. Hay, of Berry, one of ‘ our eligibles.’
The other exciting matrimonial event of the
wet\ was a wedding that broke in half suddenly.
Two\undred guests received two hundred letters
on the morning of the ceremony announcing a
postponement. There wasn’t going to be any
wedding that day. The bride was suddenly taken
to Tasmania, the bridegroom walks the town like a
man in a tranee. Bride declares she doesn’t know
why it was broken off. Bridegroom declares
he doesn’t know either. Brothers are said to be
at the bottom of the breakage, but the guests who
are now folding up their sheeny wedding gar-
ments don’t look happy. b 6
Madame Soldene’s sudden disappearance is in
everybody’s mouth. (By the way, that’s an awk-
ward sentence. You won’t like it if you take it
literally.) The genial Emily got back to Sydney
j e l a year ™ London, stayed a fortnight, and
suddeniy went off ’Ome again by the same boat,
the Ortona. I hear on good authority that the
plague scare frightened her out of the place.
The bubonic rat whose ambition it is to infect
the inmates of Govt. House will have its oppor-
tunities curtailed now that a rat-proof fence en-
circles the sacred precincts of Vice-Royalty. The
idea is likely to be taken up by Sydney’s Noblest who
will hermetically seal themselves in their dwellings
at Potts Point, while the dwellers in terraces
might erect a rat-proof covering on the plan af an
enlarged meat-safe. The sixpence-a-night dosser
will have to be content with a portable covering
on wheels which he could hitch over himself like
a hen-coop when he retired to roost. Ladies will
probably take up the lately-invented antiseptic
anklet. The Chinese dame owes her “golden
hlies ’ to the olub-foot of an old-time Empress.
Similarly in ages to come the antiquarian will dis-
cover the origin of anklet-wearing in the Great
Plague of 1900, which decimated the city and
swept away hundreds per day (by special train to
the Blue Mountains). By the way it is not the
first time that anklets have been used in the
history of N.S.W., but the former ornaments were
of heavier make and had bracelets to match.
Society has received its second shock over the
plague. A beautiful young girl, living in an
elegant home in Woollahra. has fallen victim to
the scourge.
The remark of a late V.R. Private Secretary,
in a province that shall be nameless, still rankles.
An amiable Society woman asked him confiden-
tially : “But tell me, how was it that the de
Veres were not asked?” He looked extremely
surprised. “Surely a lot of old people like that
don’t want to go to dances ! ” said he, innocently.
r l hat’s the worst of being a Vere de Sydney,
thin, hungry, distant, and to a fair, full-blooded
young man— old 1
The “Tyranny of Tears” is the play we all know
all about. Not from life. Not at all. The woman
who bursts into tears whenever she does not get
her own way is a being I, for one, can honestly
and solemnly, and very, very emphatically say I
have never met. In short, she does not exist.
How do we know all about her then ? We have
all read about her in novels, dear old novels,
written by Men. That was before Women used
to write. Please observe that you never come
across the little wet handkerchief in a Woman’s
novel.
A woman who wants her own way nowadays
just gets it. She does not cry, or sulk, or go
home to mother, or cut the buttons off his shirts
(a male novelist invented that), or go to bed, like
Balzac’s father, and stay there 20 years, and one
day get up and go on as if she had never been to
bed at all. Nothing so troublesome, so enervat-
ing, so nasty, so uncivilised, so old-fashioned. She
just says, “ I want my own way.” And takes it.
That’s where Haddon Chambers is out in his
psychology.' The dates are wrong, a hundred
years astray. A hundred years ago women had
only one weapon to fight the world with. The
name of their gun was Tears. Now they have an
armoury, and an arsenal. In a word, there’s the
Woman’s Movement.
And, the funny part of it is man prefers the old
gun (that was loaded) to the deadly new one.
That’s where Haddon Chambers is out again.
Our own Brough goes through a horrible trans-
formation in the “Tyranny.” It isn’t only that
he’s a novelist. Worse. He is pale, and fat, and
unhealthy, and looks as if he had been brought up
on tea. And he wears whiskers, and a fearful
little coat and curly rough hair. It makes you
feel you never want to read a novel again for fear
Brough—otherwise Clement Parbury—might have
written it.
The frocking is ever so charming. Mrs. Brough
and Grace Noble have it all tneir own way,
especially the latter, Parbury’s wife. Mrs.
Brough, as a typewriter, looks her best in red-gold
curly hair, a powder-blue frock with white cuffs
and collar, and vivid blue lining. Miss Noble
wears a sweet yellow crepe gown, tucked round
the skirt, with violets at her breast. She
also airs a pretty dinner frock of white mixed
silk, box-pleated downwards from the waist, and
breaking out about the feet. Another taking
dress is her pale-tan mousseline de soie, with
many little frills edged with tiny violet ribbon. A
violet hat tops this, and everyone was sorry on
Monday to see a flounce ripped off by an unman-
ageable door.
At one time and another the most absurd yarns
have been told of Lady Clinton Hope’s theatrical
experiences in Australia. A dozen years ago, or
more, Frank Clark, inflated with the success of
his “Muldoon’s Picnic” and Silk Stockings
Variety Co. at the little Victoria Hall, Mel-
bourne, brought from Yankeeland an “All Star
C 0.,” which opened at Melbourne Opera House.
The performance consisted solely of “turns,” with
no first part, and Miss May Yohe, the female
baritone, was one of the turns. Miss Yohe is
remembered as a “ladylike young person” in a
long Bernhardtesque skirt, and Australia never
even thought of her in tights until she appeared
as a “principal boy” in some illustrated papers
from London.
A certain widower with several children is about
to marry a widow, who, besides her good looks and
pretty ways, has also a quiver-full. The other-
night the two families were discussing wedding
arrangements when Ivan, aged 11,. remarked to
his step-mother elect: “ You’ve already got about
a hundred children, yet you are only just getting
married. People will wonder how you’ve man-
aged it.”
Rudyard Kipling wrote a letter for a soldier
whose right hand had been amputated, and the
incident has begun its journey round the world,
stopping at all stations, and back again. A chim-
panzee would have done so much, if he could, for
another ape, but the act, according to the para-
grapher, “ shows Mr. Kipling’s personal sym-
pathy with the wounded.” Also, “the letter
which was received by the mutilated man’s mother
is greatly prized by her.” Very likely. If her
sense of humor equals that of the paragraph person
she would have replied to her son that Kipling’s
fist on paper more than compensated for the burial
of his.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ Lady Madden’s suggestion of a Floral set of
Lancers at St. Vincent’s Hospital Ball (June 6th.)
was duly adopted. Sets of “best people” are
being arranged to represent the four seasons of the
year, with Night and Hay to bring up the rear.
Hebuting dancers are also to have a set to them-
selves, and earn the distinction of being pump-
handled by the Acting Governess afterwards.
The Sisters of Charity who run St. Vincent’s need
no hints in diplomacy. They get all the Pro-
testant power they can on the war-path, and rely
upon the faithful of their own creed to swell the
coffers. It is marvellous how much of the world
these good souls see from over their garden wall,
and how cleverly they work the ropes.
“ Rinking is to get another chance as a pastime
for Melbourne’s naicest. A new club, with Lady
Widow Janet for president, Mrs. Hodges hon.
secretary, and Lord Dick as treasurer, started its
wild career on castors at the old South Yarra Rink
one night last week. The weather behaved
abominably, but all the same his lordship—the
poor dear treasurer—sat in a draught near the
doorway and acted as money-taker in honor of the
occasion. Under the circumstances such conduct
was almost noble.
“Recently, when that dainty little singer was
to have been married at Williamstown, the
bridegroom, whose occupation leads him to go down
to the sea in ships, was still rocking in the cradle
of the deep at the moment when he was due at
the altar. As the bride and her maids were
decked for the sacrifice, the feast spread, the
guests assembled, and the musicians engaged, the
reception went on swimmingly without the
absentee. Next day, when the involuntary
laggard-in-love readied port, that mere detail—
the marriage ceremony—-was quietly gone through
with.
“Of course it was unavoidable in this case, but
I am afraid this single-harness wedding (even
though ‘ the grey mare be the better horse ’ in
nine cases out of ten) has established a dangerous
precedent. Men, cowards that they are, always
have writhed under the fierce light that beats upon
orange-blossom, and, now that the thin edge of the
wedge has been introduced, we’ll find ’em shuffling
at the tag-end just in time for bare essentials.
“But a weak-kneed bridegroom might do even
worse than shirk the starting-point. There’s a
S ass-widow in Melbourne, a popular, estimable
ly, whose long-ago bridegroom left her between
two bites of bridal-cake to consult a time-table,
and never came back. Some people argue that he
Sist have crept into a spring-lock chest for fun,
e ‘ Ginevra,’ and got fastened down. Such was
not the case, however.
“Re weddings. There was a big missionary
gathering at Kew (Melb.) t’other week, when the
only daughter of Missionary Paton, the New
Hebridean veteran, married Mr. Gillan, a mission-
ary worker in the South Seas. The bride-
groom, whose first wife is buried in the Islands,
is unanimously regarded as having secured ‘ a
cornerstone beautifully polished,’so to speak, of
the missionary fabric. The bride was born in the
mission field and knows all its ins and outs, as her
brothers follow their fine old father’s footsteps in
the Isles of the Pacific. She has travelled and
seen the world, but her heart apparently remains
true to the dusky friends of her youth. Her
father, among missionaries, is looked upon as a
Judge in Israel. Not only has he run all sorts of
risks, cannibalistic and other, in his pioneering
work, but he has gathered in thousands of pounds
for ‘ the Cause.’ Mrs. Paton has earned a good
deal of money by her pen, and is as graceful and
witty as if she had not shared in missionary hard-
ships, nursed her husband and children through
illnesses without a white person within hundreds
of miles, and seen some of her little ones laid in
improvised coffins, fitted up for them by her own
hands.
“ An old Melb. magnate aged 70, and his bride
aged 32, departed for Europe last week per mail-
boat. The lady was advised to avoid observation
by calling him “pa” which she probably will do.
“ Some of our fighting boys only come to be
death-or-glory “Bushmen” after great tribulation.
A draper of scribe’s acquaintance was wild to go
forth to war with the last contingent of ‘ men from
Snowy River.’ The doctors pronounced him
‘sound but soft,’ and when he confessed his occu-
pation, shoved him back to his haberdashery with
a sneer. Home he went, and after a week’s re-
hearsal, presented himself again as a swarthy,
swearful swaggie. This time he not only passed
flying, but heard himself chuckled over by the ex-
perts as ‘ a tough case, good for wear,’ ‘ a rough
diamond,’ and ‘a real old fighting kangaroo.’
“ Melbourne has heard nothing more of the
proposed Johnnie Aitkens Brigade for gentlemanly
scouting in South Africa. The scheme was
scouted out of existence seemingly, or perhaps
the would-be Johnnies took fright at the account
■of how that gilded yeoman the Duke of Marl-
borough, fixed himself up for warfare. The Dook
went off to the ‘Front’ with four chargers and
four sumpter horses, likewise a light Cape cart
and a private baggage waggon. Coat-of-arms
painted on cart and waggon. Two body servants
follow him to battle; each mounted on one of the
•extra gee-gees, and leading another, so that his-
Nibs may have, five horses shot under him before
he is reduced to the beastly necessity of fighting
•on foot. Two grooms also are included in the
Marlborough outfit. They will presumably stay in
camp to look after the three remaining chargers
whilst their liege lord is out on the Boer track.
What a picnic !
“ Apropos of that stage morality discussion.
Three times in as many Melbourne theatres I have
heard the wives of three leading men complaining
aloud and bitterly of Alonzo’s shocking -goings-on
with the leading lady, and his shameful neglect of
herself. One of the wives visited one particular
theatre regularly, and watched hubby making
stage love to her rival, keeping up a running
comment on his words and actions that advertised
her own woe and put the lovely hero away with
half the adorers in the circle,
“ A ‘well-known shoplifter’ of the fair sex was
up at Melb. City Court last week on the simple
little charge of ‘shaking’ a parasol from a hall-
stand in a private house. The hall door was open
seemingly, so the hardened purloiner just stepped
inside and exchanged her own battered gamp for
the newer article. A sentence of three months for
such a mild transaction nearly took her breath
away. She could barely gasp ‘Well, so help
me ! ’
“ Dr. Hopkins, whose friends mourn his death
in the Transvaal, was the son of a local Independ-
ent parson. The clever, plucky surgeon leaves a
widow, who all along found it impossible to recon-
cile herself to her husband’s departure for Africa,
though she’s strong-minded enough—having before
marriage successfully filled the post of hospital
matron. Dr. Hopkins came of an eager, strenuous
clan—a typical family-group such as one learns by
experience to recognise as cradled under the roof-
tree of a brainy Dissenting preacher. Another of
the brothers, a rising lawyer, is- pronounced by
fair friends ‘ the image of Napoleon in his youth.’
Which recalls that there is in Australia a beautiful
girl who is a direct descendant of the little Corporal
whom she facially much resembles.
“ Our ‘ plague-inspector,’ glazier by trade, has
for landlord a householder in a large way, who, ’tis
said,. has never favored his tenant with an order
for so much as a penn’orth of window-pane. Lately
the injured putty-artist visited, in his inspectorial
capacity, his landlord’s private premises, and more
in sorrow than anger, so he says, brought about a
smart fineon the score of a dirty yard. The landlord
promptly retaliated by raising the inspector’s
rent, and now our suburb is divided against itself
on the question.
“ Has anyone noticed the ohange that has come
over nurses’ outdoor uniforms ? At one time they
were so unbecoming that one wondered a nurse
ever put her nose outside a hospital, but nowa-
days they are the smartest, daintiest affairs. One
handsome nurse wears dove grey, and looks the
veriest saint. Another (who must have private
means to pay for so much laundrying) appears all
in white. Another in a beautiful shade of green.
What wonder that the masculine heart flutters at
an apparition so fresh, so pure, so neat—and, of
course, so divinely unselfish !
“ Talking of nurses, one told me a story the
other day. A child had died in hospital, and its
friends bought for it a white coffin trimmed with
colored artificial flowers. The corpse was dressed
in a silk frock, muoh betrimmed, with kid gloves
on its hands, silk stockings and kid boots 1
“ The best, and perhaps most humorous, imita-
tion of Brassey was that of the late J. F. Edgar,
who was a capital mimic. A record of it exists on
a phonographic cylinder in the possession, writer
believes, of John M'Whae, chairman of the Stock
Exchange. The little speech is a burlesque of an
address of the Old Man of the Sea, when he hadn’t
quite made up his mind whether to subscribe to
an institution or not, and was hum-ha-ing it over ;
in the end, of course, nothing is ‘ presented ’ but
the thanks of the community. But Edgar used to
say ‘ Brassey is not so bad as people make out.
He even gave me something once. ‘ What was
that?’ people would naturally ask. ‘The ear-
ache,’ was Edgar’s reply. ‘He talked to me one
morning for a solid hour, at Government House,
about the navy.’
Y ours affectionately,

Company’s Building, Martin Place, Sydney.
William Williams, whose portrait, with
those of his daughter, grandson and a great grand-
child, appear in this issue, lives at Adelong,
N.S.W., and attained his hundredth birthday on
May 3rd. He is a native of North Wales, has
been in Australia since 1826, and if he is alive on
Jan. Ist next will have had the unique experience
of having lived in the 18th, 19th, and 20th
centuries. He claims to have discovered gold
near Bathurst in 1830, and, however that may be,
he “struck it rich” at the Turon, and his sobriquet
of “ Old Gold Dust ” has stuck to him for half a
century. His acquired wealth from mining
amounted to £60,000. His chances of putting up
a still bigger score against the bowling of Father
Time are remarkably good, as he is active enough
to hobble about his garden with the help of a stick,
and he will be able to read this paragraph without
the aid of glasses.
The plague has had its advantages for some
people, though not for most. The Sydney Safe Deposit,
for instance, has had a great accession of business during
the last few months, consequent on persons having de-
cided to prepare for the worst by placing their papers,
&c., in safety. The fear is that when a house is quaran-
tined valuables may go astray. It is well to be on the
safe side, and the Safe Deposit affords perfect protection
against either fire or burglars. It is well worth a visit,
anyway. The Deposit is situated in Ash-street, near to
Paling and Co’s.*

PERSONAL ITEMS.
The people of N.S. Wales may depend
upon Premier Lyne looking after the Plague. It
has got into the very short street in which he him-
self resides.
Some men have all the luck. It is stated
that Humphrey Oxenham got—hold your breath !
—£20,000 for his charming house and grounds
opposite Randwick racecourse.
Silth, the 14-year-old Port Melbourne boy
who won the Vic. Eight Hour £lOOO lottery-prize,
the other day, earned the shilling he invested by
selling dead rats to the local corporation.
BUBONIG PLAGUE (to the Sydney aldermen): “My hearty thanks for the excellent
quarters you have given me. Were all cities as thoughtful, I would no
longer have to rove, but could settle down comfortably.”
Donald MacDonald, of Ladysmith and the
Argus, could never be persuaded to witness an
execution at Melb. gaol, but lie now says that,
after witnessing the effects of shell-fire, he can
stand anything.
Says a Victorian Oppositionist: “Ten
years ago I advertised for an office-boy, at 10s. a
week, and was answered by a youngster who
seemed smart enough, but looked too much like a
Spanish priest for my fancy. To-day, my rejected
oflice-boy faces me as a Minister of the Crown.”
Died in Bendigo Joel Horwood, who
founded Horwood’s foundry, known wherever
mining machinery is known in Australia. He
was a plucky call-payer, too —no atmosphere
breeds more of them than Bendigo’s. E. J. Hor-
wood, Broken Hill manager of the Broken Hill
Prop., is a son.
The will of the late Robert Chirnside,
formerly of Carranballac (Vic.), was proved last
week at £335,000, equally divided between real and
personal estate. No wonder that any new and
unknown “Gussie” who throws his money about
at a Melbourne bar is always addressed as “ Mr.
Chirnside ? ” by the enquiring Hebe.
Very few people know that the late Presi-
dent Ayers, of S.A. Leg. .Council, was a Hebrew
ana that his father’s name was Aarons. The latter
was a fiddler in the early days of the province,
but had luck enough to get into the Burra Copper
Mining Cos., out of which he made a fortune.
Sydney Catholic .Press, which, as the
Sydney D.T. religious .editor remarks, “has a
religious classification style of its own,” thus speci-
fies the religious persuasions of the present
N.S.W. Government: —“Mr. Lyne and Mr. See
(Anglicans), Mr. Fegan (a Wesleyan local
preacher), Mr. Perry (an Orangeman), Mr.
Hassall and Mr. Wood (Protestants).”
A. courteous letter from Melbourne :
Dear Bulletin.— One of your wise men once said there
were three types of then hearing the name, “William.”
First, the stern, hard business man, who went through
life known to everybody as William So-and-So ; secondly,
the genial “ bloke ” who answered always to the name of
“ Billy ” ; and, lastly, the joyous ass invariably called
“ Willy.” Wherefore I rise to protest against anyone re-
ferring to the Victorian P.M.U. as “Billy’’Watt! Watt
—Watt Rot! —has never been known by any other name
but “ Willy,” and that is the name Nature intended him
to wear. lie is the A.N.A. personified—a young man
with a glib tongue and a swollen head, who jumped from
a humble clerical position to a Ministerial portfolio in two
years, and who backed down from his Boer sympathies
with the speed of a Carbine when the storm was coming.
Don’t waste sympathy on Watt —pray rather that Mrs.
Jardine had been armed with a cat of scorpions.
The late manager Alfred Priestley’s ex-
planation of the failure of his Federal Bank was
curious. He said it was due to “the weeping
away of deposits.” Those who did not “weep
away ” their money wept because they didn’t.
“ Brother Alec.” being about to give up
the civic chair of Perth, W. G. Brookman, of
expensive-bed-curtain fame, aspires to the vacant
seat. What a whirligig ! Eight years ago W. G.
Brookman wasn’t buying golden bedroom-curtain
poles set with diamonds.
The present war is inflicting hideous
injustices upon unfortunate babes. Scores of them
are being called after the British Generals and
places in S’Africa. One wretched little morsel of
Sydney humanity has now been christened
Modderina Belmontina Metbuena Jones.

Four Melbourne bank-managers of the
panic period are now dead :—Grqpnluw, of the
Colonial; Colin Longmuir, of the City of Mel-
bourne ; Priestley, of the Federal; and Frank
Grey Smith, of the National. One— Finlayson, of
the Union Bank—has retired. There are left:
John Sawers, of the Australasia; J. I). Law, of
the Bank of Victoria; Guthrie, of the London
Bank ; Gyles Turner, of the Commercial; and
Murchison, of the Bank of N.S. W.
Indisposed Premier Lyne’s suggested
Cabinet meeting at his private house last week
was not the unusual thing some critics supposed.
N.S.W. Premiers have frequently held' such
meetings in their private houses, and sometimes
in the house of a colleague. Parkes held numerous
meetings of the kind in his Balmain house during
the great strike, when alleged patriotism produced
special constables as it now produces Boer-baiters.
C. H. Buzacott, M.L.C. (Q.) lately
travelling through western N.S.W., with a far-
away Federal look in his eye, landed in Sydney
48 years ago and started “comping” in S.M.
Herald office ; thence he went to Parkes's
Emigre, and later on, with a few cases
of type, shot boldly into Queensland and
turned out his own paper, Maryborough
Chronicle. Those who can picture Mary-
borough 40 years ago will appreciate
the printer’s courage at that date. Buza-
cott went into several journalistic ventures
afterwards, ending up as managing-director
of Brisbane Courier and Queenslander.
He entered Parliament in ’73, but active
politics and journalism didn’t run well
together, and for a time he had to let
both go. In early life he was a fervid
Radical ; now his prime characteristic
is “ moderation in everything.” Hois
65 years of age.
A Melb. pressman prides himself
on being the only individual in his pro-
vince who has had his boots blacked
by an Anglican Bishop. He was
visiting the Melanesian mission
station at Norfolk Island, and
Bishop Selwyn the younger ex-
plained to him that the work was
shared by all, there being no ser-
vants. Next morning the quill-
driver set about blacking his boots, when
the Bishop insisted on saving him the trouble.
There was a struggle for the brushes in which
the ho3t prevailed.
After the official proposal to create a
new Imperial Court of Appeal, with a paid
Australian head as one of its six members,
can any one wonder that Baronet Way (S.A.
Chief Justice) is against any undue exaltation of
the Federal High Court? He stands, or thinks
he does, a good chance of being the Australian
representative as he is already a P.C. Also, he
has privately circulated in London a pamphlet
emphasising his view of the situation.
“ P.”: Partly through The Bulle-
tin, and partly otherwise, S. Australia
had come to regard Premier Fred. Holder
as a long, thin person who, when not
juggling with the province’s figures,
keeps his hand in by balancing Jonah
and his whale on the edge of the science-
book which he used to teach out of in
day-school; also as a person beloved by
the W.C.T.A. for looking upon strong
drink with the great shudder of a blame-
less life. South Australia was, therefore,
too shocked for words when it read lately
that Fred. Holder had come before the
city P.M. with a sad confession de-
nouncing himself as a person who drank
the Cursed Stuff and wasted his sub-
stance in riotous beer and crayfish, and
in consequence sought an order compelling
himself to pay over all his “screw” to
his wife. The P.M., the report said, re-
monstrated—he ought not to carry his
self-abnegation so far. But Mr. Holder
was too penitent to keep back even three-
pence—his wife, he added, was a better
treasurer than he. That fine spirit led the
South Australian saints to almost forgive
the sinner, so that Holder might still have
been safe—if he had been the Premier.
As it happened, another paper came
along just then stating that the penitent
sinner’s full name was Frederick
George, and the Premier’s is Frederick
William. And that spoilt everything.
But the man got his order, nevertheless
—under the Married Women’s Protec-
tion Act, one of the measures passed
when Kingston was at his zenith.
A candidate for N.S.W. Parlia-
ment had called a meeting at a mining
township and had brought his chairman
with him. One of the audience pro-
tested against an exotic chairman, and
said that if they elected a local man they
could be sure of being allowed fully to
exercise their rights as electors. The
speaker had a tine flow of language, and
occupied about three-quarters of an
hour, and, according to arrangement,
the seconder took up about the same time.
They had their way, and the new chairman, after
thanking them at great length for the honor they
had done him, declared the meeting closed on
account of the lateness of the hour ! Whereupon
the candidate wept bitterly.
The service of a writ for libel claiming
€2000 from Rev. Dill Macky as Moderator of
N.H.W. Presbyterian Assembly created loud
rattling of dry bones, and had the case gone into
court a new defence of privilege would have been
set up. Conciliation, however, prevailed, law
proceedings were cancelled, and plaintiff and de-
fendant are again lambs with but a single baa.

Nobody has looked for pathos in the fact
that manager Frank Grey Smith died but a few
hours previous to the National Bank meeting
whereat shareholders consented to wipe half-a-
million pounds of share capital and “accumu-
lated profits ” off their slato. “ Frank Grey ”
loved his bank ! The reconstruction in ’93 well
nigh broke him up, and the necessity for writing-
down the stock seven years later must have
troubled him much.
Chief Justice Madden (Vic.), as lucky as
was C.J. Darley (N.S.W.) a short time back,
draws at the rate of £10,500 a year for his services
on the Bench and in the cocked-hat of vice-
regality. It seems to have been expected that
His Honor would regard the greater boodle of £7OOO
a year for Acting-Governorship as cancelling (pro
tern.) the £3500 a year due to his Chief-Justice-
ship, but at present lie is drawing both allow-
ances.
Out of nearly 700 members in the House
of Commons, not within living memory has one of
their names commenced with the letter “Z,”
whereas Victoria’s Parliament has never in its
living memory been without a “ Z.” A goldfields
constituency started the thing with a representa-
tive named Zincke. Then East Melb. selected Zox
and returned him regularly for 20 years. Win.
Austin Zeal, President of the Leg. Council, is a
third omega.
The death is reported from England of
Alfred Priestley, formerly manager of the Federal
Bank, Melbourne. Poor Priestley used to say,
and probably believed to his dying day, that it
was all owing to the fact that the big banks did
not prop up his tottering institution that the great
financial crisis occurred. “Look at them !” he
would say, as bank after bank closed up, “they
wouldn’t help me, and look what has happened to
them ! ” The Federal was ex-Premier Jimmy
Munro’s bank. Priestley was a portly, puffy,
pompous man, who looked like a butler ; but the
crash turned him into a white thin wreck with
staring eyes.

Aboriginalities.
At its last meeting N.S.W. Aborigines
Board voted winter clothes for 22 “ wild blacks ”
near Wentworth. “ Many years ago,” Inspector-
General Fosbery told the board, “ a blackfellow
and two gins vanished from a place called Poola-
macca, near Wentworth, and were never heard of
for about 30 years, when they were discovered—
-40 of them.” Which, except for the name of the
vanishing point, is a pretty fair summary. There
was, of course, a woman at the bottom of it.
Ninnia killed a lubra and had to “ trek,” which he
did promptly, with two young gins. They hid
themselves in the mallee and porcupine (this was
on a fenced-out part of Cuthero station), lived on
emu and kangaroo, gathered water from mulga-
roots when they couldn’t get it otherwise, and
liked it all so well that they kept on hiding for 25
years. Their tracks were often seen ; but it is a
tired place, and Ninnia had been forgiven and
almost forgotten, so nobody bothered. In ’94,
though, Cuthero was duller than usual—so dull
that at last a party was made up, mostly of half-
castes, to round up the lost tribe. There were 30
of them—six blackfellows, seven lubras, 17 pica-
ninnies. Ninnia was still alive, but he got such a
horrible scare that he didn’t live long afterwards.
A curious fact about them was that, so far : s any-
body could ever learn, not one of them had been
given a name.

“Countlee all welly bad: no lingbarkee ; no

labbit gotem bank. ”

A dusky dame of many charms, wife of a
Maori chief, eloped with a curly-headed Adonis
from a far-off village a while back, and the
abandoned husband decided to adopt the good old
custom of his forefathers in such cases made and
provided. He organised a “ robbing party ” and
raided the settlement in a perfectly friendly man-
ner, securing horses, cattle, guns, and whatever he
wanted in exchange for his faithless spouse. Then
he joyfully returned home. Simple and satis-

“ Thurla ” : I have ob-
served that sometimes from
famine or for plunder the
small black ant will raid the
nest of the soldier-ant, and after
defeating the big fellows (a sure
thing once they start the job),
they seal all the holes up with a
sticky mud, and probably live
in the nests as long as the food
lasts. If two or more blacks
get hold of a “soldier” it is a
case with him, for others
promptly swarm on to him and
nip his legs off, leaving a help-
’-'ss wreck. Under soldier-ants’
ne*,.A are said to live a couple of
small black-and - white - ringed
snakes. They will be found
i'ust under the surface of the
leap in wet weather. Was
once shown a couple by a
cocky on the Castlareagh, who
said he had ploughed the nest
up and turned them out. They
were about lOin. long and about
the thickness of a man’s little
finger. I once broke into the
nest of some beef-ants, and was
surprised to find a small colony
of the black-and-yellow sugar-
ants living with them quite con-
tentedly—maybe as slaves.

The water-chyainth is be-
coming only too common in
Australian rivers and water-
holes. Not generally known
that this plant lias proved a per-
fect curse to many American
waterways, completely choking
them up and blocking naviga-
tion. It has also the invaluable
property of absorbing an im-
nense amount of moisture,
.a ving been apparently designed
for the express purpose of dry-
ing up swamps and morasses.
A country so subject to drought
as Australia has especial reason
to fear this scourge. It already
abounds in the waters of South Queensland.

The S.A. Govt, recently purchased from
millionaire J. H. Angas the old German village of
Klemzig—closer settlement having made great
strides. Early in the 40’s, S. A.’s father, G. F. Angas,
assisted some” 30 destitute families out from der
Vaterland, and settled them at Klemzig (on the
Torrens banks), and, until 20 years ago, existed
there the artist’s ideal of the old-fashioned mud
and thatched cottages. When a certain well-
advertised “Exhibition” showman eloped with a
dark-eyed daughter of Israel, Klemzig was their
first halting-place. The swain begged milk for
the fainting lady, but when the reviver was
brought forth in a very ignominious domestic
utensil, the runaways departed hastily, heedless
of protestations that the receptacle was never in
that settlement applied to its customary pur-
poses.

“ Vigilans ” : I had beeu “ travelling ” six
days without meat when I niet three swagmen
with well-filled nose-bags, and three dogs as fat as
George Pile’s. “ How do you keep your dogs in
such condition?” I asked. “What part of the
world do you come from ? ” said one, as each
offered me mutton. I have seen sheep lying as if
they had died, but on closer examination found
that they had been killed, portions taken away,
the skin pulled into place again, and the sheep
then turned over. Very many swagmen out-back
have a dog, and that dog is always fat. Does this
state of things arise from the fact that it’s almost
useless to ask for rations now ?
“ Hogan’s Ghost ” : A bush-
hawker up this way was seated on the
box of his waggon amusing some chil-
dren with a music-box. When the box
had run-down the youngsters asked the
man to wind it up again, but he made
no answer, and, after repeated attempts
to get him to speak, the children went
home and tola their mother that the
hawker had fallen asleep while playing
for them. The mother said : “ We will
go down in the evening and get him
to play again.” But when they went
in the evening, the hawker still sat
on his box. He was dead. Almost
an ideal end, too. Dying to music,
and in giving pleasure to others.
“From sudden death, good Lord
deliver us 1” Nonsense !

“T.G.F.”. Have never seen
anything in print about the bird known
as the “ Gang-Gang.” It is of the
parrot genus—brown in color with
wavy red lines and a scarlet top-
knot. When disturbed, it makes a
noise like a com-sheller. Had one given
to me once, but have no idea where it
originally came from. Can any of your
readers enlighten ?
The cocky’s new man thought he’d make a
good start, so he was up at daybreak and made
straight to work, reckoning on scoring a point
with his boss for early rising. But half way down
the paddock he met the cockie himself, and was
angrily admonished to “Sneak down quietly. If
the other chaps see you they’ll be wantin’ a ’alf-
holiday, too!’'
• • * *
The gins in a much-frequented camp on
the outskirts of a Wastralian mining town were
lately medically examined. There were 20 of
them : eight were loathesomely unsound and two
more were bad enough to account for the
flourishing state of the local pharmacies.
» * • *
Near Beechwood, Hastings River (N.S. W.)
is a blind fossicker, who is led around by his dog
through scrub and vines, swamps and snakes. He
has sunk 12ft. for water, and has a nice set of steps
cut to the bottom of the shaft. He says he lias a
dyke 2f>ft. wide, out of which he has made 720
assays—best of which went 18dwts. gold and 4oz.
silver to the ton. Ho works like a tiger and can
sink as straight a shaft as anybody.
* * * *
The population of the Lyell (M.L.) mostly
consists of worn-out miners, shrivelled Chinamen,
the registrar, the policeman, and “King” Fran-
cis, who keeps a shanty public-house, and suffers
from the delusion that lie is Emperor of China
and King of the Lost Tribes (the latter ably repre-
sented by the frequenters of his bar. lie is en-
couraged in this Belief because he sometimes
creates a “knight,” which means free drinks for
all hands. Thus the ceremony—His Imperial
Majesty enters with the candidate for the honor
of knighthood, seats himself, and repeats an
absurd formula, which is supposed to invest the
other with the proposed dignity. Then free lush
is served by the regal barman until everybody is
gloriously drunk. Strange to say —apart from this
extraordinary craze, “ the King ” —possesses keen
mental faculties. But—it’s an ill wind that blows
nobody good.

Dear Bulletin.— Be school-teachers’ woes,
what about the unfortunate teacher of a country
grammar-school ? He is generally a young fellow
of little over 20, and in addition to his keep gets
about £30 a year, on which he is expected to dress
well, keep a horse, and belong to the local cricket,
tennis, football, and cycling-clubs. Although re-
cognised as a member of local Society, ho ranks in
it Delow a 16-year-okl bank-clerk. Also it de-
pends upon his individual boss whether his life is
a misery or not. Very often he has been the
crack scholar of some big Sydney school, and yet
his chief wonders that he can never stand more
than a year’s patronage by aitchless “leaders of
society. ” —Savage.
« * * *
“ Yar. ” : Once, spent a night at a hotel
other side of the Lachlan, and can’t forget it.
Found my way to a room that contained a single
bed, the covering of which didn’t look clean.
However, being sleepy, I chanced it, and was just
dozing off when a dirty Hindoo entered—there
was no lock—and gave me to understand that I
had his bed-offering, as evidence, some blood on
the sheet, which he said had come from a cut on
his finger, that had been injured in “ opening tin
of sardines.” In further proof of his statement,
he also drew my attention to floor, liberally spotted
with oil “ from fish,” and to a pair of much-soiled
toe-wraps, near head of bed. I was satisfied.
Dressed, and spent the night in astronomical
observations, and in shaking myself. Left before
breakfast, not wishing to express my feelings to
the landlord, who thought he could fight, and
would —the groom informed me—“job a bloke in
the eye in half a jiff.” And these pubs, are in-
spected regularly !

S. Australia’s date-plantations in the far

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Serenely went the weeks of Lent at Jamberoont Creek
Until one Friday- afternoon the Reverend John McGleeck
Went in to tea with Pat Magee, an Irish farmer, who
Would always find “ the cloth" a meal, as fanners mostly
do.
There is no need to say the creed of Reverend McGleeck
Was not the same as Pat Magee’s, of Jamberoora Creek !
For, John McGleeck, whose ample cheek was something
passing bounds,
Was Scotchbyterian Catechist who did the country
rounds.
Now Pat Magee, you’ll easy see, has quite a different
brand,
And, Kit, his wife, was just the same, you’ll easy under-
stand.
And Pat and Kit had worldly wit, a plain, hard-working
pair,
And Irish as the butter milk in Limerick and Kildare I
When Kit Magee brought in the tea the parson showed
surprise
To see a joint of mutton placed before his staring eyes

How Some Women Face
Trouble.
With the newspapers full of stories of misery
and crime, no apology is needed for re-printing a
charming story of a N.S.W. lady’s happiness and
courage. The paper from which it is clipped re-
ports the matter as a simple news item emanating
from its representative at Toowamba, via Eden.
Here lives Mrs. Ann Targett, a lady 59 years of
age. Mrs. Targett said : —“ Eight years ago indi-
gestion attacked me. Afterdating I suffered from
wind, severe pains, and a fulness which made me
drowsy and uncomfortable. I tried many reme-
dies but got worse year after year. Then my
heart became weak and I was continually suffer-
ing from sick headaches. Later on rheumatic
pains came in the left leg, and what with the pain
and loss of sleep and appetite I became very weak
and continued so, despite medical attendance.
Whilst reading the paper one day I noticed Dr.
Williams’ pink pills advertised, and commenced
them. A week later I felt decidedly improved
and was t-hys encouraged to continue them until
quite cured. Dr. Williams’ pink pills have made
me stronger and healthier than I have been for
many years.”
Nearly all ailments arise from impoverished
blood or unstrung nerves. As Dr. Williams’ pink
pills for pale people are a blood builder and nerve
tonic they therefore naturally cure these ailments
which include sciatica, neuralgia, consumption,
bronchitis, erysipelas, scrofula, etc. Dr. Williams’
pink pills are sold by Dr. Williams’ Medicine Co.,
Queen’s Place, Sydney, two and nine per box, and
by chemists and storekeepers—But mind you ask
for Dr. Williams.*
Kuppers Elberfeld Lager.
“ Illawarra ” : When skull-driving
in the Backbloeks once I got a bad back
which I couldn’t account for, till I dis-
covered that my bed was stuffed with
dirty wool, in lumps the size of one’s fist.
On enquiry I found that the bed had
done duty for three generations—grand-
father, granny, uncle Mick, aunt Bridget,
and Heaven knows how many other
ancients had died on it. I left, and eight
years later met a man with a weak back,
and learned that he was my successor at
“Monkey Flat,” and had occupied same
old bed. For the purposes of a bed I have
found nothing worse than wool, and no-
thing better than guru-leaves.

“J.H.8.”: When eel-fishing in
the Wanganui River (M.L. J I saw a swan
some distance off thrusting its head under
water in the usual way; but presently,
observing something wrong, I rowed over,
and found that an enormous eel had swal-
lowed the swan’s head. The eel couldn’t
swallow any more, nor, owing presumably
to the reversal of the feathers on the
swan’s neck, could it get rid of what was
evidently a losing contract ; with the
result that both parties presently gave up
the ghost. Hoisted them into the boat,
and found that the eel measured 9ft. in
length by 2ft. Gin. in girth. [Thought
the conger (salt-water) was the largest
known eel ; never heard of one lOir.
in diam.—Ed. B.].
Dear Bulletin. “G. 8.” (B. 81/8/’00)
says “ the ‘ wind ’ of a tree in the Southern
Hemisphere is from E. to N., or in a course that
follows the sun.” My experience, however (as a
man earning his living at the game), is that trees
“ wind ” mostly to the left; which would be from
W. to N. In splitting trees with a “ left-hand
wind ” the split will always follow the “wind,”
hut a “right-hand wind” will noarly always split
straight. I have worked trees that were quite
straight in the grain for about Sin. in from out-
side, but inside that they were like a rope. In a
pine scrub the young pines don’t see the sun until
they have pierced the mat of dense foliage over-
head ; yet they are just as “ windy ” as any hard-
wood crowing in the open forest.—Broadaxk.
* * * *
Anew terror has been added to river-
travelling Out-back (N. S. W.). The Darling began
to rise, the other day, and about the biggest of
Far-West traders, who had, months ago, decided
to settle down in Sydney, but had been drought-
bound, got on a steamer with his family and made
up-stream for the rail terminus at Bourke. But
just then the weir at -Bourke was raised, the
“fresh ” was impounded behind it, and, of course,
the steamer further down was soon left stranded !
Then the steamer passengers got out and walked,
or waited for a coach to pick them up. From
Bourke down to Wentworth that solitary weir is
■ursed hourly for seven days after every reported
“fresh.”

In Pitt-street, Sydney, the other day while
a well-known vet. had his hand far back in a
horse’s mouth feeling for irregular growths, an ex-
cited youth rushed up, and started bashing the
moke upon the nose. He supposed the expert
was having his arm chewed off !

When the Namoi (N.S.W.) blacks wanted to
appeal for rain to Indoo, the water-god, the young
single men were made to bring a certain number
of kangaroos’ bladders filled with the natural
liquid to the head of the tribe. The “ karra ” then
commenced. The liquid from the bladders was
emptied into a wooden vessel held by the chief,
who with one hand dribbled fine white sand into
it while a voung girl stirred the mixture slowly
with an emu’s quill, a low chant being kept up
during the performance, which usually lasted
about an hour.
“Lyndhursfc” : I can back up those of
your correspondents who say that crows are a pest.
Nearly all the stock-boards (composed of squat-
ters) pay 6d. for every crow killed —which doesn’t
look much as if the crow, as alleged, is the
“squatter’s friend.” It is also said that crows are
farmers’ pals, but the farmer himself is of a very
different opinion. [ln whatever relations he
may stand to pastoralist and agriculturist
—personally, as it were, the crow is a bird of great
parts. As a domestic pet he is the most amusing
of any, with his perpetual suggestion of a black-
clad hypocrite of infinite humor ; and his cry,
doleful as it is, is much less irritating than the
screech of a cockatoo. Also, he can give points
for knowingness to any cockatoo.
A TRANSLATION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Old Mrs. de Laney was “ Frinch,” but although
As rich as the richest, not quite up to date
In the manners and frills of Potts Pointers ; and so
She kept to herself on de Launay Estate.
But Nora, her daughter, was perfect in all
Accomplishments fitting her proud Norman name—
Could play the piano, or dance at a ball,
And mixed with Society’s crime de la crime.
“ Now, mother,” one evening said she, “ the M’Kew*
Have sent invitations for dinner to-day ;
And this time you really must come. No excuse 1 ”
Said madam : “ Bad scran to their dinner, I say.
“ I’ve got me own grub, an’ I’ll ate it at hoam,
Where none’ll find fault wid me shtyle an’ addhress ;
I don’t know their ways, and I don’t want to know ’em
Besides, I’d be shamin’ you, Nora, no less.”
“ But, mother, I’m sure you’re as good as the rest.
You needn’t talk much —just reply with a bow,
And don’t eat a great deal—decline if you’re pressed.”
“ Decline ! Be me sowkins, I wouldn’t know how ! ”
“ Well, listen. Speak softly ; there’s no need to yell,
And answer (no matter how hungry you feel),
* No, thank you, I’ve done most remarkably well,’
Or, 1 Thanks, but I’ve made a most elegant meal.’ ”
The dinner is served. The de Laneys are there ;
The old woman’s lips are apparently glued ;
Each course goes untasted. “ Twould make a praste
shwear,”
Thinks she, “ to be lavin’ sich iligaut food.”
“ You don’t like boiled chicken, dear Mrs. de L. —
Pray let me exchange it for some fricasseed.”
“No, thank you, I’m doin’ mosht damnably well—
In fact, I’ve been havin’ a of a feed.”

Mr. Durant Snook divorced his wife, the co-
respondents being Grosvenor Mossop and Harold
Snushall —the latter, however, leaving the court
“ without a stain.” Mrs. Snook was probably
desirous of getting her name changed at all
hazards, though her taste in problematical alterna-

For ({ore Than One Hundred years
SOAP
has maintained its supremacy in the face of the whole
world’s competition. Such a record could not be
achieved without cause. Temporary successes are com-
paratively easy, but for an article to maintain its popu-
larity generation after generation it must appeal to
something more than passing fancy. This is the case
with Pears’ Soap. It is, and always has been, an honest
product. Everywhere abroad it has found a place in
public favour equal to that for so long held in England.
Men and women alike find it good and reliable. The
man who has once tried Pears Soap in the form of a
Shaving Stick wants no other, he takes it with him on all
his journeys. The woman who travels and fails to take
a supply of Pears’ must put up with substitutes until
her burning, smarting skin inexorably demands the
“ matchless for the complexion.”

A COACHMAN’S STORY
“Rheumatism,” said a leading physician not long
since “may attack anybody, but is especially the
disease of age and poverty. Jhc immedmte cause
is an irritant poison in the blood, which, be °°? L
ing lodged in those parts of the system where the
SS lias the &* !<««
less violent inflammation. This poisonis ah . y
associated with impaired digestion on the part ot
the Stomach and liver, and the amount of it m the
system is increased by the inactivity ofthei excre
rive organs, particularly the skm, bowels, and
the correctness of this view, the fol- ,
lowing conclusion is clearly deducible from it, nam-
ely, that to relieve or cure a case of rheumatism
we should seek, first, to prevent the formation
of the blood by correcting the impaired digestion,
and, second, to stimulate the skm, bowels, and kid-
neys, that they may throw it off ; or in other
words, we must try to purify the blood. Outward
applications, although they may, and do, modify
pain at certain inflamed spots, cannot, in the
nature of things, eradicate the cause of disease.
The following case illustrates the truth ot tins
theory, and should be attentively studied by all
who are afflicted with gout and rheumatism—the
two ailments being, under different names, prac-
tically the same thing. , , ,
“Sixteen years ago I had an attack or rheu-
matic gout wliich affected all my joints, giving me
intense pain. My hands, feet, and shoulders were
puffed up and swollen, and for many weeks I suf-
fered martyrdom. After this I was from time to
time subject to rheumatism, which moved about
my system, sometimes appearing in one part and
then another. Bor five years I suffered like this.
“ In the autumn of 1885, whilst in the employ-
ment of a doctor at Bayswater as coachman, my
eyes became affectod and I was blind, not being
able to see either the numbers or names of the
streets I drove along, My eyes were like a piece
of liver, and the doctor I was with sent me first to
an eye specialist, and afterwards gave me a note,
and I went to St. Mary’s Hospital, Haddington,
where I attended as an outdoor patient for nine
months. , ,
“ I was so bad I had to give up my employment.
The doctors at the hospital made a thorough
examination of my eyes, and said they wore sound
and that my affection was caused by rheumatic
gout. They gave me medicines and drops for the
eyes; also placed blisters behind the ears and on
the temples, but £ was little better for anything.
“Some days L was better then worse, and L
feared I should lose my sight altogether. In July
1880, my brother came to London on a visit, and
urged mo to try Mother Seigel’s Syrup, as ho
thought it would drive the rheumatism out of my
system. I f?ot ti bottle of this medicine ftom
Whitoloy’s, in Wostbourno Grove, and after taking
two bottles I was wonderfully better. My sight
returned, and 1 felt bettor of myself. When l had
taken six bottles I w(ts well as ever, and have since
been well. You can publish this letter, and refer
anyone to me. (Signed) Joseph Parker, 21,
Ploointield Street, WestbourneSquare, Bayswater,
July Ist,, 1890.” .
Mr. Parker is a respectable man and worthy ot
implicit confidence He is now in the employment
of Mr. Whiteley, the famous purveyor, of whom
he bought Mother SeigeFs Syrup in the tune of
his necessity. The cure is certainly remarkable,
and demonstrates the truth of the proposition,
now admitted by the highest medical authorities,
that rheumatism is a disease of the blood, caused
at the root of it, by chronic dyspepsia and mdi
gestion. Mother Seigel’s Syrup, being the most
successful medicine in the world for all ailments
of the digestion, consequently prevents the further
formation of the rheumatic poison,_ expels it from
all places where it has produced inflammation m
the body, and hence cures rheumatism. r l he reader
will note that it is now ten years since
Mr. Parker’s recovery, during which period he
has had no relapse. Therefore the cure was real
and radical.
The Tonic.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He was a jaded, well-dressed man of thirty-eight,
and he sat on the dummy of a tram next an old
Irish laborer who smoked a pipe. The old fellow
smoked cheap tobacco with hungry zest, and our
friend smoked an expensive cigar with dull list-
lessness. Something of the contrast struck him,
for he was introspective, and he felt rather more
jaded than before. He was reviewing himself :
He had worked hard in business, probably too
hard, and he certainly had worked too hard at
“enjoying himself” ; in his prime he found him-
self envying an old man his honest smoke. And
now he had started thinking—how busy were the
streets, and how interested in their own concerns
the people ! What could they find to interest
them ? And the perky clerklets flitting about
with tiny cigarette in mouth, boldly ogling pretty
firls —too responsive some of them ah, well !
e knew all about it. And the thirsty loafers
round the bars busily battling for drinks ! Why,
they seemed to find life positively interesting
but no, he had not energy enough to want to
make a hog of himself.
“ The fact is, old man,” he told himself, you
have exhausted everything; you have exhausted
yourself ”
Great heavens ! was that a thunderbolt 1
When recovered he found a girl sitting on his
kne«; she was confused and frightened, and
quickly squeezed into a place, by his side. She
had jumped on the car while in motion and had
been thrown violently against the languid pas-
senger. . ,
But what a vision of blushing cheeks, glowing
eyes and panting bosom ! How fresh, and young, ,
and bright ! And she begged his pardon so
heartily, and, as she did so, explained that the
conductor wouldn’t stop, and it seemed so easy to
jump on. What part of the country had she
come from ? Bendigo way ? He knew Bendigo
district very well, and soon they chatted comfort-
ably ; or, rather, she talked, talked quite uncon-
strainedly, talked incessantly, merrily; and
laughed with a clear, bright, jolly laugh.. She
came to town to try and get a billet as a waitress
or something’ike that; “respectable, you know,
she added iii uecided tones, looking him full in
the face. ' She quite willingly accepted his offer to
look for something suitable.
“ Hullo ! old man,” said a friend shortly after;
“ you look pretty fit -been in the country?”
“ I think I have,” he replied. .
And he got her a nice situation in a highly
respeotable tea-room, and saw her homo at night,
and took her to theatres, and dressed himself
quite sprucely, and got quite young-looking again.
“Since I met you,” lie told her, “everything is
as rosy-red as your cheeks, and life with you .
would be one long, bright dream.” |
“ I always enjoy myself in your company, she .
prattled ; “you draw mo out of myself—and then i
you know everything.”
Marry her?—urn—no; ho hardly thought so— 1
not unless there were dangerous rivals, and he
saw none. . ,
But one night he saw her with that conceited
young ass, Talkins, who was a member of the
A.N.A., and had ambitions. Hearing his own
name, he listened.
“Well, dear,”she expostulated, “how could I
help it ? He would come pestering me, and how
could I tell what your intentions were ? I know
you’ll despise me, but I would sooner marry an
old frump like him than keep house even with
you, dear. I’ve seen so much ! But, oh !he was
heavy ! I had to pull him along all the time ! ”
And as he walked away he looked more jaded
than ever.
BLUE-BLACK.
Writes Ernest Favenc :
I am glad to find Tire Bulletin take kindly notice of
the death of that old Northern Territory man, Alfred
Pybus. I see that he died at Powell’s Creek, a station
right on his old familiar hunting-ground. I know nothing
of Pybus’s exploits with Gov. Kintore, but I knew him in
’79, when he had the reputation of being one of the best
linesmen on the Overland. I saw him putting up a big
telegraph poston the banks of the Katherine River after
the high flood of that year, when station-master Murray,
of the Katherine, did a big swimming and line-repairing
feat. The post required to be of special height and
strength, for the Katherine River is no trifle, and the
crossing wire has to be well elevated. In fact, it was a
giant Of a post, but the way Pybus and his men took
liberties with it, and chucked it about like a match, is
something to remember. Pybus was a good fellow and a
clever all-round bushman of the old type, of which so
many were in the construction-party of the Overland—
men who were perfectly at home in the bush, and entirely
self-reliant. Yet, presumably Pybus would have been
rejected for N.S.W. Bushmen’s Contingent, to judge by
some of the samples I saw walking about in kharki as
full-fledged bushmen.
Henry B. T. Strangways, who has.dug him-
self up in London to protest in the papers against
the Appeal clauses of the Commonwealth Bill, is
a pretty far-back Australian relic. Also, he is a
politician with a very scrappy record. He was
Attorney-General for a year and 11 days in the
first Reynolds (S.A.) Ministry of 1860. He was
Commissioner for Something for 20 weeks in the
second Reynolds Ministry. He was Commissioner
of the same thing under Waterhouse for 20 months,
and again under Dutton for six months, and again
under Ayers for a month. Then he was twice
Premier, once for 18 months and once for 18 days.
Thirty years ago Strangways wandered disap-
pointedly back to England, where he has been
a J.P. ever since, though nothing else is known
against his character save that his two middle
names are Bull Templar. Anyhow, as an autho-
rity on Australian affairs, Strangways is rather
out of date.
Dear Bulletin :— Be the original of Thac-
keray’s Rev. Chas. Honeywood. I knew the man
well in Tasmania. He had been at school with
Thackeray at the Charter-house, and had become
a preacher at a fashionable London church, but,
developing a weakness for liquor, had been sent
to Tasmania as a Govt, chaplain. So popular did
his preaching become, however, that it emptied
the other churches, with the result that the
preacher was relegated to a country cure, where
lie was compelled to associate almost exclusively
with bushmen and roughs. His failing accordingly
again asserted itself, and when I came to know
him he had sunk past redemption, though at times
there would be shown a flash of the old fire. I
never heard anything more pathetic than his reply
to a lady who expressed disappointment at nis
sermon. “Well, madam,” he said, with a sigh,
“ every dog has his day—and this dog’s is past! ”
—W.L.


Expressions continually coming from those who
are happy in health are actuated,, firstly by
gratitude, and secondly, by compassion.. No pay
is asked or given. They say: “I feel it my duty. ’
The following is an example. It succeeds
thousands of similar ones and precedes thousands.
Mr. E. H. MTnnes, of Manchester House,
George-street, Sydney, says: “Being for a long
time troubled with my kidneys, I tried a great
many kinds of medicines, but without doing me-
any good. One day a friend of mine told me of
Warner’s Safe Cure as being a first class medicine
for my complaint. I went and got a bottle, and
found it did me good, and by the time I finished
the third bottle I was free from all pains and
miserable feelings which I had before I took to
Warner’s Safe Cure. I can really say that while
I was in Western Australia and on the goldfields
drinking bad water, Warner’s Safe Cure was my
best friend.” Late manager, Braemar House,
Mt. Macedon, Victoria.*

The present- is not the first war engineered
by the Rhodes gang. The Matabele war of ’93,
which enabled the Chartered Cos. to seize a terri-
tory larger than N.S. W., was regarded by persons
on the spot as the result of skilful diplomacy.
A lot of the lawyers say that to abolish
the appeal to the Privy Council “ would be to step
between the Australian citizen and his sovereign.”
What they really mean is that to abolish it would
be to step between the Australian lawyer and his
sovereigns.
The making of gifts to the various con-
tingents by Jingo tradesmen is, of course, a mere
attempt to paste their advts. on the British
Throne ; but the religious publishing house which
issues the “ Kharki ” Bible brazenly, endeavors to
paste its bills on the Throne of God.
Coincidently with the appointment of
Stipendiary-Magistrate Delohery as a N.S.W.
Public Service Commissioner, the various depart-
ments were called upon to show cause why the
sexagenarians in the service should not be retired !
The new Commissioner is himself 60, which goes
to show the absurdity of retiring a man because
he is that age. Query to N. S. W. Justice Dept.: Is
Mr. Delohery 60?—Yes. Is he fit to discharge
his duties ?—Yes. Does he intend to continue his
office ?—No. He is now a member of the Public
Service Board !

G. H. Reid still cherishes hope of being in
reality the first Premier of Federated Australia.
It is true that, barring accidents, Barton will pro-
bably be “ sent for ” to form the first Ministry ;
but the first Ministry will very likely be a mere
stopgap, and probably won’t amount from a per-
manent-loaves-and-fishes point of view to a row of
pins.

For five years Premier Philp, Minister for
Railways and member for Townsville, has, in and
out of season, opposed the extension of the present
useless Bowen Railway to its natural terminus at
Charters Towers (this would promptly stiffen the
port of Townsville). Now Philp appoints a Royal
Commission to gather evidence and recommend a
route, suppose the Commission furnish a common-
sense report ?
If Australians have the spunk of a flea
they will not suffer Chamberlain’s attempt to
alter the Commonwealth Bill—which voices .A.us-
trali an opinion as no measure ever did before—to
pass without instant and active protest. The
delegates in London need and deserve a popular
meeting in every town of Australia to stress the
fact that the whole Bill was endorsed and is de-
manded by a vast majority of voters.
Ex-Premier Kingston has cabled from
England urging the liberal press of Adelaide to
spare no effort to ensure the success of Liberalism
at the Council elections which take place this
Saturday, May' 19. There are 15 candidates for
eight seats, and of these the Liberal champions
are : Central District—Kirkpatrick and Robin-
son ; Southern District—Kingston and Gordon;
Northern District—Bice and Miller; and N E
District, Moody and Russell.

turned to Sydney the other day, he, in a manly
way, gave the Boers credit ‘ for fairness and
humanity—said he did not credit all the state-
ments made as to Boer brutality . . . highly-
colored, &c. The N.G.'VV. country press (so-called)
professes to reprint the interview, but in at least
five cases out of six it calmly omits all mention of
Mr. Dowling’s references to Boer humanity.

Robinson was only a tool in the hands of Rhodes,

An old man was in Maoriland recently
discovered cradling some stuff which he had
mined from the side of the road. He was blind,
and his cradle was specially constructed so that he
could save the gold by touch. He had knocked
out only 10s. worth in three months, but his old-
age pension kept him going.
The Marquis of Salisbury’s speech at the
Primrose League on May 9, in which he re-
marked that the manner in which the Trans-
vaal had armed itself in secret to such terrible
advantage showed how foolish it would be
for England to give Ireland Home Rule, sounds
the death-knell of all attempts to conciliate the
Irish. What Salisbury said may be true, but he
should never have said it. His speech shows the
utter hollowness of all the current shamrock busi-
ness—and what a slap in the face for the Queen is
the Premier’s utterance, reminding the Irish as it
does that, like the Boers, they are a half-con-
quered race, squirming under the heel of England!

Mayor declined to take the chair at the council’s conver-

It is, of course, the women’s sentiments that are
significant. Women with votes, too. The senti-

those of every greasy worshipper of Wealth,
Power, and Greed.
From the British Army and Navy
Gazette :
Complaints are made that the Boers lire upon our am-
bulance parties. Is it not possible, however, that some-
times this is done by accident ? Our own stupidity may
account for some cases, for it is impossible to conceive of
anything more characteristic of the stupidity of red-tape
than the putting of ambulance men and Medical Corps
into the same uniform as the combatant forces. Kharki is
admirably suited to the latter : but surely the object of
the uniform of the men under the Red Cross should be to
make them as conspicuous as possible. The red cross on
the sleeves of the men cannot be seen at any distance.
Some more sensible uniform might be found for the hos-
pital attendants than kharki.
John Burns, at a great meeting of Labor
representatives recently, made a most effective
onslaught on a proposition that only working
men should be eligible as representatives. “The
sooner we get rid of the idea that the man in fus-
tian has all the virtues the better. Men like Mr.
Courtenay, Mr. J. A. Hobson, Mr. Sydney Webb
might render Democracy far greater services than
an engineer or boiler-maker. Yet because they
did not toil with their hands, they were to be cast
aside.” The proposition only secured three votes,
the British delegates showing far more common
sense than the Australian ones possessed a few
years ago.
The Saturday Review four years ago was
quite a liberal and progressive organ, and used to
talk like this about the Boers—see the issue for
June 6, 1896 :
We are accused of being Boer sympathisers, and we
confess that we think little of those people who do not
sympathise with the Boers in their gallant efforts to pre-
serve the independence of their country. Courage and
physical endurance are qualities to honor even in an
enemy, and these Boers are only our enemies when we
attack them.
Now that the Boers are fighting for their indepen-
dence in real earnest, the Review is fiercely
Jingo, and confesses that it gives a word of praise
to .Toubert merely to bring out its intense hatred
for Kruger in a more lurid light.
Premier Kingston’s protest against S.A.
Chief Justice Way’s interference with the Com-
monwealth Bill is all the more warranted because
there is a suspicion that other Australian C. J.’s
have been doing the same thing behind the backs
of the people who pay their salaries. Australian
constitutions provide for the appointment of
judges by the Parliamentary Ministries, represent-
ing the people; and it is to the Parliaments which
can impeach and discard them that they owe
allegiance. That any Australian judge, as a
judge, should treat independently with the British
Cabinet is a gross breach of decorum and an
offence against the people who by their represen-
tatives elect and pay him. Mr. S. J. Way has
cooked his own goose. He will get no great office
under the Common wealth.
The latest charge against the N.S.W.
Early Closing Act is that it is responsible for the
present prevalence of typhoid in Sydney. One -
sage solemnly writes to a Sydney daily that
In shops the only ventilation is by the front door
These have been hermetically closed at an earlv hour in
the evening. I believe this system of keeping doom
closed up has much to do with the large increase in
typhoid, as by closing the doors all the conditions for
developing typhoid, diphtheria, &e. , are brought into play
Some other genius will now be arising to point
out that the Boer war, and the Indian famine are
also results of the iniquitous E.C. Act. Genius
No. 1, however, makes one statement which, com-
ing from a leading shop-keeper, is worth the atten-
tion of the shop inspectors. “In the majority of
shops the only ventilation is by the front door.”
This is as true as it is that in a majority of shops
the fenestration is as deficient as the ventilation;
artificial lighting being substituted for windows
with evil results m the way of air contamination.
Vic. press attack* on P.M.G. Watt re the
Parker case were unjustifiable even from a Jingo
— i.e., sane Jingo—point of view. Because Watt
refused at the outset to keep billets open for any
men of the Postal department who wanted to join
the Imperial Bushmen Corps lie was likened, by
the hysterical Age, to Premier Schreiner, the half-
hearted “traitor.” The comparison is absurd.
In Cape Colony the call from the Imperial Govt,
was for a rally of as many able-bodied young-
colonials as felt disposed to fight against the
invading Boers, therefore Schreiner, by dis-
couraging the enlistment of civil servants, showed
himself more or less disloyal to his official position.
In Victoria the call was for a given number of
Bushmen to join the Imperial army at five “bob”'
a day, and the reason given by Watt for refusing
leave of absence, on war business, to the post-
office employes should be more than satisfactory
to the wildest “ patriot.” Seeing that the supply
of volunteers was in excess of the demand, "the
Ministry wished to discourage the volunteering of
civil servants in order that they might not come
into competition with the genuine bushmen who
wanted to join it.” An unanswerable argument.

In Nov., ’97, the (hen Director of
N.S.W. Charitable Institutions re-
signed, and up to date the position,
though one of the most important in
the Chief Secretary’s department,
has not been permanently filled.
Some 18 months ago the daily pa)>erB
announced that a medical man high
in the Govt, service had been ap-
pointed by the Public Service Board.
Mr. Brunker, then Chief Secretary,
refused, however, to accept the
Board’s recommendation, and the
Board declined to make any other
and shifted the responsibility on to
the Minister. lirunker was adamant;
his successor, Mr. See, has apparently
taken the same view, and the I’ublio
Service Board will not alter its re-
commendation. The result of this
absurd deadlock is that the officer
acting as director cannot do any-
thing decisive as he does not know
any day when he will be superseded,
and the department is all at sixes and
sevens. If Parliament annually
votes a large salary to provide for
an officer to “direct” the huge
asylums where some 4000 aged de-
pendents on the State are lodged,
the least the Ministry can do is to
make an appointment to the Direc-
torship.
Chamberlain is claiming
for the British Parliament, in
respect of the Commonwealth
Bill, a, right which Queen Vic-
toria never claims in respect of
the most trivial Bill of an Aus-
tralian province. The royal
assent is occasionally refused to
provincial measures; but the
royal advisers never attempt to
tinker with those measures in
detail they do not alter a
measure on a vital point and
then tender approval. Why
should the infinitely - greater
measure establishing the Com-
monwealth be subjected to an emasculation which
the small measure of a single province is nob
called upon to suffer ?
The anti-Federal argument against Federal
Australia’s power under the Commonwealth Bill
to limit Privy Council appeals is rotten at the
root. If Australia can be trusted to make laws,
surely she can be trusted to say what they mean !
How will it help the British money-lender to
reserve to the Privy Council power to interpret
an Australian statute, when to Australia is re-
served the power to make domestic statutes as
objectionable to the British money-lender as she
chooses ?
