A very old Queensland identity died a few
days ago, in the person of Canon Glennie. The
rev. gentleman came to Sydney so Pl e years
ago, and was appointed incumbent of Moreton Bay
in ’4B. Well known all over the Darling Downs
district as a kindly, genial churchman of the old
type, who did good work in the days when the lire
of a country-clergvman was no easy one. By his
death, the Q. Govt, is relieved of the necessity of
paying a church-pension of £lOO per annum.
Died last week, Robert Bulcock of Bris-
bane, a one-time famous political organises Bul-
cock was originally an overlooker in an English
cotton-mill, whence he migrated to Queensland.
For a long time he was, outside Parliament, the
right-hand man of Sam Griffith and Co., and his
skill as a political manipulator was beyond all
question. Went into Parliament finally as mem-
ber for Enoggera and, on losing that seat, became
an M.L.C. of some importance. Was an ardent
teetotaller—a conspicuous churchgoer—but his
forte lay in the so-called “purification” —“Bul-
cocking—“ of electoral rolls.” Queensland readers
will pemember his famous libel action against the
old Boomerang and the futility of Bulcock’s
onslaught on that organ.
The harvest of Jingoism :
Corporal Harkus, of N.S.W. Lancers, has died of fever
at Bloefnfontein. Harkus went to England at Diamond
Jubilee time, and during his stay distinguished himself as
a first-class horseman. On the Lancers returning home
to N.S.W. from the festivities, the question of sending a
squadron for military instruction purposes to England
was mooted, and Harkus was among those chosen to go.
While training was proceeding, the Transvaal war broke
out and many of the Lancers volunteered to embark in a
quarrel, which was none of Australia’s. But not so
Harkus : he refused to volunteer, and with a few
others returned to N.S.W. —no doubt believing that
the claim of his home and wife and children was
Saramouqt. When the returning Lancers arrived in
f.S.W., anonymous Jingoes—anonymous, mind you, for
no man ever insulted him to his face —sent the now
deceased corporal and his comrades derisive tokens in the
shape of white feathers, &c. Harkus’ life promised to be
a hell-upon-earth, and so, to, please other people, he
sacrificed his home and went away to South Africa. And
now he is dead. Writes a sergeant of his regiment :
“ I have seen a letter to-day which says that Ben’s heart
was not in the fight. I know it was not. His heart was
with his wife and family, and he was forced to go to war
in consequence of the cruel sayings of those who did not
know him.”
The Bulletin only privately knows what were
the dead man’s opinions about the merits of the
war, but it does know that Harkus—who had so
distinguished himself in English and Australian
military tournaments as to be almost famous —
was esteemed by his brother-soldiers as a fine
fellow, and one who would have fought well in
defence of his native country. More than that the
Australian Republic will expect of none of her
sons. ' " .
To prevent Bubonic Plague wear Metzler’s
“ Protective Anklets,” a certain protective against
the access of vermin and disease germs from the
ground. They are worn by several members of the
medicAl profession, including doctors of the staff of
the Board of Health, and are worn also by mem-
bers of the N.S.W. Police Force on duty in the
plague-infected quarters of Sydney, Obtainable
from all first-class chemists or from Head Office
of the. Proprietary, 14 Castlereagh-street, Sydney.*

When Forrest puts the Federal Con-
stitution BiU before the Westralian electors,
its acceptance is a foregone conclusion. Both
Perth and the goldfields will give an over-
whelming majority, and a solid “no everywhere
else will not alter the result. The goldfields are
expected to vote 20 to 1 for Federation, as the
“ayes” will include many electors who, though
perhaps lukewarm Federationists, have a hearty
hatred of the existing state of affairs. The heavy
Custom duties, the infamous meat-ring, and a
score of minor persecutions, have so goaded the
miners that either Federation, Separation, or
another Eureka Stockade was bound to happen.
Messrs. C. H. Walthers and Co. have
given the saddlery and harness trade of N.S. W.
the biggest start that it has had for 25 years. They
introduced low prices, marking in plain figures,
and best quality into the business, with the result
that their popularity is growing daily. They give
a man the exact design he wants, in saddlery or
harness, and by thus making their goods to suit
the requirements of each individual, they have
pushed their competitors right out of many dis-
tricts, and promise to lead every where before long.
Ladies are especially pleased with the comfortable
saddles made by Walthers and Co. The firm has
a grand lot of canvas rugs to suit all buyers; and
the best stock of kangaroo stock-whips in the
world. Every man and woman in the country
should send for Walthers’ catalogue. The address
is Hunter-street, Sydney.*
The case of Smith v. Smith, in Melb.
Divorce Court recently, came rather rough on
four pressmen who took notes of the proceedings.
The petitioning wife sought a dissolution of
marriage with her husband, a journalist, on the
ground of his habitual pro-beerism and financial
incapacity. He was mostly unsober, she said, what
time lie was engaged in brain-working for news-
papers at sundry places, and the evidence went
to show that Smith sometimes took two quarts of
tanglefoot before he could express the feeling of
the country in a sub-leader. Barrister Coldham,
for the respondent, asked his Honor whether it
were possible for such a thirsty Smith to have pro-
duced a respectable journal twice a week regularly.
He spoke in a tone of deepest reverence for
literature and the Press, but his argument was
wasted. Judge A’Beckett, who had been jotting
down his judgment on a sheet of paper whilst
Cold ham held the floor, decided from his own
personal knowledge of up-country newspapers
that a bi-weekly issue was not inconsistent with a
charge of habitual drunkenness against the
editorial “ we.” Then his Honor cast a regretful
glance towards the reporters’ table, and granted a
decree nisi with costs.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*

Hori Grey gifted Auckland (M.L.)
with a priceless collection of curios, books, pic-
tures, and old-time MSS., but somnolent citizens,
with characteristic apathy, have neglected to erect
a building for their preservation and suitable dis-
a’ r . At present they are mouldering away in
-a-dozen different places in the local library,
and few people know the extent of the treasure
bequeathed them. A Grey memorial-hall has
time and again been suggested, but the absent-
minded public have lortg ago forgotten the great
pro consul. Even his proposed statue is hanging
fi re, only £BOO having so far been subscribed.

Some years ago, when the veteran R.C.
Bishop Lanigan, of Goulbum (N.S.W.), was on a
pastoral tour, the priest of one of the Southern
parishes (say, Father O’Flynn) preached, on the
Sunday of the bishop’s presence, a powerful
sermon on “ Evil Literature,” with special refer-
ence to books purporting to relate the misdoings of
monks and nuns. After the service, when seated
in Father O’F.’s dining-room, the bishop compli-
mented the latter on his excellent discourse.
“But, sure,” he added, “ye must have read a
vast hape of bad books ye’self, Father, to have
prepared so grand a sermon. ” “ That’s true, m’
lord, otherwise I wouldn’t have been in a position
to warn m’ people against such stuff.” “ Quite
true,” said the bishop; “but d’ye mane to say
now that ye read that awful book, ‘ Maria
Monk ’? ” Father O’Flynn admitted that he had,
for the reason given. “’Tis a book I’ve never
seen,” said the bishop—“have ye a copy by ye?”
“I have, m’ lord. I have not destroyed it yet,
but, of course, intend doing so, now that I have
preached the sermon.” “ Very good,” said the
prelate, “ but suppose ye just read me a little to-
noight, so that I may know something about the
vile book.” The Father dutifully complied and
read some of the portion dealing with the alleged
irregularities of nuns. When the reading was
finished, the priest was for destroying the took
then and there. “Isn’t it shocking?” said he.
“Well now,” said Dr. Lanigan, “ I don’t know so
much about that. There are some very quare
nuns, Father, very quare nuns, indade. P’raps
ye’d better not burn the book to-noight, and ye
can read me some more to-morrow. ” The second
reading dealt mostly with the misdeeds of priests,
and again did the Father suggest the immediate
destruction of the took, but again did the bishop
defer execution. “Ye know, Father,” he sain,
“it’s not such an awful book after all,, there arc
some quare priests, too, Father. We’ll have the
rest of the book to-morrow.” But at the third
reading, for which Father O’Flynn mischievously
selected episcopal backslidings, his Lordship
grew mighty restive, and at the close Father
O’F. asked once more what should be
done with the book. “Well,” said the bishop,
gravely, “it isn’t such a bad took” (here lie
abstractedly tore it in several pieces) “and there
are some quare bishops too ” (here ho abstractedly

reckless man or woman, and the result is some
serious disease develops. Dr. Morse’s Indian Root

A remarkable experience was that of
James M ‘Coy, the sick sailor rescued from the
Sierra. Nevada, recently wrecked near Port Phillip
Quarantine Station. Seriously injured at the be-
ginning of the voyage, M‘Coy was lying incapaci-
tated in his bunk when the vessel, in a gale, took
the rocks at two in the morning. Yet though 23 out
of 28 perished, the invalid, through the heroism
of his mates, got to shore and was taken to the
Quarantine Station to recover alongside the
bubonic who had reached there the
day before. Not far from where the Sierra
Nevada struck is the Formosa rock, behind which
the s.s. Cheviot was lost, with all hands, a few
years ago.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Second (Cup) day of S.A.J.O. autumn races
attracted a big crowd. Money put on six races totalled
£16,043 10s. as against £11,643 on first day. Last year’s
Oup Day investments showed £17,486. As was the case
on previous Saturday the biggest race (S.A. Cup) paid the
biggest div., £27 15s. Winner—Tarquin—was a2sto 1
against chance. Amount realised by the machine for this
event was 1 5464 10s. as against £5576 15s. on previous
year’s Cup. Sixteen starters had following sums opposite
their names :—Paul Pry £378 10s., La Carabine £2019 155.,
Tarquin £lB2, Veneda • 339 10s., Dirk H immerheda £134,
The Bride .>.432, Gunga Din £253 55., Fleet Admiral
£2Ol 10s., Wild Raven £286, Nitrous £49, Viking £lOO 55.,
Peeress £93 155., Merry Pilgrim £477, Trapper £92,
Princess of Wales £370 55., Ishmael £55 15s.
• • » •
S.A. Govt, declines to proclaim even a half
holiday for Adelaide Cup-day, and, as business people
follow suit, what was once a gay festival is now a gala day
for purely sporting people only. This gradual degenera-
tion was originally due to ex-Premier Kingston, who
foreclosed on the holiday business in a pious moment to
please his W.O.T.U. friends. One of the strangest traits
in Kingston’s character is his peculiar aversion to sport-
ing men, although in his day he was a prominent athlete,
himself.
* • • •
Sensational Westralian Neckersgat-Tarpeia nag
Tarquin won S.A. Cup comfortably, ana sprang a big sur-
prise. He had top weight (9st.), and humped it to the
front in great style. Tarquin was bred by Sir Thomas
Elder, and the horse’s success stamps him everything that
Westralian form indicated. He came over to the East
carrying, a big stigma, having been eliminated from an
important race after the public had piled cash on him.
He was one of the best “ fancies” for the Australian Cup
upon arriving in Adelaide, but again went out owing,
’twas said, to his then owner’s (Towton) serious illness.
Later the horse-racing community heard that Towton
had parted with the nag for a big sum —Mr. Phillips
(bookie) being the purchaser ; and Tarquin has since raced
in Mr. P.’s colors. Tarquin ran third to Paul Pry
in the Elder Stakes on first day of meeting.
Paul Pry finished five lengths in front of Veneda, who
beat Tarquin by two, which form, it might be remarked,
didn’t make the Westralian’s Cup chance look even fair,
much less the “ cert.” it turned out.
• * » •
La Carabine’s running in Adelaide Cup may not
have been so much below her recent “ form ” as it looks
on paper. The mare likes a longer distance than 13 fur-
longs, anyhow, and at Adelaide she was humping 9.2, a
big weight for her light frame.
* * * *
Ikey Mo cut a sinfully absurd figure by com-
parison with the Tote on Adelaide Oup. Ikey in his most
reckless mood laid no more than 12 to 1 against Tarquin,
and as a rule he offered 10’s with a great show of
liberality. A backer who stood out for 15’s got only scorn
and contumely from the jewelled parasite. And yet the
silent respectable machine paid 27 to 1, or thereabouts,
after deducting its div.
• * • «
S.A. J.C. meeting wound up Saturday. Tote
showed £12,445 for the day and £41,575 10s. for
the three, £3OOO less than last year’s results.
Veneda, favorite for the Fisher Stakes, was jammed on
the rails, and so left the road open for the three-year-old
Carlyon-Waterlily filly Martagon who got home by 2
lengths. Invested on machine, £1931; winner paid
£67 Bs. The Bride was favorite in S.A.J.O. Handicap,
but Veneda won, barely squelching Dirk Hammerhand on
the post with Foulsham’s Wild Raven third. Tote showed
£2401; “ div,” £9 12s. A handsome Thunderbolt-Quality
filly (Tip Top) got away with the Juvenile Stakes, and
Promontory easily won the Totalisator Handicap. Punters
piled up £2804, and the fortunate ones secured £l5 for
every £1 on the last winner.
* * * *
Ranfurly’s sensational div. of £lOl 13s. in
Adelaide Goodwood Handicap is the biggest in that race
since D.O.D.’s huge div. of £BOO odd in ’Bl. Neither
trainer nor jockey had anything on, Scobie’s dollars being
on Blue Cap. Ranfurly was expected to break down, but
instead he went through and beat all previous time
records. They run in all shapes !
m * * *
“A. J.C.” : Re Bulletin pars 12/5/’OO anent
S.A. J.O. tote divs. and Metallician Oxy’s disinterested re-
marks. Former gave latter a hard knock. Rank outsider
and all, though Ranfurly was in Goodwood Handicap, how
many Mo’s would have laid 20 to 1 against him ; but that
apart, the tote profits go to supply the sinews of war (the
prize money) for the sport, and the more we have of the
machine the more the money raced for will swell.
“If the totalisator was legalised,” said a well-known
Vic. authority the other day, “ there would be
no fear of any club in the colonies outdoing
the Melb. Oup.” Ikey doesn’t “cotton” to the
idea for various and obvious reasons, particularly as it
would be impossible to have anything “ in the bag ” with
the machine. This system of cold-blooded robbery is the
hottest thing the punter has to fight, and is an every-day
occurrence ; the toe-rag section are not by any means the
biggest offenders either. No sane man (who knows what
he is talking about), or disinterested person would
attempt to argue against the advantages of the tote to
the public who are the main support of the turf. Every
time tote is hinted in Parliament Mo gets his cash-
lubricated influence to work and the thing is squelched
in embryo.
• » • •
It is many a day since Ikey Mo pulled such a
long face as he did at Rosehill (Sydney) on Saturday last,
when Abedare, Foley, Cast Iron and Alix, all favourites,
got home in succession. Their comments on the number
of dead ’uns were loud and deep. Truth to tell, in
writer’s opinion the safety of the stiffs lay in their
numbers, for Rosehill is not as a rule a safe course for
owners to make a cemetery of ; but when it comes to an
enquiry into the running of the majority of the entrants
at a meeting it is calculated to make the most straight-
laced club go slow.
* • • *
“ Gun ” : Quite probable that prospective win-
ners of the “Royal and Birthday” double were among
the successful nags at Rosehill, Saturday. Cast Iron
(7.10 up) won the Flying Handicap from rise of barrier to
post giving nothing else a hope ; the next horse, Un-
daunted, was 8 lengths off. Time 1.16. Cast Iron has
6.13 in the “ Royal ” and his penalty will only bring him
up to 7st. Vic. Trenton mare Alix had a soft thing in the
Stewards’ Mile, and romped away from everything which
didn’t amount to much. The win was so easy that Alix’s
chance in the “Birthday” with but 6.12 up seems very
good.
**. * *
Sydney has been having its two trots a week
lately at Forest Lodge and Brighton, but this week the
Forest Lodge fixture fell through for want of entries.
The handicapping is the trouble, and it is a hopeless task
for anyone to essay unless he has been at the game in the
past, and is to a certain extent behind the scenes in the
present. The ridiculous mark on which King Harold was
placed in two trots at Forest Lodge robbed the event of
all interest and, when the club again called for entries
owners decided they were “not taking any.” The trot
at Brighton (Syd.) last Friday was a good handicap.
Souvenir, on paper looked if anything the best thing, but
Woodham driving Don Oleve made a close race of it for a
mile when Souvenir asserted himself and won comfortably
trotting his mile and half without skip or break at a 39 A
gait.
• * * •
“ Rex ” : Jockey Stephen Tucker, at Tam worth,
recently rode five winners and one second out of the six
events 1 Tucker is employed by Maitland-trainer Cush ;
he should be heard of again ere long.
* » » *
Tas: “Martindale” in a Sydney paper says:
“ in the last 25 years I have found that bookmakers, when
they own horses, run them straighter than the majority
of other owners. You can look up back numbers of the
Turf Register, and prove that in-and-out running is
rarely noticeable on the part of horses owned by one of
them.” But does “ Martindale ” mean only the horses
running in the bookmaker’s own name ? It is generally a
tough job to find out the real owners of a lot of horses
running in Melbourne and Sydney.
* * * •
The streaky M.L. colt Advance, by Vanguard
(whose seven consecutive wins and extraordinary feat, for
a 3-year-old, of carrying 10.5-over a mile-and-a-half made
him out the best the province had seen), was ignomini-
ously beaten in his last try in a classic event. He wasn’t
one, two, or three, but, owing to state of course, the race
was almost an aquatic one. Stead’s champion, Screw
Gun, was downed the week previous by Dan O’Brien’s
Australian-bred Military, by Light Artillery. Books
wouldn’t lay either of them. Writer never knew of a real
“moral” yet.
* * * *
A festive M.L. milk-merchant has a predilec-
tion for entering his early-risers for trots (always result-
lessly). Nominated for fun, as he said, his neddies
couldn’t go as fast as a man, and backers used to laugh to
see-them in front of a sulky. T’other day, however, he
entered a hairy-heeled hohbler, and it proved a particu-
larly juicy thing. Mr. Milk Oh ! and his pastoral friends
had the cream of the market, and raked-in much spoil.
The prad is still running in a milk-trap, but the authori-
ties are unpleasantly curious as to its previous history.
• • • •
“ Scrip ” : Remarkable how some horses “ come
to life ” again. A gammy-legged plater, reported dead a
year ago, won a minor race in southern M.L. the other
day.
• • * *
A long-haired preacher paid for admission to
Nelson (M.L.) horse gallops, and, taking a prominent
stand, planted a bull-provoking red flag inscribed “ after
this the Judgment.” Before the stewards had made up
their minds what to do, the club’s starter remarking that
the thing might come in some day, commandeered the
flag. Then the preacher got nasty, and was eventually
fired out.
• • » *
Scorn, the Maribyrnong Plate - winner, that
developed into a heartless deceiver, will get no more
Bulletin pars. The colt had a bad passage to India, and
died soon alter landing.
* • • *
It is erroneous to oall Charley Westbrook or
Sievier Sutton the father of Australian cash betting
Thirty years ago Dave Ingram (“ The Stranger ”) and J. H.
White—Myra Kemble’s husband—followed the game at
Randwick, and it was known just as early at Flemington.
Those who think pony-racing in Australia a
modern turf departure, will be surprised by the following
from Sydney Gazette of 1842:—“Mr. Ouvenagh, of the
Liverpool-road, desires to give amusement to his patrons
at a pony race meeting. A horse and fat bullock will be
run for by hacks.”
* * * *
“ San Remo ” (Rockhampton): A pacer is a fast
ambler, and travels between a trot and a gallop, throwing
his legs out on either side. Pacers do not compete in trot-
ting events, but are in a class by themselves.
• • * m
Coursing threatens to boom in N.S.W. this
season. Sydney 0.0.’s opening meet at New Belmore
(Canterbury) Coursing Park last week resulted. It was
too early for big entries, but good crowds rolled up.
Lamplighter won the chief event (Metrop. Stakes) in fine
style. Saw at least one queer occurrence on second day.
Two dogs were drawn together—one who had performed
badly previously and another that had only been brought
on the ground an hour or so before. New arrival was
made a 2-to-l-on favorite, while Mo laid 6 to 4 and evens
the other. Writer saw twoor three of the cutest, who never
“ come ” unless they’ve got an absolute cert., piling the
money on the outsider —had a bit that way himself in con-
sequence. When slipped, the favorite ran 40 or 50yds.,
then turned back; “unsighted,” said the judge. Let
loose again, the favorite simply turned tail, and scooted
off in an opposite direction, evidently afraid of the funny-
looking animal running in front of it. A third trial re-
sulted similarly. Judge disqualified favorite, and gave
the course to the other, but, alas for the clever “ push,”
declared all bets off ! Writer would like to wager the
money returned to him that the favorite in this instance had
never seen a hare and had never been in the slips before.
Same sort of thing killed the game in the mother province
years ago, and will do so again unless those responsible
rise to the occasion.
• • • • ,
“ Old Timer ” : Have heard much lately re pro-
bable big coursing stakes, but don’t think they’ll equal
those in evidence in N.S.W. years ago when “Charlie”
Gregory (Acct. G.P.0.), as sec., and the late Gardiner
(“ father of coursing in Australia ”) ran the show. Then
25gs. and 30gs. each were common, and once they ran off
an interprov. competition wherein 16 dogs subscribed 50gs.
each. First prize, 500g5., went to Hopbine, a N.S.W. dog.
• • • *
Sturgeon-Cribb pug. battle is to be repeated in
Sydney; they’ve signed for another “go” less than 6
weeks hence—date as yet unfixed. Stake, £5O a-side.
Writer likes Cribb’s chance better this time ; he’ll train
harder, and, having beaten his man once, has a lump in
hand. It happens—with few exceptions—in the fighting
game that he who beats another, otherwise than through
a foul, comes out atop if they get together again. Worth
telling here that Sturgeon’s gritty showing against the
Maorilander in their recent battle caused an admirer to
present him with a cottage out Newtown way and brought
on a benefit that panned out splendidly. Next struggle is
sure to be red-hot from “ time,” and will draw a record
house.
* * « »
“ Old-Timer ” : M.L. tailor-fighter, “ Torpedo ”
Billy Murphy—one-time feather-weight bruising champion
of the world, has been heard from. He says he’s coming
home shortly but won’t bring that belt he won and held
for three years against all comers. Billy’s story is that
the N.Y. sensational picture-paper giving it wanted him
to lodge a big deposit (big enough to buy two duplicates
of the whole thing), and he couldn’t stand that. Years
ago when Murphy came out here and went under to
Griffo, he had that belt with him and hawked it round on
exhibition in a big, full-length box while the other hand
encircled three or four gold-headed canes. Recollect
what a huge “pot” Griffo upset in Murphy at the old
York-street (Sydney) club ; and also remember how he
kept over 1000 members and friends waiting till he was
found in a chemist’s shop buying perfumes. Can call to
mind, too, that Murphy (on the best of terms with him-
self) asked the referee what round he wanted the match
ended in. “ Oh,” replied that official, “ lick him as soon
as you can, Billy.” And Billy tried very hard to oblige,
but the “ Rocks ” phenomenon knew the game too well.
» • * •
Mick Dooley has again been resurrected. He
fought Jack Anderson, a powerful lumper, at Perth
(W.A.), April 30. A huge crowd rolled up to see Anderson
annihilate the old bald-head—but he didn’t. Anderson
stripped 13.9 and Dooley 13. Mick was top dog from the
outset, and kept that wicked, vicious old sinister mauley
of his in the other man’s face all the time. Anderson did
fairly well when they got close together, but at long
range he proved a veritable chopping-block. Dooley
served out gruel hot and thick in the last round, with the
result that Anderson’s seconds humanely “ skied the
rag ”; their principal was in a sorry plight. Dooley is on
the warpath now after Bill Doherty’s scalp. Felix (alleged
champion of the provinces) still looks on, content to leave
the game to others, despite the bundles of challenges
hurled at him.
• • • •
“Golgo”: Re Tressider and Worboys. Met
Jim Stanbury in the street a few days before the match,
and when I expressed my belief in Tressider, the 16-stone
giant said : “ They crack Worboys to be better than I
ever was, and Peter Kemp says he’s as good as Searle. At
the same time my hit goes on the tried man, Tressider ; I
know what he can do and I’ll throw the losecs. in.” And
results again proved that it’s safer to back form than
reputation.
**, * •
At a recent M.L. sporting shivoo a statement
by Chairman Frank La wry, M.H.R., that he expected to
carry his Consultation Bill in the new parliament was
cheered. There is a strong feeling in M.L. for legalisation
of sweeps.
• * • •
“ Bondingie ” : Boxing experts have been hunt-
ing round for weeks for a man to take on the hitherto-in-
vincible Otto Cribb. Peter Murphy, the only Vic. man
talked of in this connection, is at best a mediocrity, and
has done but little fighting of late. On Cribb’s perfor-
mances, writer doesn’t think Murphy would last 2 rounds
with him, and would suggest that some patriotic Vic-
torian takes up Friday O’Neill. Were O’Neill trained for
a month in some sylvan spot far from the madding
crowd’s ignoble swipes, he would provide a fight fit for a
king. He is a 10-stoner of wonderful skill, hits a terrific
blow, and, de-pite his luxurious habits, always enters a
ring looking like a pink statue. Had Friday O’Neill been
an ascetic, he might have been a world-beater at his
weight.

whose parents (Simpson by name) kept a tobackery in
Christchurch, M.L.

Baynton district (Vic.) boasts of a family named
Ryan, of which there are four sons and five daughters
averaging 6ft. 2in. in height. The “ boys’ ” measure-
ments are 6.2, 6.5 J, 6.6, and 6.9. One girl stands 6.2, and
another 5.10, and the remaining three just a fraction
short of the last-given height. The old man was known
in the early days of the “diggings” as “Ryan the
jumper.” He could easily clear sft. lOin. in high jump-
ing, while at hop-step-and-jump none could touch his
record of 40ft. Ryan pire stood 6.3 and was well devel-
oped as are all members of his family. Ryan mire tops
6.10.
• • • •
Saturday’s inter-provincial 8-oar race at Bris-
bane was the 24t1», and the third in which four provinces
—Vic., N.S.W., Q. and S.A. —were represented. First
was five years ago, when N.S.W. won on the Parramatta,
and the second was last year, when Vic. triumphed on the
Yarra. Contests were initiated in ’7B—N.S.W. and Vic.
being engaged, and they were at it yearly tillTas. chipped
in for that occasion only. Q. first appeared on the scene
10 years ago, and has since figured in every race. N.S.W.
held aloof from ’97 and ’9B events through a difference over
the amateur question. Of 24 races Victoria has won 19,
N.S.W. 4, and Q. 1. Wortli mentioning that all four
struggles (’92, ’95, ’9B and ’00) in Brisbane have been well
fought out. One (’95) would have been a win for N.S.W.
but for bad luck in the last half-mile. Explanation c f
Vic.’s continued success is that that province’s men have
greater facilities for oar-rowing than are available else-
where (they can go in all weathers and have a smooth
surface to row upon) and have sunk every other depart-
ment of rowing in favor of oars. Save Fittes, Victoria
hasn’t produced an amateur sculler worth mentioning,
and her best pro. was Christie who, though an all-round
champion (boxer as well) didn’t amount to anything much.
Bar the fact that Victoria led from start to
finish, the interprov. 8-oar race on Brisbane (Q.)River last
Saturday was exciting, especially the struggle between
Q. and N.S.W , who were locked together for nearly two
miles ; afterwards, however, N.S.W. went away, finishing
three lengths behind winners and two lengths in front of
Q., between whom and S.A. there were three lengths.
There was a good tide, and the boats travelled at a great
pace. First mile, 4m. 35seo. ; second, 10.30, and the full
course—from Queensport to a point about 300yds. below
Toombul wharf—ls.so. The Vies, shaped in their usual
superb style, and sustained perfect form over the whole
journey, never once appearing disconcerted or flurried.
• • • »
Sculling-race for Australian anqateur supremacy
at Brisbane last Saturday was one-sided. Slack (Q.) led
Donald (Vic.) from the jump, and passed the mile
point (6.20) four lengths to the good ; thence on, it
was a procession, with the Victorian dropping farther and
farther in the rear, till at 2 miles (14.30) he was a quarter
of a mile off. Slack slackened, but still held the same
advantage, and the affair (it wasn’t a race) finished that
way in 23.23. Slack had a stump outrigger and Donald a
long boat. Again the stump outrigger !
* * * *
“Crab”: Apropos the amateur sculling cham-
pionship, Queensland has the credit of having started
these contests. Latest winner (Slack) got away with the
first in ’92 mainly through a bursting spurt near the
finish. Lambert (N.S.W.) had shown the way for over
two miles. Subsequently Slack visited Victoria and won
the local sculling premiership. Slack scored again on the
Parramatta in ’96. Very rough water that day favored
him, for stamina is his stand-by. Two years later Q. men
had the race to themselves—other provinces sent no
reps. Last year Daley (N.S.W.) put the Queenslander
down, but didn’t face the music this time, whereat Slack
was much disappointed.
• • • •
Mooted 20th-century regatta scheme has taken
root. A big meeting of rowing and sailing men in Sydney
last week affirmed the desirableness of arranging such a
function. It is to be of inter-provincial character, and
aid will be sought from N.S.W. Govt. First day (Jan. 26)
is for rowing races on the Parramatta, and second (Jan.
28) for sailing races down the harbor.
« • » *
The late Frank Grey Smith’s connection with
Melb. cricket everybody knows about; but it is not so
well known that he was one of the promoters of the S.A.
Cricket Association, and also, after it was formed, one of
its first presidents. That was 30 years ago; Mr. Smith
was then manager of the Bank of Adelaide. Of the dozen
who were his colleagues in the Association but one
remains in Australia—E. T. Smith, who is still going
strong.

The cure of piles by the new “ Cannalin ”
treatment is fully described in the pamphlet,
“Piles, and How to Cure Them,” by Albert
Sharp. Copies, with testimonials, sent free to any
colony from the depot, 257 George-street, Sydney.*
The N.S.W. All Public Schools’ swimming pr&-
mierships were contested at Hellings’ Baths (Sydney
Domain) Saturday. Low temperature of water militated
against usual big entry, and a bleak southerly wind made
things uncomfortable for contestants. “Snowy” Baker
(Orown-street) got home in both big events, beating Pert
(Fort-street) by 2yds. in the 100 (time, 1.8 3-5) and 15yds.
in the furlong (time, 2.51). First-mentioned performance
is a record for the race, but both lads did the distance
faster earlier in the season. Brief notice given prevented
others from competing, particularly one known to writer
who would have travelled the 100 at least a sec. quicker,
and the 220 perhaps 3sec. faster.
• « * »
A crack Sydney cricket-team lately made itself
ridiculous up North. At Lismore, they met a local team,
with whom there was an under-hand bowler (Balmer) who
proved disastrous—making the innings of the Metrops.
a mere procession. M. A. Noble, of world-wide
fame, was among the Sydneyites, and absolutely refused
to play the deliveries, with the result that his wicket was
soon spread-eagled. The ninth man out saw 39 up.
Balmer’s average read five wickets for one run. Shute
(captain of visiting 11) refused to bat after lunch, and the
team took the field, where they are said to have gone in
for clown business, and were jeered and hooted. The
bowling was farcical. When Lismore had notched 60 for
two wickets they put an end to the burlesque by with-
drawing, disgusted, and the big gathering of onlookers
applauded. On what grounds did the Sydneyites base
their objection to “grubbers”? That the aforesaid
“ grubbers ” were unplayable ?

Plague played-up with interprovincial tennis
premierships this year ; it alsd prevented Victoria from

Hawksbum C.C. won the Vic. cricket premier-
ship for the season and staggered reputedly cute people
who, at the outset, didn’t give them a hope. Hawksbum
were taken under the wing of the V.C.A. four years ago.
* * * *
Bowler Jones has resumed football to keep down
flesh. He is oaptain of N. Adelaide this season. Jones
was a brilliant player whc*n South Adelaide played him
first, but cricket fame caused him to drop the more risky
sport. Clem Hill is playing with Norwood this season,
and it is on the cards that Joe Darling, a fine back man
in his day, will play for the same club.
* * * •
Adelaide football produced big surprises in the
first matches, Norwood being defeated by West Torrens
and Port Adelaide going down to North Adelaide. These
surprises are the result of the compulsory electoral system
which has put teams on such a level that a revival of the
game is confidently anticipated.

Champion cyclist Sutherland, who beat E.
Reynolds in tests (at Christchurch, M.L.) for Paris Exhi-
bition athletic gala, was afterwards flattened out by M.L.
League of Wheelmen for accepting “ appearance money ”
in addition to prizes secured from sports committees ; but
matters have since been arranged, and he and Reynolds
go to represent M.L. at Paris after all.

“ J.S.” to The Bulletin :
With reference to the recent burning of a wooden bed-
ing-factory in Sydney, how has the City Council been
administering the Building Act, which prohibits the
erection of wooden structures within the city? Also,
what has become of the Oity Improvement Board pro-
vided for by the City of Sydney Improvement Act of 79,
which provides for the appointment of a board of five
members, of whom one at least shall be a professional
architect, one a practical builder, and one a medical prac-
titioner, which board shall have power to determine any
questions concerning the operation of the Act, and may,
when expedient, dispense with the observance of any
by-law.
“David G.” discourses on a military
matter: —
“Scotty the Wrinkler” (B. li/ifOO) forgets how the
army rifle has, for good reasons, been differentiated from
the sporting-arm. The latter is used against beasts, most
of them timid and all anxious to be let alone. At all
events, they can’t shoot back. But the army rifle is used
against game that does shoot back, and doesn’t usually
run away. While the soldier is stalking his game, the
game is stalking him, and hence the need in the military
weapon for long range and flat trajectory. This f.t.
widens the danger-zone, and smokeless powder makes it
hard for hunter and hunted to detect each other, so that
a present-day battle consists of long lines of scattered
men stalking each other, the victory generally going to
the cleverest stalkers. Sometimes the stalkers Hide and
wait for the game, as in a deer-drive. But in war, the
game isn’t driven ; it goes forward of itself, trying to
stalk the staler, and the result, as described by an eye-
witness at Colenso :—“ VVe bombarded every place that
looked like holding Boers for two hours, so that you would
have thought nothing could have been left alive in the
vicinity. But when the infantry began to advance thou-
sands of bullets began pattering about, and the enemy’s
guns pitched shells all over the place—where from no one
could see. I never saw a Boer all day, and I don’t think
anyone else did.” That is the perfection of stalking—
finding where the game will pass, and instead of going to
seek it, waiting for it and remaining hidden. Also, the
effect of artillery is muqh overrated. A shell bursts and
throws up an imposing cloud of dust, but the chances are
about 1000 to 1 it hits no one. If a man is standing
right in front of a bursting shell—not near enough to be
scorched—it is 7 to I on the average that none of the
splinters will hit him.
Writes a N.S.W. Mounted Rifles officer to
a friend in Sydney under date March 31, from
Bloemfontein:
Our correspondents keep a good dealtogether, and have
had quite a number of thrilling experiences. I don’t think
any of them will be sorry to get back to sunny N.S.
Wales. There was some excitement caused the other day,
when two war correspondents had a falling out. It seems
that one of them missed a large sum of money out of his
pockets during the night, and suspected his friend. In-
formation was given to the police, and the other corres-
pondent arrested. Afterwards the charge was withdrawn,
but, unfortunately, the arrested one made an accusation
to the Provost-Marshal that his accuser had been drink-
ing with “Tommies,” a terrible offence here. So the
arrested one is on his way to Capetown, and his accuser
has had his licence cancelled. This has caused a great
deal of regret, as the gentleman who had his licence can-
celled is a favorite and a very big-hearted man.
Now who could be the journalist who missed “ a
large sum of money”? Also who is the “big-
hearted man ” who boozed-up “ Tommy ” ?

Fifty-seven years ago The Mutual Life of
New York started out to do business without any
capital, since which it has turned over pretty
nearly 200 million pounds sterling and holds for
the benefit of its members close on 62 millions. It
has distributed among policy-holders and their
representatives about 106 millions, more than 60
millions of it going to living policy-holders, so that
the old wheeze that in life assurance a man has to
“die to win’’has been exploded. The cost of
running this big corporation, including commis-
sions and taxes, comes to over 30 millions, and the
amount received for investments in the shape of
interest, dividends and rents totals 41i millions,
so that there is a good balance on the side of profit
in the working. That is the record shown in the
Consolidated Revenue Account from Feb. 1,1843,
to Dec. 31, 1900. For further information apply
to Major Rennie, general manager.*
Despite the shocking example of Sydney
and the fact that the local rats were known to be
bubonic, the plague, when it struck Melbourne the
other day, found the smellful city with no hospital
ready—not even a temporary accom modat ion -house
—so that patients and contacts, after being driven
3 miles in a hearse-like conveyance, had to be
planted in the forepeak of the gunboat “ Albert ”
for a 6-hour voyage to quarantine. In spite of the
warnings dinned into their ears for months, the 24
councils have done practically nothing, and their
sole attempt to cope with the übiquitous rat has
degenerated into a scheme well-calculated for
spreading the plague. The offer of 2d. a head for
rats has brought in some 10,000; whereas 10 times as
many would hardly make any appreciable differ-
ence in the numbers of the rodents that swarm about
the filthy sewers, the alleged creeks, the Yarra,
and the four-and-twenty delectable tips. Also,
the “household inspection” has been purely
farcical, and has done nothing with “fowl-runs”
in small backyards, evil-smelling open drains,
leaky latrine-pans discharging into rights-of-way,
stables jammed up hard against dairies, and vacant
blocks made receptacles for unmentionable garb-
age. Knowing that the plague was at the door,
the paralytic municipal councillors sat down and
observed that they were not so dirty as some
people, thank God !—and did nothing. And even
the wealthy City corporation, when encouraged
thereto by one councillor, who said he had played
on the tips when a boy, and had thriven t>n it, and
by another who considered the city quite as clean
as English provincial cities (wfijch it calmly
shelved the matter of the obtaining more garbage-
destructors. Altogether, if Melbourne shall reap
as she has sown, she is like to fare worse than even
Sydney.
Cable says that Salisbury in his new role
of historical critic lately told the Primrose League:
“ Maritime empires had invariably been killed by
a blow at the very heart.” Let’s see.. The mari-
time empire of Athens—the first in authentic
history—was slowly battered to bits by nearly 30
years of war. Carthage had its limbs, Spain,
Sicily, and Sardinia, lopped off one by one ere the
heart was touched. Genoa and Venice lost their
maritime empires 200 years before the French re-
volutionary armies crushed their hearts. What
price Salisbury as historical critic ?

When Field-Marshal Roberts comman-
deered the leading pro-Boer newspaper in the
captured Free State capital, he, with soldierly
promptness, ordered that the policy of the organ
should be altered to one of uncompromising Im-
perialism. Verily, a new terror for the newspaper
world. The Bulletin has a vision of a long rank
of Sydney scribes standing with t?embling knees
before some conquering captain who, after
smithereening the defences at the Heads, has
the fair city of Sydney bleeding at his feet, j
“Journalists, ’t’shun !” —in a voice of thunder—
“ Cha-a-a-nge policy ! ” And presto ! the thing
will be done, and they will return to their offices f
and sweep the fragments of shrapnel off the floors
and open the windows to let out the lyddite fumes
and sadly go to their desks and write in greasy I
adulation of people and principles they detest. |
And the proud foreign invader will take a special
delight in trying his ’prentice hand as managing-
editor of Sydney Morning Herald. Its leading
space he will devote to the very rortiest of French
tales, and its commercial columns will be filled
with the sort of poetry that usually begins “There
was a young man of Calcutta.” And when Field-
Marshal Neild at last succeeds in ejecting the
foreign foe, the reputation which the Herald has
built up by years of consistent gravity will have
been scattered to the winds, and its once sanctified
name will be put on the index expurgatorius along
with the defunct Dead Bird —and “ Mr. James
will be turned out of his church membership, and
Editor Curnow will be forced by a vote of the
brethren to abandon his clerical shave as one no
longer worthy of the holy distinction.
“Banjo” Paterson figures among the contri-
butors to Lord Roberts’s Bloemfontein organ,
The Friend (probably a misprint for Fiend). On
the front page it is mentioned that the old motto
of the Free State was “ All will come right,” and
that the now motto is “All has come right.’
“ The British are here to stay,” remarks The
Friend. Which is probably true —thev never yet
took a return ticket when, gun in hand, they
visited a rich country. These verses appear over
“ Banjo’s ” name:—

The Case of
Mr. JOHN HUGH JONES
(From “ The Australian Star”)
Close to the park, and within sound of the
ocean rollers when they thunder on Waverley
cliff's, the reporter found 29 Fitzgerald
Street, the home of Mr J. H. Jones,
plasterer, and an old resident of the district!
While making an appointment to meet Mr.
Jones in the evening the reporter was glad
to hear he was away at work, for a man who
lias to go plastering must be pretty sound in
wind and limb, and it was a fair inference
that, however sick Mr. Jones had been, he
had made a good recovery Later, on being
seen, Mr. Jones expressed his perfect will”
ingness to relate his experience, and the
.n eat trouble lie had gone through.
was one of the great crowd who went
vT West Australia during the boom ; my
trade w as brisk there, but even in Perth the
living accommodation was frightfully in-
adequate ; my wife and I had to camp in a
tent ; this, combined with the poor food and
bad sanitary arrangements, was, I have no
doubt in my case, the cause, and that of
thousands of others, of serious, often fatal,
illness. I first got a touch of the Barcoo
Rot— you can yet see the scars on my hands
—then I was struck down with the fever ;
in my case it was rheumatic fever, and I was
removed to the Perth Hospital. Here I was
very kindly treated for two months, when I
was sent home and attended there for three
months more.”
And did they cure you 7”
“ They cured the fever, but they did not
make me a healthy man I was like the
shattered wreck of a man, and in a terrible
state with a complication of disorders I have
never heard of in any other case Altogether
I had five doctors, and I am sure 1 must
have drunk buckets of the medicines they
ordered, and finally they gave me up as
hopeless and incurable. ”
“ What jvere your symptoms ?”
“I was crippled and bent up. I could
not stand straight, prostrate with weakness
and without the strength of a little child.
My eyesight became so weak I feared I was
going blind. My hearing was affected to
deafness. I had cold sweats and fainting
fits, and my digestion seemed ruined. Most
foods 1 dared not touch And this was not
the worst.”
“ Not the worst, Mr. Jones ; it appears
you were as bad as a man could be.”
“ So you might think, but my most serious
symptom, and what alarmed tlie doctors the
most, was violent palpitation of the heart.”
“ And did they do nothing for you ? ”
“No ; they were very kind, and I am
suie they did their best, but after giving me
all kinds of drugs they gave me up as
incurable, and finally they advised my return
to New South Wales, saying the voyage
might do me good. But, privately, a doctor
warned 'my wife, who nursed me, that my
heart was dangerously affected, that sho
must prepare herself for the worst, for it was
unlikely I should reach Sydney alive. And
although at the time this warning was kept
from me, I had little hope for myself. I
remembered having heard that heart disease
in a dangerous form is the frequent legacy of
rheumatic fever, and I had lost all hope, was
utterly prostrate and wearied out with my
long sickness, and felt as near to ‘ slipping
my cable ’ as a man can do and still lire.”
“ You did not die on the voyage, how-
ever ? ”
“.No ; I struggled through and managed
to get back to Sydney more dead than alive,
but there was no substantial improvement.
I seemed to be lingering, and a hopeless
invalid.”
“And was it then you took Clements
Tonic ? ”
“ Yes ; my sister, Mrs Hapgood, came to
me and advised me very strongly to try it;
but I had no faith in medicines, as I have
told you I had taken so much physic in
Western Australia without benefit that I
had come to hate the sight of it. But my
sister was resolute and confident, and her-
self brought me a bottle of Clements Tonic.”
“ And how did it affect you ? ”
“ I began to mend at once in a wonderful
way. My appetite improved, tho deafness
and dimness of sight began to leave me, my
colt- sweats were less frequent, and, most
impoi antof all, the violentheartpaipitations
were diminished. Some of my old vigour
returned, and I began to put on flesh, and I
wanted this badly enough, for I was little
more than a skeleton, weighing only seven
stone, a stooping cripple of skin and bone,
and I may hero say that after taking
Clements Tonic for two months, my weight
had increased to nine stone. So that you
can form an idea of what a rapid and almost
miraculous change took place in my con-
dition. One by one all my distressing
symptoms passed away, my health was
restored, and finally I became so st rong that
I was able to go back to my trade. ’’ <-
“ How many bottles did you take 1 ”
I took seven or eight of the large bottles
altogether, and they cured me as you see me
now.”
“ And you are sure it was Clements Tonic
that caused your wonderful recovery ? ”
“I am sure. What proof can be plainer
than my case, and as I have never read in
all your testimonials one ihat resembled my
own, I feel it is only common gratitude and
justice to give you one so that others
affected like myself may obtain a like
relief, and you have my free consent to
publish this in any way you may deem
best.”

W. T. Stead says in his Review that all
the generals who fought at Waterloo were under
50 years of age. Now Blucher, who played a con.
siderable part in that hard pounding match, was
73, and was in the saddle for 16 hours on the
memorable day.;
N.Z. Loan and Mercantile Agency Co., Ltd.,
reports for week ended May 12th that the marsupial
season is now in full blast with plenty of choice skins giv-
ing promise of a good time at Wednesday’s sale. On May
2nd (excluding possums) out of 57,000 skins offered by
the various brokers, 19,000—about s—were$ —were on the Co.’s
catalogue—good evidence in favor of the N.Z.L. & M.A.
Co.’s claim to be the leading fur skin brokers of the local
market. Hides and sheepskins were weaker. Queensland
butcher’s and factory and N.S.W. sloppy hides fell by £d.
while N.S.W. lots of good quality also came down by Jd.
with a tendency to drop still lower. Full-wools and faulty
lots are fully id. down, while half-growths, though not
down, are not in such strong request as during the last few
weeks. Next auction may find an improvement, but,
meanwhile, the market is decidedly irregular. Hair also
declined fully id. to Jd. per lb. Bones and horns had fair
competition and glue stocks in good demand and remain-
ing firm. The tallow market was also irregular but no
serious decline eventuated, as prime sorts were bought tin
freely at late rates although medium sorts were hardly so
well supported.*

The new Melb. Opera House is not to be constructed in
an expensive huriy like the Sydney Tivoli. In the re-
building of the Tiv. much time was saved, but the same
elegant theatre might have been completed a few months
later for two or three thousand pounds less money. The
Opera House will not be out of the architect’s hands until
next Easter, at the earliest.
From Adelaide : Williamson’s new prima-donna, Miss
Ada Weir, has a soprano voice of fine quality, and acts
well ; but she is not a Nellie Stewart, and will never cap-
tivate Johnnydom as a beauty. But she can sing.
A queer old character died in the family circle of Melb.
Bijou during the performance one night last week. He
was 70 years of age, and known to all the hands about the
house for the reason that for six months past he had
attended the theatre every night, being always in the
first rush for seats. He appeared very poor, and the 6s. a
week must have been a severe drain on his resources.
The employes thought him mad, but perhaps they are
unnecessarily severe on the performers.
Actor-Manager Rickards is very proud of his new Tivoli
(Sydney) Theatre. “ When a man builds a theatre,”
quoth he to Roderic Quinn the other day, “ he is erecting
a real, fine monument to himself.” “ Yes,” replied Quinn,
“ let there be written up * Sacred to the mummery of H.
Rickards.’ ”
Sarah Bernhardt, when in Sydney some years ago, was
much impressed by Dan O’Oonnor’s eloquence and his
fine manly figure, to say nothing of his long-lashed eyes
and that beard, and was deeply chagrined when she heard
that Daniel had lost his seat in Parliament. “ Monsieur
O’Connor,” said the divine Sarah, “ ver" magnifique 1
Not in Parlemong ? Oh ! ze ingrates ! ” So the first
thing Dan did when he was elected the other day for
Phillip Division was to send Sarah a fine panel photo, of
his physog. with “considerations of his most distin-
guished regard.”
If Sydney musicians, without protest, permit City
Organist Wiegand to he bullied out of Australia by the
City Council, they should be thoroughly ashamed of them-
selves. Wiegand is an artist; his art does honor to the
country where he lives; and his stipend is below his
deserts instead of above it. Even from a commercial
Soint of view the mean-spirited blockheads of the City
ouncil have stultified themselves —if that were possible.
But The Bulletin does not look to the Sydney organists
to avenge or to aid Wiegand. Most of them thirst for his
billet, which they have the nerve to believe they could
effectively fill, and hardly one of them has a word to say
in his favor.
Manager George Sederer claims to have created Edna
May’s charming “Belle of New York” performance.
Says that “ he measured the eight steps for every line in
* Follow On ’; showed her when to raise her eyelids and
lower them ; taught her how to stand still, how to wave
her arms, how to smile,” &c. Probably there Is a good
deal of truth in the ’Murkan man’s yarn. Louise Hepner,
who played the Belle in Australia, got a careful drilling
in the airs and graces of stage modesty from the stage
manager. He directed the raising and dropping of her
eyelids, and educated her in the art of looking too good
for this world. Louise didn’t represent a real, tough
“ Hallelujah Lassie,” but she was a fairly sweet thing in
frauds by the time her tutor had finished his heavy task.
The public didn’t think that Louise would soon after-
wards figure as the giddy respondent in a divorce-suit.
Lillah M‘Carthy, the very fine nun-like type, who was
here with Wilson Barrett, and has since been adopted as
his leading lady, was discovered by Bernaid Shaw in May,
’95, playing Lady Macbeth with a London amateur party.
The lively B.S.—whilst explaining the danger of praising
amateurs, for the reason that he rarely did so without
receiving a letter next day to the effect that the appreci-
ated one, encouraged by the praise of so exalted a critic,
was throwing up his billet as manager of the Bank of
England to seek fame and fortune on the professional
boards—did not hesitate to prediot a rapid run up for the
soulful Lillah. She has managed to get a front shop in
five years, and Shaw is justified.
Miss Alice Crawford, the recitress, is living in Melbourne
Just now, under the wing of gentle Mrs. Doubleday,
authoress of the Amy Castles boom. It is understood
that Miss Crawford has made up her mind to go on the
stage if she can get a promising engagement, and she
ought not to have to wait long for an offer. All the
makings of an actress seem to be in this handsome
Bendigo girl.
A recent London play is called “ Bush Life ’’—scene laid
In Australia. The hero is a boundary-rider, and he leaves
a bag of 400 sovs. in a cupboard, where the villain tries to
burgle it. Australian boundary-riders are so careless with
their bags of capital.
George Musgrove, it is now almost certain, will open at
Melb. Princess’s in September with English opera, for
which he has already engaged a double co., says The
Bulletin’s informant. Brough will have the theatre for
the few weeks that come between the close of Charles
Arnold’s season and the arrival of Musgrove.
Last time Harry Rickards was in London he met
Charles Godfrey unexpectedly, and the poor broken-down
singer had the gall to ask him for another Australian
engagement, which was a little too much for the mag-
nanimity of the prosperous man, considering that his old
pal Charlie had “turned dog” on him in return for many
favouts. Godfrey, what time he was singing for Rickards
at a big salary in Australia, took sundry oaths that he
would sober-up, and went off upon a wilder spree after
each vow. Again, when he had completed his engage-
ment and gone touring “ on his own,” Godfrey wired from
Tasmania that he was stony broke, and asked for
assistance. Rickards sent him £5O and the offer of
another 12 weeks’ engagement at £5O a week to give him
a chance of getting back to England with money in his
pocket. But when Godfrey did eventually get back, he,
with all the ingratitude of a dipsomaniac, villifled his
friend, as it turned out. Nevertheless the news of poor
old Charlie’s death reminded Rickards that Mrs. Godfrey
lay in a neglected grave at Melb. Cemetery, so he gave
an order for a headstone to mark the place. The foolish
couple left a hopeful son, who is said to have ’listed for
service in South Africa.

“ W.F.H.”: Went t’other evening to a complimentary
banquet tended to a successful brass band. Up till 12

Dear Bulletin.— As the short story has supplied such
an obvious want in Uterature—why not the short drama?
Present writer has been having a course of “ Leah, ’
“ Henry V.,” “Youth,” &c., none of which plays he has
been able to sit out. An hour-and-a-half is about his
limit of interest in any show ; a limit which Rickards fits
by placing the “ turns ” so that one may pick one’s hour
or so. No harm either in boiling any of the plays named
down to one-half. Not one of ’em but would be stronger
and suppler—forcing the best that is in them out of the
actors ; who, instead of droning and prosing along, would
have to rush their points and centre their climaxes as the
latter-day short story writer does. Out. cut, cut 1 —
especially the preaching—and give us acting. Put on in
one night, say, “ Youth ” and “ Leah,” at li hours each.
Then the people who want a lot for their money could
stay to the bitter end, while those who only care about
the writer’s dose of drama could make their choice of the
two plays. This is where the variety stage has got so
far ahead of the “ legitimate.”— Jack S.
“Terp.” : In an old mag., t’other day, I stumbled on a
reference to the premature ending of an operatic perform-
ance in America, owing to our W. S. Lyster having eloped
with Georgia Hodson, one of the principal singers.
When a certain theatrical co. arrived in Bendigo (Vic.),
they went in a body to a local photographer, who took
about 40 negatives of the various members of the co., but,
owing to bad weather, he could not furnish proofs before
k the co. left for Oastlemaine. To Castlemaine, therefore,
he posted finished and mounted prints, for approval, and
received a letter, in reply, stating that the members of
the co. were simply delighted with the pictures—but
giving no orders. And, when the photo.-man had written
again, requesting that such would be desirable, he was
paralysed to receive, next day, a script—“ Admit two to
the stalls ! ”
Renee-Lees, writing from London to Herr Kretschmann,
her old Sydney instructor, says : “ Liebling was delighted
and surprised at my playing. He says my wrist-action is
without reproach, and he would not alter my style at all.
He wishes me to play- at a concert which he is arranging
in Berlin.”

May 16th. and 21st. Included will be selections from
Ooleridge-Taylor and Brahms.

‘Ho ro mo nigheau donn bhoidbeaeh.’ ” The Bulletin

“S.S.”: Man’s appreciation of leg-shows is a weakness
to which chestnuts cling like mussels to a pile, but, as a
matter of fact, man’s delight in the unfrocked feminine
calf is as nothing to the noisy joy of women when the
hard, angular knees of man are exposed in a public place.
There has been a tremendous epidemic of kilted Scotch-
men in Melb. drama since the opening of the war, and
woman’s hysterical delight in razor-back shinbone is
largely responsible for the success that has attended each
production. Man surveys the procession of ballet legs
with dignified composure, woman squeals noisily and con-
tinuously, enraptured and unashamed. It is the same
when Seel capers in calico pantlets as an in-kneed ballet-
girl at the Bijou. Only women are really amused, and
they all squeal in the same key.
“ S.” : Spencer-street railway-station provides a painful
spectacle whenever a theatrical co. with a profuse leg-
element is leaving. the city. Pallid actresses, looking
hastily dressed, paint-stained, and elderly, all over the
place, followed by numerous Johnnies, all obviously
parading their best suits with the object of advertising
their acquaintance with Florrie, Flossie, and Flo. Actor
chaps are numerous, too, usually looking like caricatures
of themselves, amazingly dressed and outr6 in the matter
of hair. .They talk very loudly, with an eye to an advt.,
and their postures are suggestive of the flaming red
poster ;.but it is when they are bidding their .professional
friends in petticoats a fond farewell that they get in thei»
best work. What a gathering-up of hands, what ai
ardour of compression and expression 1 Here come th»
small, half-pint journalists, the painful fabricators of fly
spot paragraphs about the comings and goings of the
angelic Tottie and the god-like genius of the pro., who is
affectionately remembered for his marvellous impersona-
tion of a carrot in the pantomime of 1844. They mix to-
gether, and shake, and cuddle, and kiss no end ; but the
pick of the bundle is the harrowed and desolated Johnnie,
after the last scramble at the carriage-window, the tear-
ing of bouquets, and the damp farewell, stalking away
with a wet, dead face into an empty world. He is Sub-
lime 1
The best comic turn yet seen in a circus in the
backblocks lately happened in the Ovens district, Vic.
The clown was taking well and giving pleasure to all,
especially with his jibes at the expense of a grave-looking,
profusely-whiskered bushman on the front shilling seat.
Presently, however, Whiskers got weary, and he rose up,
and lurched into the ring, and smote the clown heavily
on the chin. Whiskers was very drunk, but he kept his
end up with great judgment when the clown tried to hit
back, and a thrillingly-realistic battle ensued amongst the
sawdust to the enormous delight of the audience. The
professional humorist was getting beans by the plateful,
and meanwhile an agitated consultation was being held
in the dressing-room, ending in the full strength of the
co. rushing the ring and towing the combatants bodily
away into the second tent. Then the audience arose and
howled ; its yells of delight nearly stampeded the horses,
and when the ringmaster ventured into the arena to
explain, the patrons simply encored him off the earth.
Being refused an encore, the people started to break
chairs, and do other expensive foolishness, and it was
fully 20min. before the manager was allowed to set forth
that the item could not be repeated because Whiskers
was in the lock-up and the constable refused to release
him, and the clown was laid up with a pound of steak and
two crippled ribs.
11 F.” : A foolish sermon on the subject of Australian
actors abroad appeared in a Sydney paper lately. The
writer, “ H.T.,” who evidently holds a brief for local
talent versus imported ditto, complained that “ the Aus-
tralian player has to travel to prove his or her quality.
Witness Mr. H. R. Roberts, one of the jolliest of light
comedians, and a sterling actor withal. Witness also
players who were refused engagement here, but have
succeeded in broader and more critical parts of the thea-
trical world—for example, Miss Ethel Haydon. who has
become a London ‘ star,’ and Mr. Edmond Sayers, a come-
dian who had to work without payment here little more
than a year ago, but is now a prominent and appreciated
player in Manchester. Witness Miss Florence Esdaile,
who was not allowed to rise out of the chorus here, but is
a popular soloist in London and the big provincial cities.
Now, what is “ H.T.” giving us, anyhow? Edmond
Sayers, late of Australia and now of Manchester, is a
novice I never heard of till “H.T.” mentioned him, but
concerning the other people I can speak from memory.
H. R. Roberts, as the leading man of Maggie Moore’s co.,
was duly appreciated here in drama, burlesque, and panto-
mime. We rated him at his full value, or thereabouts,
and thus far there is ho news of his making a bigger
mark abroad. Manager Williamson, for obvious reasons,
didn’t jump at the chance of engaging Roberts what time
the young man was not acting with Maggie Moore, but
Brough tried him in comedy, and he was a fair success-
nothing remarkable. The fact that “ H.T.” has forgotten
Roberts’ connection with the Brough co. speaks for itself.
Miss Ethel Haydon got a show in a pantomime at the
Princess’s and in comedy at the Bijou, and the reason she
didn’t get “star” parts was that Nellie Stewart and
Violet Varley were available when she (Ethel H.) took to
the professional stage. The starring of Miss Haydon in
London was limited to one musical comedy. “ H.T.” is
very much at fault in his statement that Florrie Esdaile
was not allowed to rise out of the chorus. Long previous
to her chorus singing (in Italian opera) at the Princess’s
she had played comic-opera parts. Nellie Stewart, if I
remember rightly, gave Miss Esdaile a show during her
period of management—the “ Paul Jones ” period.
Also, it will surprise “ H.T.” to hear that
Miss Esdaile was a popular soloist at Rickards’s
for some months ere she went to London to try her
luck. If she is a more popular and higher salaried
soloist there than she was here it is because good soprano
voices are comparatively scarce in London, whereas they
are plentiful in Australia. Singers must be exceptionally
fine (or famous) to earn big fees in a land of song. Florrie
Esdaile did well to go to England ; she would probably
have done badly in Italy. And now to mention three
Australian favorites who were not required to “go
abroad to prove their quality.” Miss Stewart was “ Our
Nellie ” before she went to London the first time. She
returned without having made an appearance in the Big
Smoke, and took her place again as first favorite. Her
next visit to London resulted in an engagement which
brought her no glory ; she returned to Australia and was
more “ our Nellie” than ever. She had paid two unsuc-
cessful trips ’Ome before she got her engagement at
Drury Lane. Pattie Browne was the case of a popular
Australian actress who managed to get a good footing on
the London stage. When she lately visited her native
country in a “ star” capacity, her native country didn’t
enthuse over the lady. And Essie Jenyns was the case of
a young Australian actress who took this country by
storm and made a fortune for the management, and re-
tired into private life (as Mrs. Woods) without having
tried to prove her worth in any other land.
A Ministerial rule adopted by all N.S.W. Cabi-
nets empowers Ministers absent in the country on
§ublic duty to charge extra allowances for every
ay they are out of Sydney. Then allowances run
from two to three guineas a day. Parliament has
never been invited to pass an opinion on the
matter, and indeed nine-tenths of the members
don’t know that the practice exists. These extra
payments never go directly on the Estimates;
combined with other charges they escape observa-
tion under some such heading as “ Incidentals.”
The Minister who draws the extra money is the
sole judge as to what is public duty and how long
he is to spend over it. There was a time in N.S. W.
history when a Premier told certain of his col-
leagues who played the praotice for “ all that was
in it,” that their conduot was soandalous and if
discovered and brought before the House would
be publicly condemned by him. But it wasn’t
discovered —or the few members who knew of it
hoped to be equally fortunate Ministers them-
selves some day soon. The head of the Lyne ad-
ministration is sticking to his “last” pretty closely
but there are indications that the gay old gadabout
spirit flourishes in the Cabinet. Far-away districts
involving long absences from Sydney are charged
for Ministerial visits and the extra pay allowance
bell will be kept in perpetual tinkle-tinkle. To
keep the heart of the House soft, Ministers pro-
pose to carry members by the dozen and score
with them. It should be insisted on, that the
money thus used is the public taxes, and Parlia-
ment should demand that all extra payments
should appear on the Estimates opposite the name
of the Minister who draws the cash.

“ Steve ” to The Bulletin :—Re the proposed
demand for a scaffolding and machinery inspector
for Sydney, S.M. Herald writes of “ the neces-
sity which is said to exist for the appointment.”
“ Said to exist! ” It’s a matter of the worker as
against the employer—therefore, of course, un-
willing old Granny feels bound to pooh-pooh the
whole thing, as far as she dares in the lace of her
own everyday reportings. But that the necessity
is a very urgent one no practical man doubts, and
the local Trades Council should ask, not for a mere
stop-gap inspector, but for a short Act of Parlia-
ment, providing for the compulsory registration of
all proprietors of machinery or scaffolding, and
for the periodical inspection of their plant. Also,
appointing an inspector, who shall have power to
condemn and destroy all faulty or insecure scaf-
folds or machines, and whoso duty it will be to
examine into and report upon all scaffold-acci-
dents, no matter how trifling the results. Pro-
vision to be made for debarring any person from
altering or interfering with the traces of any such
accident, previous to official examination, and
responsibility for the safety of scaffolds and
maohines to be fixed absolutely upon the owners
thereof.
Tasmanian Anglican Synod seems by late wires
to have set rolling for Australia the disputatious
stone of the validity of prayers for the dead.
Primate Smith, of N.S.W., dislikes doctrinal
controversy, but several of his clergy say he must
take hand in this whether he likes or no. The
English law does not forbid prayers for the dead
in Anglican churches, but the Bishops as a general
rule do, or if they allow prayers, they whittle
away their significance. For instance, the Arch-
bishop of Canterbury, under loyal pressure, pre-
scribed a prayer lately for British soldiers who die
in the Boer war, and, on being pulled up, pleaded,
first, that the civil law was not against the prac-
tice, and, second, that “The prayer prescribed for
the dead is not for all the dead, but only for the
faithful. To the faithful,” his Grace goes on,
“ the Lord has promised entrance into the King-
dom of Heaven, and just as we pray for that
Kingdom to come, knowing that it certainly will
come, so we may pray that the faithful departed
may be admitted there, knowing that they cer-
tainly will.” If Primate Smith can say yes-no in
finer style than that his “ cautious ” clergy will be
proud of him.
“Jack Shat ” on Sydney municipal awfulness :
The commercial and industrial interests of Sydney are
beginning to discover that they have too long been a
crowd of landlord-ridden helots, and have for years been
paying rates for services the Council has failed to render
—as, for instance, the removal of garbage and refuse.
The Council holds that it needs only remove street and
household refuse, leaving the shops and factories to pay
private contractors to remove their refuse, yet these
people pay city-rates based on high rentals (in some cases,
In the principal thoroughfares, reaching as high as £lO
and £l5 per week). And this, by the way, is one of the
financial fakes by which our slum-owning Council has
managed to keep the rates on its own property low
—thus penalising the large tenant-taxpayers for the bene-
fit of the slums and rookeries. Also if the large business-
people of Sydney don't look to it, they will, with the
advent of the destructors, have a fresh plant sprung upon
them, as the Council is contracting for the removal and
destruction of street and household refuse only. So that,
messieurs the shop-keepers, you will have a by-law in
force compelling all refuse to he destroyed, and will thus,
in addition to having to pay twice for refuse-removal,
have to pay another tribute to the destructor-company to
have your refuse burnt. And our precious Council will
thus still manage to keep the rates low on its own and its
friends’ slums and rookeries.
An English jury recently gave a Mrs.
Gray £25 damages on the ground that Mrs. Clark
had written to Mr. Gray alleging that Mrs. Gray
unduly winked at and mashed Mr. Clark. Among
other matters it transpired that Mrs. Clark had
been wildly jealous of Mrs. Gray for a long time,
and at last grew so bad that the crushed Mr.
Clark signed this dooument at his wife’s dicta-
tion :
I faithfully promise, on my solemn oath before
Almighty God, that I will not look after Gray’s wife oppo-
site, either at the window or in the street or yard, or at
the brewery doors or windows, or when standing in the
brewery. And I further undertake in no way to have
any communications or signs between myself and the
aforesaid woman, so help me God. While Eva is away I
also promise to answer truthfully my wife’s questions on
this subject three times daily.
This was sworn to before a J.P. Fortunately the
average J.P. is a serious and humorless man else
he would burst with inward merriment at some of
the documents brought before him. When this
scribe was a much younger and more irresponsible
being he had a billet once in the office of a
religious and serious J.P. A really nice girl
brought her young man there one day. She was
in deadly earnest and he was embarrassed
and sheepish. She explained that they were
engaged, but she was afraid he was of frivolous
life and conversation, and that he had doubts
about the literal truth of the Scriptures. And she
couldn’t marry a man who had doubts about the
literal truth of the Soriptures, so as the wedding
was fixed for next day (she said this with the light
of a great, deep love shining in her brown eyes) he
had agreed to oome before a J.P. and swear that
he believed everything between the two boards of
the holy volume. And he swore manfully, and
they went away quite happy. Then the J.P.,
who had a streak of humor under his seriousness,
looked at the writer, and the writer looked at
him, and the J.P. said, “Poor girl!’’and then
“Damn !” and then he said “Oh, Lord !” and
fell under his desk in convulsions. And when he
came out from under the desk he remembered that
he had disgraced himself by saying “Damn!”
and for the rest of the day he made such a row
about a “ t ” that wasn’t crossed in the ledger that
the writer nearly resigned his billet on the spot.
The Man Who Didn’t Pear.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He was brave as any hero,
That in drama ever played;
But his courage sank to zero
When the “ Plague ” in Sydney stayed.
Then his face but seldom brightened,
And his tone became severe ;
Still, he said he wasn’t frightened,
And the “ Plague ” he didn’t fear.
When they said “ Inoculation,”
He remarked he would not mind
Taking on that operation
For he felt that way Inclined ;
Not because it might prevent it—
That was never his idea—
And he spoke as though he meant it,
When he said he didn’t fear.
Now and then he’d try to cheer up,
And to show no signs of fright,
Yet took Someone’s “Patent Syrup"
’Cause “ his liver wasn’t right” ;
For he said he thought a tonic
Would his system nicely clear ;
Not because of aught “ Bubonic ”
For the “ Plague ” he didn’t fear.
Any stories he would swallow
That might chance to come his way,
And the press-reports would follow
Very closely every day.
He would change just like our weather,
And to both extremes would veer—
Not the sajue two days together—
Still the “ Plague ” he didn’t fear.
He had liquid disinfectant
Sprinkled on the office floor ;
Then like “ Oliver,” expectant,
He would call aloud for “ more."
Next with health ideas he trifled,
And with sulphur fumes severe,
He the office nearly stifled,
Yet the “ Plague ” he didn’t fear.
Then the “ Fresh Air” theme was mentioned,
So he opened windows wide,
And with thoughts the best intentioned
Let cold air and dust Inside.
Then the weather growing colder
He abandoned that idea,
And remarked In tones much bolder
That the “ Plague ” he didn’t fear.
Then he heard from “ Health Board’s” tracing
That the rats were all to blame,
So poor mice he started chasing—
(lie was feeling rather game).
From dead rats he kept his distance,
Though the looker-on might sneer ;
Yet he stated with persistence
That the “ Plague ” he didn’t fear.
« • • •
Years have passed ; the “ Plague ” is banished,
And the town from scare is free ;
But this man long since has vanished,
And in fancy this we see :
In the far Pacific Ocean,
On a lonely island drear,
Lives a man with but one notion—
That “ The Plague he doesn't fear."
G H. P.
