Reparation.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ He’s dead,” said the Wi ' —the widow —to the
other woman. She spoke dm-y, standing motion-
less. The other woman looked at her uncompre-
hendingly.
“ Dead ! He !” she said.
She stared round the room—his study—and sank
into a chair, repeating “ Dead ! ”
“ Yes,” said the wife listlessly.
“ When ?” asked the other woman.
“Last night,” said the wife. “You knew we
expected it, didn’t you ?”
“ Yes,” the other replied. “ But I can’t realise
it. It’s —it’s so impossible.”
“It has happened, however,” said the wife. She
leant against the mantelpiece, looking into the
fire. In her light eyes tears welled up and over-
flowed. She wiped them away with a wet hand-
kerchief.
“Can I do anything for you?” asked the other
woman, with a movement of pity. Her own eyes
were-dry ; from her strong face all color had faded,
leaving the scarlet of her lips and heavy darkness
of hair and brow and eyes intensified by its pallor..
The wife shook her head. ‘ ‘ Thank you ;my
brother is with me,’’she said. “There is nothing.”
There was a silence, and then the wife spoke
again. “It will be hard for you, too, you who
have worked for him so long. You will, I suppose,
try to get another position as secretary ?”
“I—suppose so,” assented the other woman,
with forced steadiness.
“Now that I think of it, there is ono thing I
must ask of you,” the wife went on. “Will you
help me to arrange his papers and books ? You
know which manuscripts are finished and which
unfinished and—and all that ; don’t you ? Some
of his work can be printed at once.” Something
of animation came into her listless voice.
The other woman looked at her enquiringly.
“ He has left me all his books and papers to treat
exactly as I judge best,” the wife added, a
faint triumph in her tones. “Yes, he told me
he had done that. He said it was reparation.
Reparation ! for being such a good, patient, kind .
Eiusband, I suppose ! ”
“ Reparation ! ” repeated the other woman. She
drew her breath in sharply with the word, and for
a moment the calm of her face seemed breaking ;
but she mastered herself and added in even tones ;
“ If I am to do anything for you it fhust be now.
I —l have arranged to go away to Melbourne.”
“Now?” said the wife, with a kind of norvous-
ness. Then, with an effort, “ Yes — now, if you
will.”
“ It will not take long,” said the other woman.
“ Everything is arranged. It was the last thing I
did before ” She stopped and stood up. Beside
her tall deep-breasted body the wife’s meagre
figure looked like a sickly child’s.
“You need not trouble about the book-shelves,”
said the wife. “Anyone could catalogue the
books. Poor things, they will have to be sold ! ”
She gave a sentimental sigh. The other woman
glanced along the rows of those well-beloved, well-
used friends and shivered. She said nothing, but
began taking papers out of the pigeon-holes and
drawers in the escritoire and laying them in separ-
ate piles upon the table.
“There will bo rough notes,” she explained.
“Rough notes ; some short essays that he might
have altered later ; a number of fragments—things
he was adding to from time to time ; and two or
three big works completed, or almost completed. ,
These two ” (she laid her hand on two piles of
manuscript) “could be called complete, so far. as
they go. This ” (she drew forth a bundle from the
bottom of a drawer) “is an older thing.’ It’s com-
plete, but he was keeping it back for a time.
Some of the material—that which is incomplete,
or in the rough, will need editing. Possibly ” (her
voice trembled for the first time), “possibly you
will find that I may be as suitable an editor as you
can get. You could then ”
She stopped, interrupted by an inarticulate cry
from the wife, who had been glancing at the three
packages which represented the finished works.
At sight of the title-page of one of these came the
gasping cry ; and the other woman looked up with
a sudden fear.
“ This,” said the wife, leaning her clenched hand
on the manuscript, “this is the translation from
that man—that horror—who attacked Christianity;
morality, decency ” She stopped, and the
other woman moved round and faced her. “Oh !
how glad I am that no one has seen it but me !
How glad,” the wife went on, and she caught it up
in her hands, “for now no one else shall see it
She moved with a feverish impulse to-
wards the fireplace. The other woman moved
step for step with her. “No one else, no one
else ! ” the wife repeated, in her thin voice. She
stooped to lay the packet in the fire. But the
other woman caught back her hand.
“ Stop,” she said.
The wife stood upright, and the blood rushed
over her face.
“Forgive me,” said the other woman, quietly ;
“Forgive me for interfering. If you will listen a
moment” , , ,
“What do you mean?” interrupted the wife,
haughtily. , . , , , „
“You will thank me for this by-and-by,” said
the other woman. “If you consider a little you
will not destroy it.”
I shall not consider ! I shall destroy it—now.
God’s punishment would descend upon me if I did
not. ft is a blasphemous book. It shall burn.”
She moved once more towards the fire. The other
woman, with a strong movement, took the packet
from her. ... ,
“ Listen,” she said, her passion rising up, and
once for all breaking the stiff restraint of her
manner. , ,
“ How dare you ! ” • the wife began.
“ Listen,” said the other, sternly. “ This is
your husband’s work ; there is work of his own,
original work, in this packet, to be incorporated in
the volume with the translation. It is ’ her voice
trembled—“ it is the finest thing he has ever done;
into it he put more of his brain, more of his .soul,
than into anything else he ever did. Whatever
your opinions are, however this clashes with them,
you would not destroy the outcome of a life of in-
tellectual work, you would not deprive the world
of it—even if it were the work of a stranger, you
could not. But when it is your husband’s ! ”
“ More than ever because it is his ! ” buret out
the wife! “ I will not add the sin of publishing it
to the world to his sin in writing it. To let people
be contaminated, ruined by it ! And to bear the
scandal, the shame of seeing his name— my name
—upon it! No; never, never. Give it to me—
and go away, please. Go quietly away.”
“ You don’t understand,” said the other woman,
fighting to keep some control over herself, to ex-
plain. “ This is great—it’s a great’piece of litera-
ture. You have no right—no one has the right—
to destroy such a thing. Publish it anonymously,
if you are afraid. Do not set your puny standards
of the right and the wrong against the magnifi-
cent brain and spirit of your husband; they .are
here—here in this little packet—that brain and
spirit; and you would' destroy them ? . Think !
You cannot. Oh, why, why did he-— rt her voice
broke and she stopped.
“ Why did he what?” cried the wife, furiously.
How dare you talk to me like this ? Go, go away
at once — -at once; do you hear? Before I call my
brother. Before I ”
“I will not,” said the othfer woman. The self-
control was altogether gone now. A passionate
fire burnt in the deep eyes. “ I will not—unless
you promise not to destroy tliis ”
The wife stamped her foot, and cried again,
“Go-go.”
“No,”said the other woman. “ And —no, you
shall not call anyone. If you" do, you shall be
made to hear the truth —before all the world. Ah,
it will be a scandal the world will enjoy ! ”
“What do you mean, you—you wicked crea-
ture?” gasped the wife, cowed by the deep voice,
the blazing eyes. . -
“Never mind what I mean,” said the other
woman, trying once more to restrain herself.
“ Give me your word that this book shall not be
destroyed, and I will go away and never trouble
you again!”
“ I will not promise,” said the wife sullenly. “ I
shall do as I please with the papers. It is my
business, not yours.”
“You are mad, to provoke me so,” said the
other woman. She suddenly took the wife by the
shoulders and shook her to and fro.
“ Don’t you know—don’t you understand ? It is
my business ; mine, mine ; for he and I were one,
were united as you could never conceive of union.
This is mine as well as his. The life-blood of one
of us was the life-blood of the other. Don’t you
understand? —no, poor' thing, how could you?
What can you know of lpve like ours ? Oh, my
love ! my dear dead king ! how could you do this
tiling ? How could you leave these children of
ours to be maimed, distorted, destroyed even !
Reparation! Yes, conscience always pricks you
a little; not mine, not mine. Why should it? I
took nothing that was another’s-wnot yours, poor
thing,” as her eyes fell on the wife. “Our love
hurt no one, and added the deepest joy, the mo3t
glorious completeness to two lives. But your con-
science pricked, my king, and you have made
reparation. Yes, but a reparation that tortures
me, kills me. Have I deserved it," my dear, dead
love?” j
She stopped and leaned her h,ead upon the
manuscript as a stprm of sobs shook her.
“ If only I wera. dead too !” she rhoanod.
The wife gathered herself together and spoke in
a hard, thin voice.
“ Now that you have soiled the memory of the
dead and shown your own foulness, you will go,
perhaps,” she said. “I am not friglitened of your
threats to expose yourself for the sake of— that.”
She pointed to the manuscript.
The other woman raised her head and dried her
teas. She smiled. “You think not?” she said.
“ You are wrong. What do I care how much
people know? You oare, but I don’t.” She rose
and pulled her heavy veil down over her face.
“ Now take your choice,” she said. She took up
the manuscript. “ Either you let me walk out of
the house with this, or you call someone to take it
from me, and to hear my story. I have proofs,
by the way—letters, you know.”
The wife shuddered. The other woman laughed
softly. “ Ah, you are a coward,” she said gently;
and walked slowly towards the door. The wife
stood silent,, watching her. Once she made a
desperate movement as if to clutch at the other;
but the soft voice repeated, “Letters lovej-
letters,” and the wife’s hand fell nerveless. At
the door the other woman stopped. A sudden rej-
vulsion of feeling came—she looked on the dreary
figure within the room. A great pity welled uii>
in her, and she came back and stood before thd
wife.
“I am sorry—infinitely sorry,” she said. “I
should not have told you. I tried—tried hard to
fight it down, but you tortured me, you roused me,
till it was impossible to bear. My passions are
strong and I—l am so weak. You cannot forgive
me—oh, I know that, Ido not ask that; but if,
some day, you should feel that you could under-
stand the love and misery and longing and adora-
tion that made me defend this—if you should ever
feel that, would you tell me ? ”
The wife moved no muscle of her face in reply.
The light eyes stared forth void of expression.
The thin lips kept bitterly closed. The only
movement she made was to draw her black skirt
away from contact with the other woman’s black
skirt.
The other woman turned and walked firmly out;
bearing the manuscript with her.

It behoves the Australian citizen hence-
forth to be most inquisitive and particular on the
subject of Eiis omelettes. The noble restaurateur
isn’t likely to use new-laid eggs at anything up to
half-a-crown a dozen while he can buy the Chinese-
grown article at one-seventh of the price. “Chow-
Chow ” writes to The Bulletin :
As a general rule when the wholesale price of eggs -in
Australia gets down to 6d. or 7d. a dozen the hen goes on
strike—on the ground, no doubt, that such a figure will
nob remunerate any self : respecting Australian fowl for her
time and trouble. And the law of supply and demand
soon adjusts matters. But in China fowls are apparently
bitten by the genius loci of Cheapness, and, fed on things
unmentionable to ears polite, they produce: eggs that can
be landed in Australia at about 3d. per dozen! Last year
5000 dozen of these eggs *were imported jto Sydney, and
striking the market in the off-season of the local product,
they sold easily at from 9d. td Is. per dozen.
This season it is anticipated that the importations will
reach 100,000 doz. These eggs, which are much smaller
than the Australian variety, are apparently preserved in
silicate of soda, and though the shells are exceedingly
dark in color, no fault is to be found with the appearance
or flavor'of the “meat.” However, in view of the
nauseous mysteries of Chinese poultry-farming, and the
fact that bacteriologists have recently discovered noxious
bacilli in egg-yolk, it will be well for the Australian health
authorities to keep a watchful eye on the matter. The
drastic cheapening of the egg will probably mean a great
blow to the Australian poultry industry; arid, as
the inability of the white worker to compete with
the jaw-wage yellow alien was the cause of restrictive
legislation against Chinese immigration, qur Parliaments
will doubtless soon be confronted with a demand to put-a
poll-tax'on the Chinaman’s hen-fruit. These preserved
eggs must hot be confounded with the real Chinese
“ kept ” egg which “ John ” uses himself. It is subjected
to an intricate preserving process, and, being wrapped in
rice-chucks; is buried in the ground, where it remains for
perhaps 20 or 30 years. One of the greatest delicacies at
a Mongolian’s wedding feast is a dish of these which had
been put away, especially for the occasion, at the time
when the bridegroom’s little slant eyes first looked with
innocent wonderment on the horn spectacles of the pig-
tailed accoucheur. Eggs of this antiquity, and so treated,
are as black as coal, and have a perfume that could not
fail to reach the nose of even a Sydney sanitary inspector.

“Jerome D.”. That Maoriland, scientist
who (according to a recent “Red Page”)
believes that the sun, acting on the ocean’s sur-
face, generated primal life, stands, philosophically
speaking, not alone. This idea, with modifica-
tions, has been cherished by whole races of people
such as the Aztecs, the Chinese, and the Japanese;
nor can it be demonstrated that the contention is
not scientifically sound. The Chinese theory of
the Yim and the Yeng, or the sexual principle of
creation, is in one sense on all fours with the ideas
of the M.L. scientist. A Chinese once pointed
out to me the close analogy between the human
frame and the physical world, the bones being
comparable to the mountain ridges, the flesh to
the soil, the vital fluids to the waters of the ocean
and the rivers, the hair to the vegetation, and the
eyes to the two principal celestial luminaries. [A
mere modification, this, of the macrocosm-micro-
cosm notion.] Chinese poets also find in the rain-
bow the mingling of the male and female elements
of the heavens and the earth respectively—surely
a graceful poetic conception. The poets of the
West also buttress the idea. Thus Swinburne, in
“ Hertha ” :
“ First life on my souroes,
First drifted and swain,
Out of me are the forces
That save or that damn.”
It is at least soothing to imagine that our ancestry
thus majestically extends beyond what Stevenson
calls “the blfie-oehinded ape,” and it has been well
said that the worship of the sun—the visible
source of all light and life known to us—is the
noblest and most natural form of idolatry.

Ships and Sailormen.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Ye spread a thousand shining sail
Within the harbor’s narrow rounds :
At greater seas doth courage fail ?
Why seek ye not the outer bounds ?
Ye stand on brink of nationhood
(E’en now fulfilment’s in your ken),
But ere your claim to manhood’s good,
Ye must build ships—bleed sailormen.
Trust not at all to foreign hands,
Nor e’en depend too much on kin ;
Your lives and honor—’your broad lands
Should guarded be by those within.
The day will come, for come it must,
When your sires’ ships will cross the foam,
On them and you this lesson thrust—
Man’s first clear duty lies at home.
What will ye then if men you’ve none
To guard your seas and heritage ?
What has been may again be done,
And ye be swept from history’s page—
Made vassal of some hated state :
Your freedom gone—arouse ye, then!
Rise up to greatness ! frustrate fate !
Go vessels build —breed sailormen !
Without your gates, to greet each keel
Of your own ships, in sunlit seas,
Like emeralds cast on polished steel
I«e isles fair as Hesperides. •
Negleet them not ! your trust they are,
Grow strong and, ’hind a freeman’s sword,
Warn old-world greed that reaches far,
“Hands off ! ” here we are overlord.
Your sires they sailed on seas unknown,
In galliot and caravel;
On rotten brigs their flags were flown—
Brave hearts that feared not man or Hell !
The sport of every adverse tide,
Tossed here and there by varying winds :
Have ye lost all their sailor pride,
And lost likewise their dauntless minds ?
Remember Nile and Trafalgar !
On Jervis, Blake, and Nelson call!
Or from the years that lie afar,
Hail greatest sea-dog of them all—
Stout Drake, who sailing joyed the chance
That led him to a foeman’s main,
Careless of wrath of braggart France, '
Heedless of threats of haughty Spain.
A nation to be truly great
Must not alone keep clean its doors,
But have the power to close the gate
To road and outpost round its shores;
To hold afar the unworthy horde,
Men to all freedom alien—
To do this ere ye draw the sword
Ye must build ships—breed sailormen.

F. W. HU[?]ME.
The Wrong Man.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Strange that I should write to you! I, whose
name is lettered on the headstone of a tomb. The
northern sun has blistered the gilded inscription,
the rain has bleached the granite carving, the
rank tropical grass has covered the little heaped-
up mound, and the moss has overgrown the
wooden railings. The tree within the railed-off
space has blossomed many times, and the flowers
have bloomed and faded, but the characters are
still readable, telling how I died on 10th January,
1882.
Yet I am writing to you !
To you, who saw the shattered corpse gathered
from the shaft in which I had been working; who
heard the story of the explosion, and who stood
beside the grave that summer afternoon; who
maybe dropped a tear as the earth was shovelled
in—for who, beside the open grave of a friend, can
hear the falling earth rattle upon the coffin-lid
without a weakening of the eyes ? Perhaps you
wept—but how should I have known ? And what
avails it; since, if you did, it was over the wrong
man?
My mate and I had worked for months at the
prospecting shaft in which the accident occurred.
At dawn on that January morning I woke him.
He had been restless all night; we were expect-
ing every shot to bring us on to gold, and both of
us had gold-fever badly. The day before he had
received a letter from the girl to whom he was
engaged, and to whom he would* be married as
soon as our fortunes were made. I knew her
writing, and he had shown me her photo. Often,
too, he had read me extracts from her letters, and
I, whose matrimonial venture had miscarried, had
frown to envy him the anticipation of so much
appiness as the future promised.
After breakfast he and I went below, and the
braceman, as usual, remained on top. This was a
thick-headed fellow whom we paid between us,
and it was owing to his denseness that I became
the “wrong man.” My mate and I drilled and
charged a couple of holes each. Then he signalled
the braceman and was hauled up, while I re-
mained below to fix the charges, which I did
with one foot in the loop of the brace-rope ready
to be hauled aloft as soon as I had lit the snuffs.
The shaft was about 60 feet deep, and as the rope
tightened and swung me up, the fuses began to
splutter, and it was full of smoke. Up, and up,
and up.; till, when about midway, the rope parted
just above my hand, and I went crashing down-
wards. My face struck the sidewall, and when I
reached the bottom I lay dazed by the smash and
paralysed by the awful danger, for the hissing
fuses were nearly burnt out. I heard my mate
shouting on the brace, but I could make no reply.
Then I remembered that but a few feet up we
had driven in about 10 feet. If I could only clam-
ber up ! The walls were roughly dressed and not
timbered, and the butts of old shot-holes would
give me a footing. Blind and bruised and des-
perate, I started, and had just reached the drive
and clambered in when the charges exploded in
quick succession ; a blast of heated air and dust
shot pasf 'me, and a shower of slate and quartz fell
all around. Deafened by the explosion, choked
by dynamite-fumes, and weak from loss of blood,
I fainted.
Swathed m cotton-wool and oil, and bandaged
from head to foot, I woke in the hospital wardf'lt
had been like a long fevered dream of pain but
Was over The wind was blowing
through the open windows, and out on the lawn
the convalescent patients were sitting in the sun
By-and-by a gentle hand aroused me from a langor
that was not sleep; a little white-capped nuree
drew aside the curtain, and I saw beside her the
original of the photograph my mate had shown
wirt? n hl de - in / f ? r ™ onths 1 had been mates
with her intended husband, I did not think it
strange that she should come to see me ; but when
she knelt beside my cot and kissed me and went
over me, I did think it somewhat strange. I
wanted to talk, and marveled that the words did
not come. I found afterwards that my face and
jaws wers plastered and bandaged so that I could
not move my lips. The little nurse soon hurried
the weeping girl away, and I was left wondering.
.Later in the day you came, and for many days
after, and every day, my mate’s sweetheart was at
my bedside. One day the nurse read to me an
account of the accident. I was listening drowsily,
watching the sunlight and shadow on the garden
and langmdiy wondering when I would be allowed
on the lawn—when I was galvanised into breath-
less attention by what she was. reading: “The
braceman says deceased remained below to fire the
charges, and when he was being hauled up the
rope broke, and he fell to the bottom. Having
called to him and received no reply they concluded
he was insensible from the fall, and his mate, who
had been working below with him, again descended
i ii_ e r ?P e ’ * lo P m g to be able to remove him or to
cut the fuse before the dynamite exploded. In
t us he was unsuoce isf ul, and nearly lost his own
life, as he was found in the drive shattered beyond
recognition, and removed to the hospital, where
he now lies, while the unfortunate man at the
bottom was blown to bits.”
But the man at the bottom had not been blown
to bits ; for, as I have said, I was sheltered in the
duve when my mate came down the rope to save
me—though he was killed, and fell to the bottom.
1 wanted to talk and tell them so, but my jaws
wore set in plaster of Paris, and my hands,
swathed in layer on layer of wadding, were useless
to me. 1 looked at the card over my head and
saw my mate’s name, instead of mine. Ah ! and
then 1 remembered that he and I were much alike
in build and age and complexion. Small wonder,
then, that, disfigured as I was, even his sweet-
hoart was deceived. I kept silence even when,
d lys afterwards, the bandages were removed.
Why ? Because of my unhappy marriage. You
knew that my youthful choice was unfortunate,
and here 1 saw a hope of liberation. We had not
lived together for years, but her extravagance had
beggared me. t had no other object in view, only
to be freed from the intolerable affliction of an un-
faithful wife—for at that-time I did not know that
the charges which had killed my brave old mate
and disfigured me for life had laid bare the reef,
carrying heavy gold, and made me rich.
L left the hospital Under my old mate’s name,
and when I overhauled his property, which had
thus become mine, I found and read the letters
from his girl, and found that they had met but a
few times in a southern city, and that their subse-
quent correspondence had resulted in an engage-
ment. Hearing of the accident, she had come to
nurse him. Judging by the letter, she was very
fond of him, and I had worked with him long
enough to know that he was, among a thousand,
worthy any woman’s love. But so intense was
t in love that found expression in her letters that
I feared the consequences should she ever know
the truth. But why should she know? My life
had been a loveless one, and far from perfect;
but, with the incentive before me, could I not live
up to this woman’s ideal !
I took a sudden resolution and visited the far
western town where lived the woman who had
been my wife. Had I seen one indication that
she had changed from her evil ways, or any reason
to hope that we could have lived happily together,
I would have revealed my identity and given her
another chance. But when, secure in the disguise
of my disfigurement, I sought out the hut in which
she lived, and asked for news of her husband, she
told me of his death with such an unwomanly
exultation that I left her without regret, and came
back to marry and learn to love the girl whose
affections had been so strangely transferred to
me. I found tlisCt during my absence you had
erected a monument over my grave, and my flesh
crept a little as I read my name in gilt letters
on it.
My wife had formed such a high estimate of my
poor old mate’s character—which now became
mine—that I feared I should never be able to live
up to it, and his previous life and mine had been
such a contrast that I had to alter a lot to make
my future coincide with his promise of the past.
But it was worth all the sacrifices.
Anyway, old fellow, thanks for the tombstone,
though it is over the wrong man.
Q.
CURLEW.
“ Student,” to The Bulletin :
For the information of “ S.” (8., 5/5/’00), who compares
“Banjo’s” assertion that shells travel faster than the
sound of their discharge with Donald MacDonald’s re-
mark that besieged soldiers become quite expert in dodg-
ing shells, it maysbe explained .that each correspondent is
right. The true application of their statements depends
entirely upon the distance..between cannon-muzzle and
the person shot at. Sound travels in free dry air with
an initial velocity of 335 feet per second ; the report of a
cannon (with a lilb. powder' charge) possesses an initial
velocity of 1187 feet per second-; at a distance of 75 feet,
it advances to 1267 feet per second ; and at a distance of
100 feet from the muzzle, it travels 1202 feet per second.
The velocity of a bullet, ball, or shell attains its maxi-
mum at-some little distance from the muzzle, anil then
diminishes rapidly. Owing to the dissipation of energy-
in surrounding space, the sound of the “ discharge.” at
the cannon’s mouth does not travel anything like as far,
laterally, as the ball or shell itself, which is propelled with
concern rated initial energy in one straight line. No
particle of the sound of the discharge penetrates after a.
couple of miles or so, although the cannon ball may then
be travelling onwards at the rate of perhaps 1500 feet per
second. So much in favor of “Banjo”; but the sound caused
by the passage of the modern shell itself (notof its original
discharge) hurtling through the air is audible before the
shell arrives at its destination; theshejl is also visible, espe-
cially by an observer at long range. In comparing the
testimony of war-correspondents, liberal allowances must
-be made for variations in the conditions of observation.
Different makes of projectiles are ignorantly compared
with each other on similar bases. The report of the dis-
charge of the modern rifle is rarely audible beyond 1200
yards ; but volley-firing can be heard distinctly' a mile or
more away. The old rifle-bullet had an initial velocity of
500 feet per second (half that of the average cannon-ball),
and a man was struck before the report had got half-way
to him ; now, bullets go somewhat faster, biit at long-
range the “sizzle” of the slowly-moving bullets them-
selves may be heard in advance, through the air-friction,
on a cold night or morning. In all these questions it
must be remembered that (1) the intensity of sound de-
pends upon the density (i.e., temperature and moisture) of
the surrounding air ; (2) on the direction of the wind ; (3>
the acoustic powers of the listener ; (4) the neighborhood
of sound-transmitting bodies. For instance, sound travels
four times as fast in water as in air. Try to shoot fish,
and see the poor bag the amateur will get*; the sound of
the report reaches their ears before the bullet strikes
them; but with atmosphere-surrounded man, matters are
on an opposite footing.
